The Karramiyya's Proof from Visibility and Its Rebuttal
Razi presents the Karramiyya claim and the companions' initial rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's argument that God must be in a direction because He is seen, and the companions' logical response questioning the transfer of a premise from the witnessed to the unseen world.

Core Idea
What holds in the witnessed world does not automatically apply to the unseen.

The Karramiyya sect argued that since God will be seen on the Day of Judgment, He must be located in a direction. In this passage, Imam Razi presents their claim and then records the response of the Sunni scholars, who challenge the universality of the premise. They introduce a critical distinction between self-evident and inferential premises, forcing the opponent to justify their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to grasp the overall flow. Notice the compact style of the text.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the companions' argument.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the Karramiyya's claim and the companions' objection. Note that the companions do not outright deny the premise but question its application.
The companions say 'we do not grant that everything seen is in a direction.'
3
Analyze the logical structure
The companions break the premise into two types: self-evident (badihiyya) or inferential (istidlaliyya). Understand the implication of each: if self-evident, no proof needed; if inferential, it requires justification.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the distinction between the witnessed (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib) is a fundamental tool in kalam arguments. This prevents hasty analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They said: 'We do not grant that everything seen is specifically in a direction. Rather, there is no dispute that the matter is so in the witnessed world. But why do you say: if it is so in the witnessed world, then it must be so in the unseen?' Its explanation: this premise is either self-evident or inferential. If it is self-evident, then there is no need for this proof to establish that God is in a direction. That is because it is established in the witnessed world that every self-subsistent...

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of a theological premise. Razi presents the Karramiyya argument that since God is seen (on the Day of Judgment), He must be in a direction (jiha). The companions reject the premise that 'everything seen is in a direction' as universal. They concede it holds in the witnessed world (al-shahid) but question its application to the unseen (al-gha'ib). They then analyze the premise: it is either self-evident (badihiyya) or inferential (istidlaliyya). If self-evident, the Karramiyya would not need this proof; if inferential, it requires justification. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'that every self-subsistent...' (kullu qā'im bi al-nafs), which likely introduces the idea that in the witnessed world, everything self-subsistent is in a direction—a point completed later in the text.

Misconception: The companions simply reject the Karramiyya argument without reasoning.
Correction: They actually engage logically: they challenge the premise's universality and analyze it into self-evident or inferential, forcing the opponent to defend the transfer from witnessed to unseen.

Razi's Critique of the Witnessed-Unseen Analogy
Razi critiques the inference from seen to unseen regarding direction using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two logical flaws in using the witnessed world to argue that God must be in a direction: redundancy and inconsistent application of the analogy.

Core Idea
Analogy fails when applied inconsistently: what holds in seen may not hold for unseen.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of the Karramiyya by examining the principle that 'whatever holds in the witnessed world must also hold in the unseen.' He points out that if they accept this principle, their argument becomes redundant; if they reject it, their reasoning is unsound. Moreover, they inconsistently allow exceptions for some attributes but not for direction. Let's follow his logical dissection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let Razi's own logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct arguments: redundancy and inconsistency.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Razi presents two critiques. First, if the principle 'what holds in witnessed holds in unseen' is accepted, then direction is directly proven without needing the detour through visibility. Second, if the premise 'everything seen is in a direction' is not self-evident, it remains unproven. Then he adds an inconsistency: they allow exceptions for size and composition but not for being opposite to the seer.
3
Trace the logic of redundancy
Razi says: Since God is self-subsistent, and by the principle, whatever holds in the witnessed holds in the unseen, then direction (which holds for self-subsistent objects in witnessed) must hold for God, making the visibility argument unnecessary.
This shows the Karramiyya's argument is either redundant or relies on a premise they haven't proven.
4
Examine the inconsistency objection
In the witnessed world, a seen object must be opposite to the seer, have size, extension, and parts. The Karramiyya say God is seen but without these attributes. If they allow such differences, why not allow that being opposite to the seer also differs?
This undermines the analogy by showing selective application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو مختص بالجهة. وثبت أن البارى تعالى قائم بالنفس فوجب القطع بأنه مختص بالجهة لأن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد وجب أن يكون في الغائب كذلك. فإذا كان هذا الوجه حاصلا في إثبات كونه تعالى في الجهة، كان إثبات كونه تعالى في الجهة بكونه مرئياً، ثم إثبات أن كل ما كان مرئياً فهو مختص بالجهة، تطويل من غير فائدة، ومن غير مزيد شرح وبيان. وأما إن قلنا: إن قولنا إن كل مرئي فهو مختص بالجهة ليست مقدمة بديهية، بل هي مقدمة استدلالية. فحينئذ ما لم يذكروا على صحتها دليلا، لا تصير هذه المقدمة يقينية. وأيضاً إنا كما لا نعقل مرئياً في الشاهد، إلا إذا كان مقابلاً أو في حكم المقابل للرائي، فكذلك لا نعقل مرئياً إلا إذا كان صغيراً وكبيراً، أو ممتداً في الجهات، أو مؤتلفاً من الأجزاء. وهم يقولون: إنه تعالى يرى، لا صغيراً ولا كبيراً، ولا ممتداً في الجهات والجوانب والأحياز. فإذا جاز لكم أن تحكموا بأن الغائب مخالف للشاهد في هذا الباب، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن المرئي في الشاهد - وإن وجب كونه مقابلاً للرائي إلا أن المرئي في الغائب - لا يجوز أن يكون كذلك؟
So He is specific to a direction. And it is established that the Creator is self-subsistent, so it is necessary to judge that He is specific to a direction because necessary knowledge holds that everything established in the witnessed world must be so in the unseen. So if this aspect is established in proving that He is in a direction, then proving that He is in a direction by His being seen, and then proving that everything seen is specific to a direction, is a prolongation without benefit and without further explanation and clarification. And if we say that our statement 'everything seen is specific to a direction' is not a self-evident premise but an inferential one, then as long as they do not bring a proof for its soundness, this premise does not become certain. Moreover, just as we do not conceive a seen object in the witnessed world unless it is opposite or in the position of being opposite to the seer, so we do not conceive a seen object unless it is small and large, or extended in directions, or composed of parts. And they say: He is seen, neither small nor large, nor extended in directions, sides, and places. So if you are allowed to judge that the unseen differs from the witnessed in this respect, then why is it not also allowed that the seen in the witnessed world – even though it must be opposite to the seer – yet the seen in the unseen is not allowed to be so?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a logical critique of the inference from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). Razi argues that if the Karramiyya claim that God is seen (and therefore in a direction) relies on the principle that what holds in the witnessed holds in the unseen, then that principle itself can be used to directly prove direction without the detour through visibility—making that detour redundant. If, on the other hand, the premise 'everything seen is in a direction' is not self-evident but inferential, it remains unproven. Razi then levels a stronger objection: in the witnessed world, a seen object must be opposite the seer, have size, extension, and composition. But the Karramiyya affirm that God is seen without these attributes. If they allow that the unseen can differ from the witnessed regarding these attributes, then why not also allow that the condition of being opposite the seer may not apply to God's visibility? This argument undermines the analogy between witnessed and unseen about visibility.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
علم ضروري
مقابل
رائي
صغير
كبير
ممتد
أجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of analogy from witnessed to unseen is always valid and straightforward.
Correction: Razi shows that the principle can be applied inconsistently, leading to flawed conclusions if exceptions are selectively allowed.

Checkpoint
What problem does Razi point out with the Karramiyya's use of the principle 'what holds in the witnessed holds in the unseen'?
A) The principle is false.
B) If the principle is accepted, it directly proves direction without needing the visibility argument.
C) The Karramiyya never used that principle.
D) The principle only applies to self-evident premises.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the Karramiyya's use of analogy is either redundant or inconsistent, thus failing to prove that God is in a direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you insist that others follow a rule you yourself break? Razi exposes the inconsistency of allowing exceptions in one area while demanding strict analogy in another. Where do you do the same regarding your beliefs about God?
?
You have likely heard arguments from analogy about God. Have you ever stopped to test whether the analogy is being applied consistently? What would it cost you to admit that your own reasoning may be selective?
?
If you were the Karramiyya, how would you defend yourself? Would you be willing to let go of the condition of opposition for visibility? What does your attachment to that condition say about your conception of God?

Fourth Doubt: Raising Hands and Its Refutation
Razi presents the fourth doubt and its counterargument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the argument from raising hands by pointing to the equally universal practice of prostration.

Core Idea
Raising hands is no proof — prostration counters it just as well.

The fourth doubt appeals to a universal religious practice: raising hands to heaven. Razi's answer is simple but powerful: the same universal instinct leads people to place their foreheads on the ground. This parity undermines the proof that God is located in a direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and its claim
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the objectors argue that raising hands to heaven is a universal practice among religious people, claiming it proves that all minds instinctively believe God is above.
2
Identify Razi's counterargument
Razi responds by pointing to another universal practice: placing foreheads on the ground in prostration. Understand why this is a direct counter: if one practice proves direction, the other would prove the opposite, making the argument inconclusive.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the counterargument is complete in its essence.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why Razi's method is effective: he uses the same type of evidence (universal practice) to neutralize the opponent's claim. This is a classic dialectical move in kalam.
This shows that universal practices cannot be used as decisive proof for specific theological claims about God's location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: تمسكوا برفع الأيدي إلى السماء. قالوا: وهذا شيء يفعله أرباب النحل. فدل على أنه تقرر في جميع عقول الخلق كون الإله في جهة فوق. الجواب: إن هذا معارض بما تقرر في جميع عقول الخلق: أنهم عند تعظيم خالق العالم يضعون جباههم على الأرض. ولما لم

So it indicates that it is established in all minds of creation that God is in the direction of above. The answer: This is countered by what is established in all minds of creation: that when they magnify the Creator of the world, they place their foreheads on the ground. And when [the argument is] not...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt (shubha) against God's transcendence above direction. The objectors claim that the universal practice of raising hands in supplication toward heaven proves that all people instinctively believe God is in the direction 'above' (fawq). Razi's answer is that this is countered by another universal practice: people prostrate by placing their foreheads on the ground when worshiping. This act suggests that God is also associated with the earth or below, thus the practice is not decisive. The passage ends mid-sentence ('wa lammā lam'), likely continuing to elaborate on this counterargument on the next page.

Misconception: One might think that the universal practice of raising hands to heaven is a strong proof that God is located above.
Correction: Razi shows that the equally universal practice of prostration (placing foreheads on the ground) would then prove God is below, so the argument is inconclusive.

Universal practices are ambiguous and cannot decisively prove a specific direction for God.

The Fourth Example: Ta'wil of the Hadith of the Two Surahs
Imam Razi cites the fourth example of a hadith requiring ta'wil and Ahmad ibn Hanbal's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about Surah al-Baqarah and Al Imran coming like clouds requires ta'wil, and how Ahmad ibn Hanbal's response supports the necessity of interpretation.

Core Idea
Even Ahmad ibn Hanbal used ta'wil to defend against anthropomorphism.

This lesson presents the fourth example from Imam Razi's series of hadiths that require ta'wil. The Mu'tazila used a vivid hadith describing the Quran coming like clouds on the Day of Resurrection to argue for its createdness. Ahmad ibn Hanbal's answer shows that even the most traditionist scholars resorted to metaphorical interpretation when the literal meaning would imply an unworthy attribute of Allah or the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how it presents both the Mu'tazila argument and Ahmad's response in two concise sentences.
The phrase 'كذا وكذا' indicates the hadith is abbreviated.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila used this hadith to claim that the Quran is created because it is described as coming on the Day of Resurrection (a temporal event).
The hadith literally says 'the Quran comes like clouds'—if taken literally, it suggests the Quran is a physical object.
3
Analyze Ahmad ibn Hanbal's response
Ahmad interprets the hadith figuratively: 'He means the reward of their reciters.' This shifts the meaning from the Quran itself to the reward it brings.
This is a clear example of ta'wil from someone often viewed as anti-rationalist.
4
Reflect on the necessity of ta'wil
Consider why even a traditionalist like Ahmad had to use ta'wil. How does this support the principle that texts must be interpreted when their literal meaning leads to false beliefs?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : حكى أن المعتزلة تمسكوا فى خلق القرآن ، بما روى عنه عليه السلام أنه تأتى سورة البقرة وآل عمران كذا وكذا ( يوم القيامة ) كأنهما غمامتان . فأجاب أحمد بن حنبل ( رحمه الله ) وقال : « يعنى ثواب قارئهما، وهذا تصريح منه بالتأويل .
The fourth: It is related that the Mu'tazila argued for the creation of the Quran by what was narrated from him, peace be upon him, that Surah al-Baqarah and Al Imran will come such and such (on the Day of Resurrection) as if they are two clouds. So Ahmad ibn Hanbal (may Allah have mercy on him) answered and said: 'He means the reward of their reciters,' and this is an explicit statement from him of ta'wil.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides the fourth example from a series of hadiths that require ta'wil (interpretation) to avoid anthropomorphism. The Mu'tazila had used a hadith that describes Surah al-Baqarah and Al Imran coming on the Day of Resurrection like clouds as evidence for the creation of the Quran, implying the Quran is a created entity. Imam al-Razi reports that Ahmad ibn Hanbal responded by interpreting the hadith metaphorically: 'He means the reward of their reciters.' This shows that even a figure like Ahmad ibn Hanbal, often associated with literal acceptance, resorted to ta'wil when necessary. Thus this example demonstrates the necessity of ta'wil for seemingly corporeal descriptions of the Quran.

Key Terms

المعتزلة
خلق القرآن
أحمد بن حنبل
الغمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only rationalist theologians (mutakallimun) use ta'wil; traditionalists like Ahmad ibn Hanbal always take texts literally.
Correction: This hadith shows Ahmad ibn Hanbal himself performed ta'wil, interpreting the coming of the surahs as their reward.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ahmad ibn Hanbal's ta'wil of the hadith shows that even traditionalist scholars interpret metaphorical descriptions to avoid corporealism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that seems to describe the Quran as a physical object coming on the Day of Resurrection, do you immediately accept the literal meaning, or do you pause to consider what the Salaf said? How does Ahmad ibn Hanbal's ta'wil challenge you to trust the scholars' interpretations over your initial impression?
?
The Mu'tazila used the same hadith to argue for the creation of the Quran. Have you ever used a single text to support a belief without checking how the early generations understood it? Will you commit to studying the methodology of the Salaf before forming theological conclusions?
?

Four Hadiths on Divine Attributes: The Necessity of Ta'wil
Four hadiths each followed by the assertion that ta'wil is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why four specific hadiths that attribute seemingly human qualities to Allah require allegorical interpretation (ta'wil) to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Even clear-sounding hadiths about Allah require ta'wil to avoid attributing human form to Him.

Imam Razi presents four short hadiths that, if taken literally, would imply Allah has body parts or spatial location. Each hadith is followed by his assertion that ta'wil is necessary. In this lesson, we will examine these examples and see how the author argues that literal interpretation is impossible, thus demanding allegorical understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the four hadiths
Read the Arabic text of the four hadiths and their translations. Notice the anthropomorphic expressions.
These are authentic hadiths from the Prophet pbuh.
2
Identify the literal problem
For each hadith, ask: what attribute or action would be attributed to Allah if taken literally?
For example, the womb hanging onto rights, the mosque shrinking, two fingers, and being with broken hearts.
3
Study the author's reasoning
Notice how Razi argues that literal meaning is impossible. For the heart-between-fingers, he says 'we know by necessity' that there are no fingers inside us. For the 'I am his hearing,' he notes that sight is not Allah.
4
Apply the principle
Conclude that ta'wil is required for any text that implies corporealism or spatial location for Allah.
This lesson reinforces the core principle of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قوله عليه السلام : « إن الرحم يتعلق بحقوقى الرحمن ، فيقول سبحانه وتعالى : أصل من وصلك، وهذا لابد له من التأويل . السادس : قال عليه السلام : « إن المسجد لينزوى من النخامة ، كما تنزوى الجلدة من النار ، ولا بد فيه من التأويل . السابع : قال عليه السلام : « قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين من أصابع الرحمن ، وهذا لا بد فيه من التأويل ، لأنا نعلم بالضرورة : أنه ليس في صدورنا إصبعان بينهما قلوبنا . الثامن : قوله عليه السلام - حكاية عن الله تعالى - : ( أنا عند المنكسرة قلوبهم )، وليست هذه العندية إلا بالرحمة . وأيضا : قال حكاية عن الله تعالى في صفة الأولياء : فإذا أحببته كنت سمعه الذي يسمع به ، وبصره الذي يبصر به، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة : أن القوة الباصرة التي بها يرى الأشياء ليست هي الله ( سبحانه و تعالى ) .
The fifth: His statement, peace be upon him: 'Indeed the womb hangs onto the rights of the Most Merciful, so He, glorified and exalted, says: 'Be joined to the one who joins you,' and this must have ta'wil. The sixth: He said, peace be upon him: 'Indeed the mosque shrinks from sputum as the skin shrinks from fire,' and there must be ta'wil in it. The seventh: He said, peace be upon him: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful,' and there must be ta'wil in it, for we know by necessity that in our chests there are not two fingers between which are our hearts. The eighth: His statement, peace be upon him—narrating from Allah the Exalted—: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken,' and this 'withness' is nothing but mercy. And also: He said, narrating from Allah the Exalted concerning the description of the saints: 'When I love him, I am his hearing with which he hears, and his sight with which he sees,' and it is known by necessity that the power of sight by which one sees things is not Allah (glorified and exalted).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents four hadiths that attribute seemingly human or material qualities to Allah, each followed by the author's assertion that ta'wil is necessary. The fifth hadith describes the womb (ar-rahim) as 'hanging onto the rights of the Most Merciful' and Allah speaking in a way implying spatial proximity. Ta'wil must remove the anthropomorphic implication. The sixth hadith states that the mosque 'shrinks' from sputum like skin shrinks from fire, describing the mosque as having a sensory reaction—again needing a metaphorical interpretation. The seventh hadith says the believer's heart is 'between two fingers of the Most Merciful,' which would imply Allah has fingers; the author notes that it is self-evident that we do not have two fingers holding our hearts inside us, so ta'wil is mandatory. The eighth hadith includes two divine sayings: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken' and 'When I love him, I am his hearing and his sight.' The author explains that 'withness' (al-‘indiyya) is only by mercy, not spatial presence, and that the statement 'I am his hearing and sight' cannot be taken literally because hearing and sight are not the essence of Allah. Thus these examples reinforce the principle that any text attributing bodily parts or spatial location to Allah must be interpreted allegorically.

Key Terms
التأويل
الرحم
المسجد
النخامة
الإصبعان
القلب
العندية
الرحمة
القوة الباصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these hadiths are obvious metaphors and do not require formal ta'wil, or that they can be taken literally since they are from the Prophet.
Correction: Imam Razi emphasizes that even these short hadiths demand deliberate ta'wil because the literal meaning would attribute impossible qualities to Allah, such as body parts or spatial location, which contradict tanzih.

Checkpoint
In the seventh example, why does the author say ta'wil is necessary for the hadith about the believer's heart between two fingers?
A) Because the hadith is weak
B) Because we know by necessity that our hearts are not between actual fingers
C) Because the heart is not physical
D) Because the hadith is not from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The four hadiths demonstrate that any scriptural text attributing bodily parts, spatial location, or sensory actions to Allah must be interpreted allegorically to uphold divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith about Allah having 'two fingers' or 'being the hearing and sight' of a believer, do you immediately pause to interpret it figuratively, or do you allow a literal image to form in your mind? What practical step will you take today to train yourself to apply ta'wil before any anthropomorphic thought settles in your heart?
?
The author shows that our own intuition ('we know by necessity') tells us that these texts cannot be literal. Yet many Muslims still carry a mental picture of Allah with human traits. Where in your heart do you find it hardest to maintain tanzih (transcendence)? Will you commit to reciting the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) every time you feel that mental image forming?
?
The hadith 'I am with those whose hearts are broken' and 'I am his hearing and sight' are meant to give comfort, not to describe Allah's essence. How does knowing that this 'withness' is purely mercy change the way you seek closeness to Allah? Will you now actively seek to be among those 'broken-hearted' ones, knowing that Allah's mercy is with them in a special way?

Ibn Furak's Ta'wil of Allah's Recitation
Ibn Furak's metaphorical interpretation of Allah's recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Furak interpreted the hadith about Allah's recitation of Ta Ha and Ya Sin as a metaphorical act of making speech heard and understood.

Core Idea
Ta'wil turns apparent divine actions into meanings befitting Allah's transcendence.

This lesson examines an early Ash'ari scholar's approach to a hadith that seems to attribute literal recitation to Allah. Ibn Furak shows that such language must be understood metaphorically, preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Ibn Furak's interpretation
Read the Arabic hadith and then Ibn Furak's ta'wil. Notice how he replaces 'recited' with 'made appear, heard, understood'.
2
Identify the key terms
Highlight the Arabic verb 'قرأ' and Ibn Furak's explanation 'أظهر وأسمع وأفهم'.
This is a clear example of ta'wil.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Consider why Ibn Furak felt the need to interpret this hadith non-literally. What attribute of Allah is preserved?
It preserves Allah's transcendence above literal speech actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسند الدارمي أبو محمد في مسنده عن أبي هريرة ـ رضى الله عنه - قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( ان الله تبارك وتعالى قرأ « طه » و « يس » قبل أن يخلق السموات والأرض بألفي عام ، فلما سمعت الملائكة : القرآن ، قالت : طوبى لأمة ينزل هذا عليها ، وطوبى لأجواف تحمل هذا ، وطوبى لألسنة تتكلم بهذا » . قال ابن فورك : معنى قوله : ( ان الله تبارك وتعالى - قرأ ( طه ) و ( يس ) أي : أظهر وأسمع وأفهم كلامه من أراد من خلقه الملائكة في ذلك الوقت . فقد أول ابن فورك ( قراءة الله ) بمعنى مجازي هو « أظهر وأسمع وأفهم » . وقال ابن عباس في قوله تعالى : ( الرحمن على العرش استوى » قال : « بريد خلق ما كان وما هو كائن الى يوم القيامة ، وبعد القيامة » فقد أول ابن عباس استواء الله بمعنى مجازي هو خلق ما كان وما هو كائن . وقال الطبرى فى قوله تعالى : ( وألقيت عليك محبة منى ولتصنع على عينى » قال : « محبة منى » أي القيت عليك رحمة . فقد فسر المحبة بالمعنى المجازى وهو الرحمة . وأبعد المحبة عن المعنى الحقيقي. وقيل فى ) ولتصنع على عينى ( أى تربى وتغذى على مرأى منى . أى له عناية خاصة الله
Al-Darimi Abu Muhammad narrated in his Musnad from Abu Hurayra — may Allah be pleased with him — that he said: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Indeed Allah the Blessed and Exalted recited 'Ta Ha' and 'Ya Sin' two thousand years before He created the heavens and the earth. When the angels heard the Quran, they said: Blessed is the people upon whom this descends, and blessed are the hearts that carry this, and blessed are the tongues that speak with this.' Ibn Furak said: The meaning of His statement 'Allah recited 'Ta Ha' and 'Ya Sin'' is that He made His speech appear, and made it heard, and made it understood by whomever He willed of His creation among the angels at that time. So Ibn Furak interpreted Allah's 'recitation' (qira'a) in a metaphorical sense as 'He appeared, made heard, and made understood.' And Ibn Abbas said concerning the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (20:5): 'He intended the creation of what was and what is to be until the Day of Resurrection, and after the Resurrection.' So Ibn Abbas interpreted Allah's 'istawa' in a metaphorical sense as the creation of what was and what is to be. And al-Tabari said concerning the verse 'And I cast upon you love from Me and that you be made under My eye' (20:39): 'Love from Me' means I cast upon you mercy.' So he interpreted 'love' (mahabbah) in a metaphorical sense as mercy, and he distanced love from its literal meaning. And it was said concerning 'And that you be made under My eye' that it means 'you are raised and nourished under My sight,' i.e., he has special care from Allah.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This footnote provides additional textual support from three early authorities for the principle of ta'wil. The first example is from Ibn Furak (a prominent Ash'ari theologian). He interprets the hadith about Allah 'reciting' Ta Ha and Ya Sin before creation. He takes 'recitation' metaphorically as 'making heard and understood,' not actual recitation by Allah. The second example is from Ibn Abbas, a companion known for exegetical knowledge. He interprets the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arshi istawa' by saying it means 'He intended the creation of what was and what is to be until the Day of Resurrection and after,' thus avoiding a literal sitting on the Throne. The third example is from al-Tabari, the great commentator. He interprets 'love from Me' (mahabbatan minni) as 'mercy' (rahma), showing that love attributed to Allah is not the same as human emotion but signifies grace. The note also mentions the phrase 'and that you be made under My eye' as meaning 'under My sight/care.' Collectively, these citations demonstrate that the practice of ta'wil is not an innovation; it goes back to the earliest generations. This reinforces the author's argument that ta'wil is necessary for every rational person, as even the salaf employed it.

Key Terms
التأويل
ابن فورك
قراءة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ta'wil was invented later, but this shows early scholars used it.
Correction: Ibn Furak, a prominent early Ash'ari, employed ta'wil for this hadith.

Checkpoint
How did Ibn Furak interpret Allah's 'recitation' in the hadith?
A) Literal reading aloud
B) Making speech appear, heard, and understood
C) Writing in a book
D) Creating the letters

Key Takeaway
Ibn Furak's ta'wil of 'recitation' shows that early theologians understood divine actions in a metaphorical sense to preserve transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Ibn Furak's ta'wil that 'recitation' means 'made appear, heard, and understood', does your heart accept this as a valid understanding of Allah's action, or do you feel a resistance because it seems to reduce the majesty of Allah's speech? What in your upbringing or assumptions causes that resistance?
?
If you had heard the hadith about Allah reciting Ta Ha and Ya Sin without any commentary, would you have imagined Allah reciting with a tongue and voice? What does that reveal about your innate tendency to anthropomorphize Allah, and how can you actively train your heart to submit to ta'wil when the apparent meaning conflicts with tanzih?
?
The angels upon hearing the Quran even before creation exclaimed, 'Blessed are the tongues that speak with this.' Do you truly believe that the Quran you recite is that same speech from Allah, even if we cannot fully comprehend how? What would change in your relationship with the Quran if you internalized that its origin is beyond human analogy?

Ibn Abbas's Ta'wil of Istawa
Ibn Abbas's metaphorical interpretation of istawa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Abbas interpreted the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arshi istawa' as a metaphor for divine creation and governance.

Core Idea
Istawa does not mean literal sitting but signifies Allah's absolute control.

This lesson explores the interpretation of the prominent companion Ibn Abbas regarding the famous verse about Allah's 'establishing' over the Throne. He gives a creative ta'wil that links it to creation itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Ibn Abbas's explanation
Read the Arabic verse and Ibn Abbas's comment. Note the word 'بريد' which likely means 'يريد' (He intends).
2
Understand the metaphorical shift
Compare the literal meaning of 'istawa' (to settle, to mount) with Ibn Abbas's interpretation: 'the creation of what was and what is to be'.
This is a radical ta'wil that redefines the entire verse.
3
Consider the significance
Reflect on how this ta'wil avoids anthropomorphism. What does it say about Allah's relationship to creation?
It emphasizes Allah's all-encompassing creative power.

Source Text

Al-Tabari's Ta'wil of Divine Love and Sight
Al-Tabari's metaphorical interpretation of divine love and sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Tabari interpreted 'love' from Allah as mercy and 'under My eye' as under His care.

Core Idea
Divine attributes like love and sight are understood metaphorically as mercy and care.

This lesson looks at al-Tabari's exegesis of a verse where Allah says He cast love upon Moses and that he be made under His eye. Al-Tabari explains these terms in a way that avoids attributing human emotions or physical eyes to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and al-Tabari's interpretation
Read the Arabic verse and al-Tabari's comment on 'love from Me'. Note how he equates it with mercy.
2
Examine the second phrase
Read the explanation of 'under My eye' as 'raised and nourished under My sight'.
Note: This second part is attributed to 'it was said' (وقيل), not necessarily to al-Tabari.
3
Identify the ta'wil principle
Both interpretations move from literal meanings (emotional love, physical eye) to metaphorical ones compatible with Allah's majesty.
This demonstrates the necessity of ta'wil for verses that suggest corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse 'I cast upon you love from Me' (20:39), do you internalize Allah's love as a tangible mercy and care in your daily life? Identify one specific moment today where you felt that mercy – and what does that tell you about your relationship with Allah?
?
Al-Tabari teaches that even the 'eye' of Allah is not a physical eye but a metaphor for His special care. Have you ever subconsciously pictured Allah with human-like features? How can you train your heart to feel His watchful care without falling into anthropomorphic imagination?
?
If Allah's love is ultimately mercy, what does that demand from you in terms of how you show mercy to others? Think of one person you find difficult to love – how can you translate your understanding of divine mercy into a concrete act of care toward them this week?

God Alone Creates the Human Form
Razi uses Quranic proof to refute naturalistic explanations of human form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the human form is entirely the creation of God, not of any natural or generative power, and how the Quranic names al-Khaliq, al-Bari', and al-Musawwir support this.

Core Idea
Only God creates and shapes the human form — no natural power shares in this.

In this final sub-interpretation of the hadith, Imam Razi turns to the most explicit evidence affirming God's sole agency in forming the human image. He cites a well-known verse from Surah al-Hashr that links three divine names — al-Khaliq, al-Bari', al-Musawwir — to clarify that no physician or philosopher's theory of formative powers can account for this. As you read, notice how each name is given a precise definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its literal English translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you analyse.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the three names and their definitions
Razi lists three names of God from Quran 59:24: al-Khaliq, al-Bari', al-Musawwir. Find the definition he gives for each and note the specific function each name indicates.
These definitions are not mere synonyms; each highlights a distinct aspect of creation.
3
Reflect on the contrast with natural powers
Razi explicitly denies that the formative and generative powers (quwwa musawwira wa muwallida) have any role in shaping the human image. Consider why physicians and philosophers might claim otherwise, and why this theological point matters.
This is a direct refutation of naturalistic explanations of human form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : المقصود منه : بيان أن هذه الصورة الإنسانية إنما حصلت بتخليق الله تعالى ، وإيجاده . لا بتأثير القوة المصورة والمولدة . على ما تذكره الأطباء والفلاسفة . ولهذا قال الله تعالى : وهو الله الخالق البارىء المصور ( الحشر ٢٤ ) ، فهو « الخالق ، أى فهو العالم بأحوال الممكنات والمحدثات و « الباريء ، أي هو المحدث للأجسام والذوات بعد عدمها ، و «المصور» أي هو الذي يركب تلك الذوات على صورها المخصوصة وتركيباتها المخصوصة
Fourth: The intended meaning is to show that this human image is only obtained by the creation and bringing into existence of God Almighty, not by the effect of the formative and generative power, as the physicians and philosophers mention. And for this reason God Almighty said: 'He is God, the Creator, the Maker, the Shaper' (Quran 59:24). So 'al-Khaliq' means He is the knower of the states of possible and originated things, and 'al-Bari'' means He is the originator of bodies and essences after their non-existence, and 'al-Musawwir' means He is the one who composes those essences upon their specific forms and their specific compositions.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is the fourth sub-interpretation, which aims to demonstrate that the human form is solely the result of God's creation and existence-bestowal, not of any formative or generative power (quwwa musawwira wa muwallida) as claimed by physicians and philosophers. Razi supports this by citing Quran 59:24, which lists three names of God: al-Khaliq (the Creator), al-Bari' (the Maker), and al-Musawwir (the Shaper). He explicates each: al-Khaliq is the knower of all possible and originated things; al-Bari' is the one who brings bodies and essences into existence after they were nothing; and al-Musawwir is the one who composes those essences into their specific forms and structures. Thus, the hadith is aligned with the Quranic affirmation that God alone is the fashioner of all forms.

الخالق
البارىء
المصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The formative and generative powers (natural or biological forces) contribute to shaping the human form.
Correction: No natural power has any effect; only God through His names al-Khaliq, al-Bari', and al-Musawwir creates and forms the human image.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you believe that no natural power shares in shaping you, why do you still worry about your appearance or health as if they are in your control? Can you surrender your physical self to the One who fashioned it, trusting Him with its preservation?
?

The Fourth Interpretation: Human Form from Allah Alone
Imam Razi explains the fourth interpretation: human form is from Allah alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the human form is created solely by Allah, not by natural forces, and that the divine names al-Khaliq, al-Bari, and al-Musawwir each highlight a distinct aspect of this creation.

Core Idea
Our form is a direct gift from Allah, not from nature's powers.

Imam Razi presents the fourth interpretation of a hadith about creation: the human form is entirely from Allah's creative act, not from the ‘formative’ or ‘generative’ powers claimed by physicians and philosophers. He then grounds this in the Quranic verse that names Allah as the Creator, the Maker, and the Fashioner, explaining how each name reveals a unique dimension of His creative work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Notice how Razi states the claim clearly at the beginning.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi asserts that the human form is attained by Allah's creation and bringing into existence, not by the influence of natural powers. Ask yourself: why does he contrast with physicians and philosophers?
This refutes those who attribute human form to natural processes.
3
Examine the three names
The passage lists three names of Allah from the Quran: al-Khaliq, al-Bari, al-Musawwir. For each, note the specific meaning Razi gives. Write them down: al-Khaliq as Knower of contingent things, al-Bari as Originator of bodies after non-existence, al-Musawwir as Composer of essences into specific forms.
These are not synonyms; each adds a layer to understanding Allah's creative role.
4
Connect to the hadith
Razi is explaining a hadith about creation. See how this analysis supports the idea that the entire human form is from Allah, not from any secondary cause.
This reinforces divine transcendence and human dependence on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : المقصود منه : بيان أن هذه الصورة الإنسانية إنما حصلت بتخليق الله تعالى ، وإيجاده . لا بتأثير القوة المصورة والمولدة . على ما تذكره الأطباء والفلاسفة . ولهذا قال الله تعالى : وهو الله الخالق البارئ المصور (۸) ، فهو « الخالق ، أى فهو العالم بأحوال الممكنات والمحدثات و ( الباري ، أي هو المحدث للأجسام والذوات بعد عدمها ، و «المصور» أي هو الذي يركب تلك الذوات على صورها المخصوصة وتركيباتها المخصوصة
The fourth: its intended meaning is the explanation that this human form is only attained by the creation (takhliq) of Allah Most High and His bringing into existence, not by the influence of the formative and generative power, as the physicians and philosophers mention. And for this Allah Most High said: 'He is Allah, the Creator (al-Khaliq), the Maker (al-Bari), the Fashioner (al-Musawwir)' (59:24). So He is 'al-Khaliq', i.e., He is the Knower of the states of contingent and originated things; and 'al-Bari', i.e., He is the Originator of bodies and essences after their non-existence; and 'al-Musawwir', i.e., He is the One who composes those essences upon their specific forms and their specific compositions.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth interpretation, which explains that the human form is brought about solely by Allah's creation and bringing into existence (ikhraj min al-'adam), not by the formative power (quwwa musawwira) or generative power (quwwa mulawwida) as claimed by physicians and philosophers. The author supports this by quoting the verse from Surat al-Hashr (59:24) where Allah is described as al-Khaliq, al-Bari, al-Musawwir. He then explains each name: al-Khaliq meaning He knows all contingent and originated things; al-Bari meaning He brings bodies and essences into existence after they were non-existent; and al-Musawwir meaning He composes those essences into their specific forms and compositions. Thus, the hadith underscores that the human form is entirely from Allah.

Correction: Razi shows they are distinct: al-Khaliq emphasizes knowledge of creation, al-Bari emphasizes bringing into existence from nothing, and al-Musawwir emphasizes giving specific form.

?

Proof Texts for the Word 'Nafs' in Scripture
Razi presents Qur'anic verses and hadiths cited to support the word nafs for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic verses and hadiths that anthropomorphists cite to argue that God has a 'nafs'.

Core Idea
The word 'nafs' appears in scripture about God, but not as a body part.

In this passage, Razi presents the evidence that proponents of anthropomorphism (tajsim) use to claim that God has a 'nafs' in a literal sense. They appeal to four Qur'anic verses and three hadiths. Understanding these proof texts is essential before we see how Razi refutes them in the following analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall content.
Notice that the passage is divided into two sections: Qur'anic verses and hadith reports.
2
Identify the four Qur'anic verses
Focus on each verse mentioned. Note the specific phrase containing 'nafs' for each.
The verses refer to Moses, Jesus, mercy, and warning.
3
Identify the three hadiths
Read each hadith carefully. Notice the different contexts: remembrance, glorification, and creation.
The first hadith is a hadith qudsi (God speaking directly).
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why are these texts being presented? How might an anthropomorphist use them?
Remember: Razi is not affirming these interpretations; he is setting them up for refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث فى لفظ النفس
احتجوا على إطلاق هذا اللفظ بالقرآن والأخبار . أما القرآن : فقوله تعالى في حق موسى عليه السلام : واصطنعتك لنفسي ، وقال حاكيا عن عيسى عليه السلام : تعلم ما في نفسي ولا أعلم ما في نفسك ، وقال في صفة أهل الثواب : كتب ربكم على نفسه الرحمة ، وقال في تخويف العصاة : ويحذركم الله نفسه . وأما الأخبار فكثيرة : الخبر الأول : ما روى أبو صالح عن أبي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: يقول الله تعالى: أنا مع عبدي حين يذكرني، فإن ذكرني في نفسه ذكرته في نفسي، وإن ذكرني في ملأ ذكرته في ملأ خير منه. الخبر الثاني: قوله عليه السلام: سبحان الله وبحمده عدد خلقه ورضاء نفسه وزنة عرشه. الخبر الثالث: عن أبي هريرة عن النبي أنه قال: لما قضى الله الخلق كتب في كتابه على نفسه فهو عنده: إن رحمتي سبقت غضبي.
The third chapter: On the word nafs. They argued for the use of this word with the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an: His statement, exalted be He, concerning Moses, peace be upon him: 'And I chose you for Myself (li-nafsi)'; and He said, narrating from Jesus, peace be upon him: 'You know what is in my nafs and I do not know what is in Your nafs'; and He said regarding the description of the people of reward: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself (nafsihi)'; and He said in frightening the disobedient: 'And God warns you of Himself (nafsahu).' As for the reports, they are numerous: The first report: Abu Salih narrated from Abu Hurayra from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'God, exalted be He, says: I am with My servant when he remembers Me. If he remembers Me in his nafs, I remember him in My nafs; and if he remembers Me in a gathering, I remember him in a better gathering.' The second report: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Glorified be God and with His praise, (as many as) the number of His creation, the satisfaction of His nafs, and the weight of His throne.' The third report: From Abu Hurayra from the Prophet that he said: 'When God created the creation, He wrote in His book with Himself (nafsihi) — it is with Him: "My mercy prevails over My wrath."'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter on the word 'nafs'. It presents the evidence that anthropomorphists (mujassima) use to argue that God has a 'nafs' in the literal sense. The author lists four Qur'anic verses: about Moses (20:41), Jesus (5:116), mercy (6:54), and warning (3:30). Then three hadiths: a hadith qudsi on remembrance, a hadith on glorification, and a hadith on mercy preceding wrath. This exposition sets up the proof texts that will be refuted in the following analysis.

Key Terms

القرآن
الأخبار
الذكر
اصطنعتك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verses and hadiths prove that God has a literal 'nafs' like a human soul or body.
Correction: Razi is presenting these texts as the evidence used by anthropomorphists, not as his own affirmation. He will later refute the literal interpretation and show that 'nafs' here must be understood metaphorically or in a manner befitting God.

Key Takeaway

Five Linguistic Meanings of 'Nafs'
Razi enumerates five meanings of 'nafs' with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct meanings of the Arabic word 'nafs' as explained by Imam Razi, each supported by Quranic evidence or common usage.

Core Idea
The word 'nafs' has five meanings: body, blood, spirit, intellect, and essence.

Imam Razi begins by clarifying that 'nafs' is not a univocal term; it carries multiple meanings in the Arabic language. Before we can discuss how it applies to God, we must first grasp its linguistic range. In this lesson, we explore the five meanings with their evidences, building a foundation for theological interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read Razi's enumeration of the five meanings from 'واعلم أن النفس' to 'وخامسها ذات الشيء وعينه'. Note how each meaning is introduced and supported.
Notice the pattern: 'أحدها... ثانيها...' etc.
2
Identify each meaning and its evidence
For each of the five meanings, identify the proof Razi gives (Quranic verse or common saying). Write them down.
3
Reflect on the fifth meaning
The fifth meaning 'essence' is crucial for the next lesson. Understand that it refers to the very core of a thing.
This meaning is used for God, as Razi will argue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النفس جاء في اللغة على وجوه: أحدها البدن. قال الله تعالى: كل نفس ذائقة الموت، ويقول القائل: كيف أنت في نفسك؟ يريد: كيف أنت في بدنك؟ وثانيها الدم. يقال: هذا حيوان له نفس سائلة، أي دم سائل. ويقال للمرأة عند الولادة: إنها نفست بخروج الدم منها عقيب الولادة. وثالثها الروح. قال الله تعالى: الله يتوفى الأنفس حين موتها. ورابعها العقل. قال تعالى: وهو الذي يتوفاكم بالليل ويعلم ما جرحتم بالنهار، وذلك لأن الأحوال بأسرها باقية حالة النوم إلا العقل فإنه هو الذي يختلف الحال فيه عند النوم واليقظة. وخامسها ذات الشيء وعينه. قال الله تعالى: وما يخدعون إلا أنفسهم، واقتلوا أنفسكم، ولكن ظلوا أنفسهم. فإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لفظ النفس في حق الله تعالى ليس إلا الذات والحقيقة. فقوله: واصطنعتك لنفسي كالتأكيد الذي يدل على مزيد المبالغة. فإن الإنسان إذا قال: جعلت هذه الدار لنفسي وعمرتها لنفسي فهم منه المبالغة. وقوله تعالى: تعلم ما في نفسي ولا أعلم ما في نفسك المراد: تعلم معلومى ولا أعلم معلومك. وكذا القول في بقية الآيات. وأما قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن رب العزة: فإن ذكرني في نفسه ذكرته في نفسي فالمراد: أنه إن ذكرني بحيث لا يطلع غيره على ذلك.
And know that 'nafs' in the language has several aspects: First: the body. God said: 'Every nafs will taste death.' And one says: 'How are you in your nafs?' meaning 'How are you in your body?' Second: the blood. It is said: 'This is an animal with flowing nafs,' i.e., flowing blood. And it is said of a woman at childbirth: 'She nafasa' because of the blood flowing after birth. Third: the spirit. God said: 'God takes the souls (anfus) at the time of their death.' Fourth: the intellect. God said: 'And He is the One who takes you at night and knows what you have committed during the day' — because all states remain during sleep except the intellect, for it is what changes during sleep and wakefulness. Fifth: the essence and self of a thing. God said: 'They deceive none but themselves (anfusahum);' 'Kill yourselves (anfusakum);' 'But they wronged themselves (anfusahum).' So if you know this, we say: The word 'nafs' with respect to God, exalted be He, is only the essence (al-dhat) and the reality (al-haqiqa). So His saying 'And I chose you for Myself (li-nafsi)' is like an emphasis indicating extreme exaggeration. For if a person says: 'I made this house for myself (li-nafsi) and built it for myself,' one understands exaggeration. And His saying: 'You know what is in my nafs and I do not know what is in Your nafs' means: You know my knowledge, and I do not know Your knowledge. And similarly for the rest of the verses. And as for his saying, peace be upon him, relating from the Lord of Might: 'If he remembers Me in his nafs, I remember him in My nafs,' the meaning is: if he remembers Me in such a way that no one else knows about it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis followed by theological interpretation. The author lists five meanings of 'nafs' in Arabic: body, blood, spirit, intellect, and essence. Each is supported by evidence. Then he applies this to the divine case: when used of God, 'nafs' means only the essence (al-dhat) and reality (al-haqiqa). He interprets the earlier verses and hadiths as emphasizing God's sole agency or expressing knowledge, not a literal 'self' in the human sense. This refutes anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
نفس
البدن
الدم
الروح
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fourth meaning of 'nafs' according to Razi?
A) Spirit
B) Intellect
C) Blood
D) Essence

Key Takeaway
The word 'nafs' in Arabic has five distinct meanings, the fifth being 'essence', which forms the basis for its theological application to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'kullu nafsin dha'iqatul mawt', which meaning of 'nafs' do you typically think of? How does focusing on the meaning 'body' change the weight of that verse in your heart?
?
The word 'nafs' can mean intellect, yet you sleep and your intellect is suspended. How often do you live as if your mind is always awake, neglecting that your intellect is a temporary gift from Allah? What will you do today to remember His absolute control over your very consciousness?
?
You use the word 'self' constantly—'I deceived myself', 'I wronged myself'. But does this lesson reveal that your 'self' is not a single thing? In which of the five meanings are you most heedless of Allah's ownership over that aspect of your existence?

Divine Application of 'Nafs' as Essence
Razi applies the fifth meaning of 'nafs' to God and reinterprets relevant texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'nafs' when applied to God means only His essence, and how key Quranic verses and a hadith are reinterpreted to avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
When used of God, 'nafs' refers to His essence, not a body or spirit.

Having established the linguistic meanings of 'nafs', Imam Razi now turns to its theological application. He argues that in reference to God, 'nafs' can only mean the essence and reality, and demonstrates this by reinterpreting verses that might otherwise suggest a literal 'self'. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage focusing on the principle
Read from 'فإذا عرفت هذا' to the end. Note Razi's key claim: 'لفظ النفس في حق الله تعالى ليس إلا الذات والحقيقة'.
2
Examine the reinterpretation of verses
Look at how Razi explains 'واصطنعتك لنفسي', 'تعلم ما في نفسي', and the hadith. Understand each as an expression of essence or secret knowledge.
He uses an analogy of a person saying 'I made this house for myself' to show emphasis.
3
Reflect on the need for this interpretation
Consider why it is necessary to interpret these texts as referring to essence rather than a literal self. This preserves God's transcendence.
This is a key principle in kalam to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النفس جاء في اللغة على وجوه: أحدها البدن. قال الله تعالى: كل نفس ذائقة الموت، ويقول القائل: كيف أنت في نفسك؟ يريد: كيف أنت في بدنك؟ وثانيها الدم. يقال: هذا حيوان له نفس سائلة، أي دم سائل. ويقال للمرأة عند الولادة: إنها نفست بخروج الدم منها عقيب الولادة. وثالثها الروح. قال الله تعالى: الله يتوفى الأنفس حين موتها. ورابعها العقل. قال تعالى: وهو الذي يتوفاكم بالليل ويعلم ما جرحتم بالنهار، وذلك لأن الأحوال بأسرها باقية حالة النوم إلا العقل فإنه هو الذي يختلف الحال فيه عند النوم واليقظة. وخامسها ذات الشيء وعينه. قال الله تعالى: وما يخدعون إلا أنفسهم، واقتلوا أنفسكم، ولكن ظلوا أنفسهم. فإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لفظ النفس في حق الله تعالى ليس إلا الذات والحقيقة. فقوله: واصطنعتك لنفسي كالتأكيد الذي يدل على مزيد المبالغة. فإن الإنسان إذا قال: جعلت هذه الدار لنفسي وعمرتها لنفسي فهم منه المبالغة. وقوله تعالى: تعلم ما في نفسي ولا أعلم ما في نفسك المراد: تعلم معلومى ولا أعلم معلومك. وكذا القول في بقية الآيات. وأما قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن رب العزة: فإن ذكرني في نفسه ذكرته في نفسي فالمراد: أنه إن ذكرني بحيث لا يطلع غيره على ذلك.
And know that 'nafs' in the language has several aspects: First: the body. God said: 'Every nafs will taste death.' And one says: 'How are you in your nafs?' meaning 'How are you in your body?' Second: the blood. It is said: 'This is an animal with flowing nafs,' i.e., flowing blood. And it is said of a woman at childbirth: 'She nafasa' because of the blood flowing after birth. Third: the spirit. God said: 'God takes the souls (anfus) at the time of their death.' Fourth: the intellect. God said: 'And He is the One who takes you at night and knows what you have committed during the day' — because all states remain during sleep except the intellect, for it is what changes during sleep and wakefulness. Fifth: the essence and self of a thing. God said: 'They deceive none but themselves (anfusahum);' 'Kill yourselves (anfusakum);' 'But they wronged themselves (anfusahum).' So if you know this, we say: The word 'nafs' with respect to God, exalted be He, is only the essence (al-dhat) and the reality (al-haqiqa). So His saying 'And I chose you for Myself (li-nafsi)' is like an emphasis indicating extreme exaggeration. For if a person says: 'I made this house for myself (li-nafsi) and built it for myself,' one understands exaggeration. And His saying: 'You know what is in my nafs and I do not know what is in Your nafs' means: You know my knowledge, and I do not know Your knowledge. And similarly for the rest of the verses. And as for his saying, peace be upon him, relating from the Lord of Might: 'If he remembers Me in his nafs, I remember him in My nafs,' the meaning is: if he remembers Me in such a way that no one else knows about it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis followed by theological interpretation. The author lists five meanings of 'nafs' in Arabic: body, blood, spirit, intellect, and essence. Each is supported by evidence. Then he applies this to the divine case: when used of God, 'nafs' means only the essence (al-dhat) and reality (al-haqiqa). He interprets the earlier verses and hadiths as emphasizing God's sole agency or expressing knowledge, not a literal 'self' in the human sense. This refutes anthropomorphism.

Correction: Razi clarifies that 'nafs' for God means only His essence, not a soul or body, and all relevant texts are interpreted accordingly as emphasis or reference to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
In the Quran and hadith, every use of 'nafs' in relation to God must be understood as referring to His essence, not a human-like self, preserving His transcendence.

Meaning of 'Nafs' in Hadith Expressions
Razi explains that 'nafs' in certain hadiths refers to essence, not soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in certain hadith phrases, the word 'nafs' refers to the divine essence (al-dhat) and serves as a rhetorical tool for emphasis, not as a reference to a soul or human-like self.

Core Idea
'Nafs' in divine contexts means essence, not soul—it's about emphasis, not anthropomorphism.

Imam Razi tackles a subtle point: when we encounter the word 'nafs' (self) in certain hadith expressions, are we meant to think of God having a soul? He shows that careful reading reveals the word simply emphasizes the divine essence. This lesson walks through his argument, clarifying how 'nafs' functions as a rhetorical device rather than a literal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's argument carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi cites three examples involving 'nafs' in divine speech.
2
Identify the three cases Razi examines
Razi discusses: (1) the concept of 'remembrance in the soul' applied to God, (2) the phrase 'pleasure of His Self,' and (3) the phrase 'upon Himself.' For each, see how he argues against a literal interpretation.
3
Understand Razi's conclusion
Razi concludes that in all these contexts 'nafs' means 'essence' (al-dhat) and its use is for emphasis and exaggeration (mubalaghah and ta'kid), not to indicate a soul. Reflect on why this matters for avoiding anthropomorphism.
This guards against thinking God has a soul like humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرته بإنعامى والإحساني ، من غير أن يطلع عليه أحد من عبيدى. لأن الذكر في النفس ، عبارة عن الكلام الخفى ، والذكر الكامن في النفس . وذلك على الله تعالى مجال . وأما قوله : (سبحان الله ، زنه عرشه ، ورضاء نفسه ، فالمراد : ما يرتضيه الله تعالى لنفسه و لذاته . اى تسبيحا يليق به . وأما قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كتب كتابا على نفسه، (1) زيادة فالمراد به : كتب كتابا ، وأوجب العمل به . والمراد من قوله « على نفسه ، : التأكيد والمبالغة في الوجوب واللزوم . فثبت : أن المراد بالنفس فى هذه المواضع : هو الذات . وأن الغرض من ذكر هذا اللفظ : المبالغة والتأكيد . وبالله التوفيق .
You mentioned it by My grace and favor, without any of My servants knowing about it. Because remembrance in the soul is an expression of hidden speech, and the hidden remembrance in the soul. That is applicable to God Almighty. And as for His saying: 'Glory be to God, weighing His Throne, and the pleasure of His Self,' the intended meaning is: what God Almighty approves for Himself and for His essence, i.e., a glorification befitting Him. And as for his saying, peace be upon him: 'He wrote a book upon Himself,' the intended meaning is: He wrote a book and made it obligatory to act by it. The intended meaning of 'upon Himself' is emphasis and exaggeration in obligation and necessity. So it is established that the intended meaning of 'nafs' in these contexts is the essence, and that the purpose of mentioning this word is exaggeration and emphasis. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical argument about the word 'nafs' (self/soul) in certain hadith phrases. Razi begins with a quote from a divine saying where God says 'You mentioned it by My grace and favor, without any of My servants knowing about it.' He then explains that 'remembrance in the soul' refers to hidden inner speech, and that such a concept is applicable to God. Next, he addresses two specific hadith expressions: First, the phrase 'Subhan Allah, zina 'arshihi, wa rida' nafsihi'—he interprets 'nafs' here as what God approves for His own essence, meaning a glorification that befits Him, not a literal self. Second, the Prophet's saying 'He wrote a book upon Himself'—he explains that 'upon Himself' is for emphasis, indicating the obligation is firmly established. He concludes that in all these contexts, 'nafs' means the essence (al-dhat), and the word is used for emphasis and exaggeration, not to denote a soul or human-like self. This guards against anthropomorphic interpretations.

The Linguistic Meaning of 'Al-Samad'
Razi's first answer: reinterpreting 'Al-Samad' as 'the one to whom one turns'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi reinterprets the divine name 'Al-Samad' linguistically to mean 'the one to whom all turn for needs' rather than a solid body.

Core Idea
'Al-Samad' means the Master to whom all turn, not a solid body.

Imam al-Razi confronts an anthropomorphist claim that the name 'Al-Samad' indicates God is a body. He offers two powerful responses. In this first lesson, we explore his linguistic argument: that 'Al-Samad' is derived from the verb 'ṣamada' meaning 'to intend' or 'to turn to', making Allah the ultimate refuge. Razi supports this with poetry and a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how Razi begins by citing the anthropomorphist position.
The passage includes the first answer.
2
Identify the linguistic shift
Observe how Razi redefines 'Samad' from a passive meaning (something solid) to an active meaning (the one to whom people turn).
3
Examine the evidence
Look at the poetry and the hadith from Ibn Abbas that Razi uses to support his reinterpretation.
These are crucial for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في لفظ الصمد
قال الله تعالى : ( الله . الصمد ، ذكر بعضهم في تفسير الصمد ، : أنه الجسم الذي لا جوف له . ومنه قول من يقول لسداد القارورة : العماد . وشيء محمد أي صلب، ليس فيه رخاوة . قال ابن قتيبة : دو على هذا التفسير تكون الدال مبدلة من التاء ، وقال بعضهم : « الصمد : الأملس ، من الحجر الذي لا يقبل الغبار ، ولا يدخل فيه شيء ، ولا يخرج منه شي.
واحتج قوم من جهال المشبهة بهذه الآية في إثبات أنه تعالى جسم . وهذا باطل . لأنا بينا أن كونه أحدا ينافي كونه جسما ، فمقدمة هذه الآية دالة على أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المراد من الصمد : هذا المعنى ، ولأن الصمد بهذا التفسير ، صفة الأجسام الغليظة ، وتعالى الله عن ذلك .
والجواب عنه من وجهين :
الأول : إن الصمد فعل بمعنى مفعول ، من صمد إليه أي قصد . والمعنى : أنه المصمود إليه في الحوائج . قال الشاعر :
ألا يكر الناعي حيرى بني أسد
بعمر و بن مسعود وبالسيد الصمد
•
و قال آخر :
علوته بحسامى ، ثم قلت له :
خذها حذيف ، فأنت السيد الصمد
والذي يدل على صحة هذا الوجه : ماروى ابن عباس رضى الله عنه - أنه لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا : ما الصمد ؟ فقال عليه السلام : «السيد الذي يصمد إليه فى الحوائج، قال أبو الليث صمدت صمد هذا الأمر : أي قصدت قصده .
الوجه الثانى فى الجواب : إنا سلمنا أن الصمد في أصل اللغة : المصمت الذى لا يدخل فيه شيء غيره. إلا أنا نقول : قد دللنا على أنه لا يمكن ثبوت هذا المعنى فى حق الله تعالى ، فوجب حمل هذا اللفظ على مجازه . وذلك لأن الجسم الذي يكون هذا شأنه، يكون مبرأ عن الانفصال والتباين والتأثر عن الغير . وهو - سبحانه وتعالى - واجب الوجود لذاته . وذلك يقتضى أن يكون تعالى غير قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فكان المراد من الصمد في حمه تعالى : هذا المعنى ) وبالله التوفيق
Chapter Four on the word 'Al-Samad'. Allah Most High said: 'Allah, Al-Samad.' Some have mentioned in the interpretation of 'Al-Samad' that it is the body which has no cavity. And from that is the saying of one who says for the stopper of the bottle: 'al-ʿimād'. And a thing 'muṣammad' meaning solid, having no softness. Ibn Qutaybah said: according to this interpretation, the dāl is substituted for the tā'. And some said: 'Al-Samad' is the smooth thing, from the stone that does not accept dust, neither does anything enter it nor anything come out of it. A group of the ignorant anthropomorphists used this verse to prove that He Most High is a body. This is false. For we have shown that His being 'Aḥad' (One) contradicts His being a body. So the preamble of this verse indicates that it is not possible that what is meant by 'Al-Samad' is this meaning. And because 'Al-Samad' according to this interpretation is an attribute of coarse bodies, and Allah is exalted above that. The answer to it is from two aspects: First: that 'Al-Samad' is a verbal noun in the sense of a passive participle, from 'ṣamada ilayhi' meaning he intended. And the meaning is that He is the one to whom one turns for needs. The poet said: 'Does not the announcer of death call the bewildered of Banī Asad, by ʿAmr ibn Masʿūd and the master, al-Ṣamad?' And another said: 'I overcame him with my sword, then said to him: take it, Ḥudhayf, for you are the master, al-Ṣamad.' And what indicates the correctness of this aspect is what Ibn ʿAbbās (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated: when this verse was revealed, they said: what is 'Al-Ṣamad'? He (peace be upon him) said: 'The master to whom one turns in needs.' Abū al-Layth said: 'ṣamadtu ṣamada hādhā al-amr' meaning I intended its direction. The second aspect in the answer: We concede that 'Al-Ṣamad' in the original language is the solid thing into which nothing else enters. However we say: we have demonstrated that it is not possible to establish this meaning with respect to Allah Most High, so it is necessary to carry this word to its metaphorical sense. For the body that is such is free from division, separation, and being affected by others. And He - glorified and exalted - is necessary of existence by Himself. And that requires that He Most High is not subject to increase or decrease. So what is meant by 'Al-Ṣamad' with respect to His (?) Most High is this meaning. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the anthropomorphist (mushabbiha) interpretation of the divine name 'Al-Samad' from Surah Al-Ikhlas. Razi begins by citing various lexical interpretations of the word: some say it means a solid body with no cavity, like a stopper; others say it means smooth stone that nothing enters or leaves. He then states that a group of ignorant anthropomorphists used this verse to argue that God is a body. Razi dismisses this as false, giving two reasons: first, the preceding phrase 'Allah, Al-Ahad' (God, the One) negates composition, and if God is One, He cannot be a body. Second, the interpretation of 'Samad' as solid applies to coarse bodies, and God is exalted above that. Then Razi provides two detailed answers to the anthropomorphists' claim. The first answer argues that 'Samad' is actually a verbal noun meaning 'the one to whom one turns for needs' (al-maṣmūd ilayhi fī al-ḥawā'ij). He supports this with two lines of poetry and a hadith from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defined 'Al-Samad' as 'the master to whom one turns in needs'. The second answer concedes that the lexical meaning might be 'solid and impenetrable', but argues that even then it cannot be applied literally to God. Instead, it must be taken metaphorically, because a body that is solid and impenetrable is free from division and being affected—attributes that suit God's necessary existence and immutability. Thus, the intended meaning is that God is not subject to increase, decrease, or change. Razi concludes with 'Wa billāhi al-tawfīq' (And with Allah is success).

Key Terms
الصمد
المشبهة
المصمود إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Al-Samad' literally means a solid, cavity-free body.
Correction: Razi shows that the root meaning is 'to intend/turn to', so 'Al-Samad' means the One to whom all turn for needs, not a physical body.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the correct linguistic meaning of 'Al-Samad'?
A) A solid body with no cavity
B) The one to whom one turns for needs
C) The eternal and unchanging
D) The self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
Razi reinterprets 'Al-Samad' linguistically to mean 'the Master to whom all turn for needs', invalidating the anthropomorphist claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are truly desperate, where do you instinctively turn? Is it to Allah alone, or do you rely on people, status, or possessions? How does the meaning of 'Al-Samad' reveal a gap between your belief and your actual behavior? Commit today to turn to Him in your next need, big or small.

You recite 'Allahu al-Samad' daily, but do you live as if He is the only one who can satisfy your needs? Name one specific need you have right now that you have not sincerely asked Him for. Will you ask Him now, and trust that He is indeed the Master to whom all turn?

The Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Al-Samad'
Razi's second answer: metaphorical interpretation of 'Al-Samad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses a metaphorical interpretation to reconcile the literal meaning of 'Al-Samad' as 'solid' with God's transcendence.

Core Idea
Even if 'Al-Samad' means solid, it metaphorically describes God's immutable self-sufficiency.

Imam al-Razi offers a second answer to the anthropomorphists: even if we concede the literal meaning of 'Al-Samad' as 'solid and impenetrable', it cannot apply literally to God. Instead, it must be taken metaphorically. Razi explains that a solid body is free from division and change, and these qualities point to God's necessary existence and absolute independence from increase or decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Razi's second answer carefully. Notice how he concedes the lexical meaning but then argues for metaphor.
2
Understand the metaphorical shift
See how Razi takes the attributes of a solid body (impenetrability, no division) and applies them to God in a way that affirms His transcendence.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on how this metaphorical interpretation aligns with God's necessary existence (wajib al-wujud) and immutability.
This lesson deepens your understanding of tanzih.
