The Anthropomorphist Proofs for Two Hands
Razi enumerates the anthropomorphist proofs from Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic and hadith evidence that anthropomorphists use to claim literal two hands for Allah.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists cite Quran and hadith to claim Allah has literal hands.

Imam Razi begins this chapter by presenting the arguments of those who insist on taking the attribute of 'hands' literally. He cites two Quranic verses and a detailed hadith. This lesson examines these proofs as they appear in his text, before his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the evidence types
Read the Arabic passage once to see the overall structure: first Quranic verses, then a hadith. Notice the author's framing: 'They argued with the Quran and the reports.'
The passage is a presentation of opposing views, not Razi's own position.
2
Examine the two Quranic verses
Look at the verses: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' (38:75) and 'But His two hands are outstretched' (5:64). Ask: How do these verses support a literal interpretation?
Anthropomorphists take 'beyond' and 'outstretched' as describing physical hands.
3
Analyze the hadith for details reinforcing literal hands
The hadith mentions 'His two hands were clenched' and 'both His hands are right, blessed'. Identify how these phrases suggest physical movement and distinction between right and left.
The hadith is long; only the relevant portion is quoted. The author notes this.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why Razi presents these proofs. He will later refute them. This lesson sets up the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن عشر في ما تمسكوا به في اثبات اليدين لله عز وجل. احتجوا بالقرآن، والأخبار. أما القرآن: فقوله تعالى: ( ما منعك أن تسجد لما خلقت بيدي )؟ وقوله تعالى: ( بل يداه مبسوطتان ). وأما الخبر: فما روى ابن خزيمة عن أبي هريرة ( رضي الله عنه ) قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: « لما خلق الله آدم ونفخ فيه: عطس. فقال: الحمد لله، فحمد الله، بإذن الله. فقال له ربه: يرحمك ربك يا آدم. ثم قال له: يا آدم اذهب إلى الملائكة فقل: السلام عليكم. فلما ذهب وقال: قالوا: عليكم السلام ورحمة الله. ثم رجع إلى ربه. فقال: هذه تحيتك وتحية بنيك وبنيهم. فقال الله تعالى - ويداه مقبوضتان - اختر أيهما شئت. فقال: اخترت يمين ربي - وكلتا يديه يمين مباركة - ثم بسطها فإذا فيها آدم وذريته. فقال: أي رب ما هؤلاء؟ فقال: هؤلاء ذريتك، فإذا كل إنسان مكتوب عمره بين عينيه. واعلم: أن هذا الحديث طويل. ومقصودنا هنا: هذا القدر. وقد
Chapter eighteen: On what they hold onto in proving the two hands for Allah Almighty. They argued with the Quran and the reports. As for the Quran: His saying: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' And His saying: 'But His two hands are outstretched.' As for the report: That which Ibn Khuzayma narrated from Abu Huraira (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him and his family) said: 'When Allah created Adam and breathed into him, he sneezed. He said: Praise be to Allah, so he praised Allah by Allah's permission. His Lord said to him: Your Lord has mercy on you, O Adam. Then He said: O Adam, go to the angels and say: Peace be upon you. When he went and said: They said: Peace be upon you and the mercy of Allah. Then he returned to his Lord. He said: This is your greeting and the greeting of your children and their children. Then Allah said - and His two hands were clenched - choose whichever you wish. He said: I choose the right hand of my Lord - and both His hands are right, blessed - then He spread them and there was Adam and his offspring. He said: O Lord, who are these? He said: These are your offspring, and behold, every person had his lifespan written between his eyes. Know that this hadith is long. Our purpose here is this amount. And'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the anthropomorphist arguments for the existence of literal 'two hands' for Allah. The author, Imam Razi, structures it as a chapter heading followed by two types of evidence: Quranic verses and a hadith. First, he cites two Quranic verses: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' (38:75) and 'But His two hands are outstretched' (5:64). These are commonly used by those who take the attribute of 'hands' literally. Then he presents a lengthy hadith from Ibn Khuzayma via Abu Huraira, which describes Adam's creation, his greeting of the angels, and then Allah showing Adam his offspring with their lifespans written, using the phrase 'His two hands were clenched' and 'He chose My right hand'. The hadith explicitly mentions 'both His hands are right, blessed'. The author notes that only the relevant portion is quoted, as the full hadith is long. The passage ends abruptly with 'وقد' (and), indicating continuation on the next page. This evidence is presented as the basis for the claim of literal hands, which Razi will likely refute in subsequent discussion.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage only presents the anthropomorphist claim; it does not affirm it. The verses and hadith are interpreted metaphorically by mainstream Sunni theology.

Key Takeaway

?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and being asked about your belief in His attributes. Will you be able to say that you accepted the verses and hadith without distorting their meaning? What concrete steps will you take today to ensure your belief is pure from anthropomorphism?

Proofs for Affirming Allah's Right Hand
Imam Razi cites Quran and hadith to prove the attribute of Yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic and hadith evidence used by Imam Razi to affirm the attribute of Yamin (Right Hand) for Allah.

Core Idea
The Quran and Sunnah affirm Allah's Right Hand, but without anthropomorphism or denial.

Imam Razi opens a new chapter dedicated to proving the divine attribute of Yamin (Right Hand) through scriptural sources. He first cites two Quranic verses that explicitly mention Allah's right hand, then presents three hadiths from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that reinforce this attribute. As you read, note that these texts are presented as evidence, not as an invitation to imagine a physical limb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysis. Then read the full English translation to grasp the flow of evidence.
The passage is a clear enumeration of proofs.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
Locate the two Quranic verses cited. Note their references and what they say about Allah's right hand.
Az-Zumar 67 and Al-Haqqah 45.
3
Examine the first two hadiths
Look at the hadith that says 'Both His hands are right' and the hadith about Allah seizing the earth and folding the heavens with His right hand.
These emphasize the comprehensiveness and power of Allah's right hand.
4
Study the third hadith about Adam
Consider the long hadith about Allah creating Adam and bringing forth his progeny with His right hand, distinguishing those for Paradise and Hell.
This hadith ties the attribute to divine decree.
5
Reflect on the purpose of these proofs
Understand that Imam Razi is providing textual evidence, but the Salaf accept these attributes without ta'til (denial) or tashbih (comparison).
The passage does not delve into interpretation; it only presents the proof texts.

The Nineteenth Chapter: On Affirming the Right Hand (Yamin) for Allah. They adduced (against him?) the Quran and the reports. As for the Quran, His statement Most High: 'And the heavens are folded in His Right Hand' (39:67), and His statement: 'We would have seized him by the right hand' (69:45). As for the reports, they are many: The first: His statement (peace be upon him): 'Both His hands are right.' The second: From Abu Hurairah that he said: The Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) said: 'Allah will seize the earth on the Day of Resurrection, and fold the heavens with His Right Hand. Then He will say: I am the King. Where are the kings of the earth?' The third: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated in the chapter on 'Faith in Destiny' from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: I heard the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) say: 'Verily Allah created Adam, then wiped his back with His Right Hand, and brought forth from him offspring, and said: I created these for Paradise, and with the deeds of the people of Paradise they will act. Then He wiped his back and brought forth from him offspring, and said: I created these for the Fire, and with the deeds of the people of the Fire they will act.'
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter that provides textual evidence for affirming the attribute of Yamin (Right Hand) for Allah. It first cites two Quranic verses: 'The heavens are folded in His Right Hand' (Az-Zumar 67) and 'We would have seized him by the right hand' (Al-Haqqah 45). Then it lists three hadiths: 1) The Prophet's statement 'Both of His hands are right'; 2) The hadith of Abu Hurairah about Allah seizing the earth and folding the heavens with His Right Hand on Judgment Day; 3) The hadith of Umar about Allah creating Adam and wiping his back with His Right Hand, bringing forth the progeny destined for Paradise and Hell. These texts are proof that the attribute 'Yamin' is affirmed, but according to the methodology of the Salaf, they are accepted without ta'til (denial) or tashbih (anthropomorphism).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming a right hand for Allah necessarily implies a physical limb or body part.
Correction: The passage only presents the proof texts without interpretation. In Sunni theology, these attributes are affirmed but understood in a manner befitting Allah's majesty, without anthropomorphism (tashbih) or denial (ta'til).

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi presents Quranic verses and three hadiths as textual proofs for the divine attribute of Yamin, which Muslims affirm without exaggeration or denial.

?

Evidence for Allah's Yameen from Qur'an and Hadith
Qur'anic and hadith evidence for yameen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic verses and prophetic hadith that are cited as evidence for affirming the attribute of yameen (right hand) for Allah.

Core Idea
The Qur'an and hadith affirm Allah's yameen, but its meaning requires careful interpretation.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the scriptural proofs that opponents of tanzih often use to argue for a literal hand. He quotes two Qur'anic verses and four hadith reports. These texts are the foundation for the discussion, but as we will see in the next lesson, their true meaning points to power, not a limb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passages carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice that the first part is the chapter heading and the second part presents the evidences from Qur'an and hadith in order.
There are two Qur'anic verses and four hadith reports. Identify each one.
2
Identify the evidences
List the two Qur'anic verses and the four hadith reports. For each hadith, note the narrator and a brief summary.
The hadith include: (1) 'Both His hands are right,' (2) Allah grasping the earth and folding the heavens, (3) creation of Adam and predestination, (4) charity placed in Allah's right hand.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Imam Razi lists these evidences. Are they proof of a literal hand or something else? This will be answered in the next lesson.
The purpose is to show that these texts must be interpreted in a way that befits Allah's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع عشر
في
إثبات اليمين لله تعالى

احتجوا عليه بالقرآن والأخبار.

أما القرآن: فقوله تعالى: "والسموات مطويات بيمينه" وقوله: "لأخذنا منه باليمين".

وأما الأخبار فكثيرة:
الأول: قوله عليه السلام: "كلتا يديه يمين".
والثاني: عن أبي هريرة أنه قال: قال رسول الله: "يقبض الله الأرض يوم القيامة، ويطوي السموات بيمينه، ثم يقول: أنا الملك، فأين ملوك الأرض؟".
والثالث: روى صاحب شرح السنة في باب "الإيمان بالقدر" عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه قال: سمعت النبي يقول: "إن الله خلق آدم، ثم مسح ظهره بيمينه، ثم استخرج منه ذرية فقال: خلقت هؤلاء للجنة، وبعمل أهل الجنة يعملون. ثم مسح ظهره فاستخرج منه ذرية فقال: خلقت هؤلاء للنار، وبعمل أهل النار يعملون".
والرابع: روى ابن خزيمة في كتابه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم قال: "إن أحدكم يتصدق بالتمرة من كسب طيب – ولا يقبل الله إلا طيبا – فيجعلها في يده اليمين، ثم يربيها كما يربي أحدكم فلوه وفصيله، حتى يصير مثل أحد".

واعلم: أن اليمين عبارة عن القوة والقدرة. والدليل عليه: أنه الجانب الأيمن سمي باليمين لأنه أقوى الجانبين، وسمي الخلف باليمين لأنه يقوى عزم الإنسان على الفعل أو الترك. قال الشاعر:
إذا ما راية رفعت لمجد
تلقاها عرابة باليمين

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: ظهر الوجه في قوله تعالى: "والسموات مطويات بيمينه". وأما قوله تعالى: "لأخذنا منه باليمين" فالمراد منه يمين المأخوذ أي أخذنا بيمين ذلك الإنسان، وهو كأن يقال: أخذت بيمين الصبي وذهبت به إلى المكتب. وإن كان المراد يمين الآخذ فالمراد منه القوة والقدرة. وإذا عرفت ذلك في الآية فاعرف مثله في الأخبار.
Chapter Nineteen: On Affirming al-Yameen for Allah Almighty. They argued against it with the Qur'an and the reports. As for the Qur'an: His saying, the Most High: 'And the heavens are folded in His right hand (yameenih),' and His saying: 'We would have seized him by the right hand (al-yameen).' As for the reports, they are many: First: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Both of His hands are right.' Second: From Abu Huraira that he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Allah will grasp the earth on the Day of Resurrection, and fold the heavens with His right hand (yameenih), then say: I am the King. Where are the kings of the earth?' Third: The author of Sharh al-Sunna narrated in the chapter 'Faith in al-Qadar' from Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: I heard the Prophet say: 'Verily, Allah created Adam, then wiped his back with His right hand (yameenih), then extracted from him offspring and said: I created these for Paradise, and with the deeds of the people of Paradise they act. Then He wiped his back and extracted from him offspring and said: I created these for the Fire, and with the deeds of the people of the Fire they act.' Fourth: Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book from the Prophet, may Allah bless him and his family and grant them peace, that he said: 'One of you gives charity with a date from a good earning – and Allah accepts only what is good – then He places it in His right hand (yameenih), then He raises it as one of you raises his foal and his young camel, until it becomes like Mount Uhud.' And know: that al-yameen is an expression for strength (al-quwwa) and power (al-qudra). And the evidence for it: that the right side (al-janib al-ayman) is called al-yameen because it is the stronger of the two sides, and the successor (al-khalaf) is called al-yameen because it strengthens (yuqawwi) a person's resolve to do or leave an action. The poet said: 'Whenever a banner is raised for honor, 'Araba receives it with the right hand (yameen).' If you know this, then we say: The meaning appears in His saying, the Most High: 'And the heavens are folded in His right hand (yameenih).' As for His saying, the Most High: 'We would have seized him by the right hand (al-yameen)' – the intended meaning is the right hand of the seized one, i.e., We seized that man by his right hand, analogous to saying: I took the boy by his right hand and took him to school. But if the intended meaning is the right hand of the seizer, then the intended meaning is strength and power. And if you understand that regarding the verse, then understand likewise regarding the reports.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument for affirming the attribute of 'al-Yameen' (the Right Hand) for Allah while interpreting it in a way that befits His transcendence. The author begins by stating that the opponents (likely anthropomorphists) argued using the Qur'an and hadith. He then presents the two Qur'anic verses: 'And the heavens are folded in His right hand' (az-Zumar 67) and 'We would have seized him by the right hand' (al-Haaqqa 45). Following that, he lists four prophetic hadith: the first stating that both of Allah's hands are right; the second describing Allah grasping the earth and folding the heavens with His right hand; the third narrating the creation of Adam and the predestination of his offspring to Paradise or Fire; and the fourth about charity being placed in Allah's right hand and nurtured. After presenting this evidence, the author provides the key interpretive move: 'al-yameen' is an expression for strength (quwwa) and power (qudra). He supports this linguistically by noting that the right side is called 'yameen' because it is stronger, and that the 'khalaf' (successor or back) is called 'yameen' because it strengthens resolve. He cites a line of poetry where 'yameen' clearly means power. Then he applies this interpretation: in the first verse, the meaning is the power of Allah; in the second verse, he offers two possibilities: either the 'yameen' belongs to the seized person (meaning his literal right hand) or, if attributed to the seizer, it means strength and power. He concludes that all reports should be understood similarly, i.e., metaphorically. The passage thus guards against anthropomorphism by affirming the wording but interpreting the meaning in a transcendent sense, consistent with the Sunni principle of tanzih.

Key Terms
اليمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many hadith reports does Imam Razi present in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi presents two Qur'anic verses and four hadith as the scriptural basis for affirming yameen, setting up the need for proper interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah takes your charity in His right hand and raises it until it becomes like a mountain, does that change how you view your small acts of giving? Will you now be more intentional about giving from a good earning, knowing that Allah values it so highly?
?
The hadith about Adam's offspring being created for Paradise or Fire can be unsettling. Do you trust Allah's wisdom and justice in predestination, or do you struggle with the idea that some are created for the Fire? What does this suggest about your heart's reliance on His mercy?
?
These texts affirm a 'right hand' for Allah, yet we know He is unlike His creation. When you encounter such descriptions, do you find yourself tempted to imagine a physical hand, or do you submit by saying 'we believe, we do not know how'? Which response brings more peace to your heart?

Interpreting Yameen as Power and Its Application
Linguistic interpretation of yameen as power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and rational argument for interpreting yameen as strength and power, and how this applies to the scriptural proofs.

Core Idea
Yameen in Arabic means strength and power, not a limb.

In the previous lesson, we saw the scriptural evidence for yameen. Now Imam Razi provides the key interpretive principle: yameen is a word that denotes strength and power. He supports this with linguistic reasoning, a poetic citation, and then applies it to the Qur'anic verses and hadith reports. This is a classic example of tanzih — affirming the scriptural wording while negating any anthropomorphic meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic argument
Read the part starting from 'واعلم' to the end. Notice how Imam Razi connects the word yameen to the root meaning of strength.
He points out that the right side is stronger, and that 'khalaf' (successor) is called yameen because it strengthens resolve.
2
Examine the application to the verses
See how Imam Razi applies this interpretation to the two Qur'anic verses. For the first verse, he says the meaning is clear: it refers to Allah's power. For the second, he offers two possibilities.
Pay attention to the nuance: the yameen could belong to the seized person, or if to Allah, it means power.
3
Apply the same to the hadith
Imam Razi concludes that the same interpretation should be applied to all the hadith reports. Understand why this is necessary for maintaining tanzih.
This ensures that no anthropomorphic meaning is attached to the reports.

?

Second Objection: Implies Ignorance of God
Imam Razi gives the second proof that the hadith imply God's ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of the hadith appears to imply that God lacked knowledge of the final numbers of people in Paradise and Hell, and how this serves as a rational objection requiring figurative interpretation.

Core Idea
A literal reading of the hadith suggests God did not know the final counts.

Imam Razi presents a second logical objection: if we take the hadith at face value, it suggests God did not know in advance how many people would enter Paradise or Hell. This directly contradicts the attribute of omniscience. Let's carefully trace his reasoning and see why the literal meaning cannot be correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: hadith implies ignorance, therefore over-creation, therefore need for extra creation and God's foot.
The sequence is important for understanding the argument.
2
Identify the key implication
Identify the main claim: the hadith implies God was not knowledgeable of the measure of people of reward and punishment.
3
Understand the rational contradiction
Ask yourself: Why would creating too large a Paradise and Hell point to ignorance? Because an all-knowing Creator would create exactly the right size from the start.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Connect to the need for ta'wil
Realize that this impossibility forces us to interpret the hadith metaphorically, not literally. Imam Razi uses this as a proof that the literal meaning is false.
This shows how rational arguments are used in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: إن هذا الحديث يقتضى أنه تعالى ما كان عالما بمقدار أهل الثواب والعقاب، فلا جرم خلق الله الجنة والنار أوسع من قدر الحاجة ولا جرم احتاج إلى أن يخلق للجنة خلقا آخر، وأن قدمه في النار.
Second: This hadith implies that He – exalted is He – was not knowledgeable of the measure of the people of reward and punishment, and therefore God created Paradise and Hell larger than the necessary capacity, and therefore He needed to create another creation for Paradise and to place His foot in Hell.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the literal interpretation of the hadith. Imam Razi argues that if we take the hadith at face value, it would mean that God did not know in advance how many people would enter Paradise and Hell. This is because the hadith says that Hell will not be filled until God places His foot in it, and Paradise will have extra space until God creates more people. If God already knew the exact numbers, He would have created them exactly the right size from the beginning. The fact that there is extra space and a need to fill it later implies that God's knowledge was incomplete – which is impossible for God. Therefore, the literal meaning cannot be correct.

Key Terms
عالما بمقدار
أهل الثواب والعقاب
أوسع من قدر الحاجة
يخلق للجنة خلقا آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith simply describes a future event without implicating God's knowledge.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the literal description logically implies God lacked foreknowledge of the final numbers, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does the second objection say the hadith wrongly implies about God?
A) That He is unjust
B) That He is not all-knowing
C) That He is unable to fill Hell
D) That He changes His mind

Key Takeaway
The second objection reveals that a literal reading of the hadith clashes with divine omniscience, proving it must be interpreted figuratively.

Would you have the courage to challenge them using the logic from this lesson, or would you remain silent to avoid conflict? What is at stake if you stay silent?

Divine Joy and Wonder: Metaphors for Divine Pleasure
Razi interprets hadiths about God's joy and wonder as metaphors for divine pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi interprets scriptural attributions of joy and wonder to God as metaphorical expressions of divine approval and magnification.

Core Idea
God's 'joy' and 'wonder' are metaphors for His pleasure and magnification of deeds.

This passage from Razi's Asas al-Taqdis addresses two scriptural descriptions that seem to attribute human emotions to God: joy (farah) and wonder ('ajab). Razi explains that these are not literal emotions but rather metaphors for divine approval (rida) and the magnification of actions. Through careful interpretation, he shows how the language of the Quran and Hadith can be understood in a way befitting God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the content sink in before you interpret.
Notice the two main topics: joy and wonder.
2
Identify the interpretation of joy
Find where Razi presents the hadith on repentance and welcoming, and note how he interprets 'joy' as a name for 'approval' (rida).
This is a key metaphorical move.
3
Identify the interpretation of wonder
Find the discussion of wonder, including the Qur'anic reading, and note how Razi interprets it as 'magnification' (isti'zam) of an act.
Wonder arises from magnifying something great.
4
Reflect on the overall approach
Consider why Razi uses metaphorical interpretation for these attributes. How does this preserve God's transcendence?
This is a standard kalam method for anthropomorphic expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن والعشرون فى الفرح عن النعمان بن بشير - رضي الله عنه ـ عن النبي الا أنه قال الله أفرح بتوبة العبد من العبد ، إذا ضلت راحلته في أرض فلاة في يوم قائظ ، و راحلته عليها زاده ومزاده. إذا ضلت ( راحلته أيقن بالهلاك) (1) وإذا وجدها ، فرح بذلك . فالله أشد فرحاً بتوبة عبده من هذ العبد ، وقال : ( لا يطأ الرجل المساجد للصلاة والذكر ، إلا تبشبش الله تعالى إليه ، ما يقتبشيش أهل الغائب بغائبهم ، إذا قدم عليهم ، والتأويل : هو أن من يرضى بالشيء يفرح به فيسمى الرضا : بالفرح . وهذا هو الكلام به. . في البشاشة . ومن هذا الباب قوله : ( عجب ربكم من شاب ليس له ما صبوة ، وفي حديث آخر : عجب ربكم من ثلاثة : القوم إذا اصطفوا في الصلاة ، والقوم إذا صلوا في قتال المشركين ، ورجل يقوم إلى الصلاة في جوف الليل، وقرأ : «بل عجبت و يسخرون» ـ بضم التاء - وذلك بدل على ثبوت هذا المعنى في حق الله تعالى . واعلم : أن التأويل هو : أن التعجب حالة تحصل عند استعظام الأمر . فإذا عظم الله تعالى فعلا ، إما في كثرة ثوابه أو فى كثرة عقابه ، جاز إطلاق لفظ التعجب عليه (و بالله التوفيق ) (۲)
The twenty-eighth chapter on joy: From al-Nu'man ibn Bashir (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (ﷺ) that he said: 'Allah is more joyful at the repentance of a servant than a man whose riding beast is lost in a barren land on a scorching day, and his beast carries his provisions and his water-skin. When his riding beast is lost, he is certain of destruction, and when he finds it, he rejoices. So Allah is more intensely joyful at the repentance of His servant than this man.' And He said: 'No man treads the mosques for prayer and remembrance except that Allah Most High welcomes him, more than the family of an absent one welcomes their absent when he returns to them.' The interpretation: it is that whoever is pleased with something rejoices at it, so approval is called 'joy'. And this is the speech about alacrity. From this same category is His saying: 'Your Lord wonders at a young man who has no youthful passion.' And in another hadith: 'Your Lord wonders at three: people who line up in prayer, people who pray while fighting the polytheists, and a man who gets up for prayer in the depth of the night.' And He recited: 'Rather, I am amazed, and they mock,' with damma on the ta. That indicates the affirmation of this meaning with respect to Allah Most High. Know that the interpretation is: wonder is a state that occurs when magnifying a matter. So if Allah Most High magnifies an act, whether for the abundance of its reward or for the abundance of its punishment, it is permissible to apply the term 'wonder' to Him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal interpretation of scriptural texts that attribute joy (farah) and wonder ('ajab) to God. The author first presents two hadiths: one comparing God's joy at repentance to a man finding his lost beast, and another about God welcoming those who frequent mosques. He then gives the interpretation: joy is a metaphor for divine approval (rida), because one who is pleased rejoices. He then extends this to the concept of 'bishasha' (alacrity). Next, he addresses hadiths about God's wonder, including the Qur'anic reading 'bal 'ajibtu' (with dammah). He explains that wonder is a state arising from magnifying something; thus, when God magnifies an act—whether for great reward or great punishment—the term 'wonder' can be applied metaphorically to Him. The passage systematically shows that these terms do not imply literal emotions in God but are expressions of His pleasure or magnification of deeds.

عجب
بشاشة
استعظام

Correction: Razi explains that these are metaphors for divine approval and magnification, not literal emotions.

Interpreting the Divine Attribute of Joy and Wonder
Razi interprets divine joy and wonder via ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses ta'wil to interpret anthropomorphic attributes like joy and wonder, affirming them while preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Divine joy and wonder are real attributes interpreted as pleasure and magnification, not emotions.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses two scriptural attributes: joy (al-farah) and wonder (al-ta'ajjub). Through careful ta'wil, he shows that these terms refer to God's pleasure and his magnifying of actions, not emotional changes. This lesson will guide you through his argument, revealing a consistent method for understanding anthropomorphic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the two hadith about joy and cheerfulness. Notice the language of intense human emotion. Ask: how can we attribute such feelings to Allah?
The hadith use vivid imagery to emphasize the magnitude of divine joy.
2
Study Razi's ta'wil for joy
Razi says joy = pleasure (rida). Understand the principle: whoever is pleased with something rejoices. So for Allah, joy is his pleasure.
This shows that ta'wil connects the attribute to a compatible divine meaning.
3
Extend to wonder
Razi applies the same method: wonder = deeming something great. For Allah, it means he magnifies an act by reward or punishment.
The Quranic verse 'But I wonder' supports this interpretation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن والعشرون فى الفرح عن النعمان بن بشير - رضي الله عنه ـ عن النبي الا أنه قال الله أفرح بتوبة العبد من العبد ، إذا ضلت راحلته في أرض فلاة في يوم قائظ ، و راحلته عليها زاده ومزاده. إذا ضلت راحلته أيقن بالهلاك وإذا وجدها ، فرح بذلك . فالله أشد فرحاً بتوبة عبده من هذ العبد ، وقال : لا يطأ الرجل المساجد للصلاة والذكر ، إلا تبشبش الله تعالى إليه ، ما يقتبشيش أهل الغائب بغائبهم ، إذا قدم عليهم ، والتأويل : هو أن من يرضى بالشيء يفرح به فيسمى الرضا : بالفرح . وهذا هو الكلام به. . في البشاشة . ومن هذا الباب قوله : عجب ربكم من شاب ليس له ما صبوة ، وفي حديث آخر : عجب ربكم من ثلاثة : القوم إذا اصطفوا في الصلاة ، والقوم إذا صلوا في قتال المشركين ، ورجل يقوم إلى الصلاة في جوف الليل، وقرأ : «بل عجبت و يسخرون» ـ بضم التاء - وذلك بدل على ثبوت هذا المعنى في حق الله تعالى . واعلم : أن التأويل هو : أن التعجب حالة تحصل عند استعظام الأمر . فإذا عظم الله تعالى فعلا ، إما في كثرة ثوابه أو فى كثرة عقابه ، جاز إطلاق لفظ التعجب عليه و بالله التوفيق
Chapter Twenty-Eight: On Joy. From al-Nu'man ibn Bashir may Allah be pleased with him from the Prophet that he said: Allah rejoices more at the repentance of His servant than a man whose riding camel is lost in a barren land on a scorching day, and his riding camel carries his provision and water-skin. When his riding camel is lost, he is certain of destruction; and when he finds it, he rejoices at that. So Allah rejoices more at the repentance of His servant than this man. And he said: 'A man does not tread the mosques for prayer and remembrance except that Allah Almighty cheerfully welcomes him, more intensely than the family of an absent one cheer when their absent one returns to them.' The interpretation (al-ta'wil) is that whoever is pleased with a thing rejoices in it, so pleasure (al-rida) is called joy (al-farah). That is the meaning of cheerfulness. And from this category is his saying: 'Your Lord wonders at a young man who has no youthful passion.' And in another hadith: 'Your Lord wonders at three: people who line up in prayer, people who pray while fighting the polytheists, and a man who gets up to pray in the middle of the night.' And he recited: 'But I wonder and they mock' with damma on the ta'. That indicates the affirmation of this meaning with respect to Allah Almighty. Know that interpretation (al-ta'wil) is that wonder is a state that occurs when a matter is deemed great. So when Allah Almighty magnifies an act, either by its abundant reward or by its abundant punishment, it is permissible to apply the term wonder to Him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive (ta'wil) argument about the divine attribute of joy (al-farah). Razi begins by quoting a hadith in which the Prophet describes Allah's joy at a servant's repentance exceeding a man's joy at finding his lost camel. He also cites a hadith about divine cheerfulness (bashasha) toward those who go to mosques. Razi then provides the interpretation: joy in God is not an emotional change but rather denotes divine pleasure (rida). He extends this to the attribute of wonder (ta'ajjub), as in the hadith 'Your Lord wonders at a young man...' and the Quranic verse 'بل عجبت' (But I wonder). Razi explains that wonder is a state of deeming something great; for God, it is permissible because it refers to God's magnifying an act by great reward or punishment. Thus, the passage establishes that such attributes are interpreted relative to God's perfection, focusing on the end-purpose (pleasure, magnification) rather than any physical or emotional change.

Interpreting Divine Modesty: The Universal Rule
Razi defines human modesty and proposes a universal interpretive principle for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's method for interpreting bodily attributes like modesty when applied to God, specifically the principle of focusing on ultimate purposes rather than initial physical states.

Core Idea
Divine modesty does not mean shame; it means generosity.

Razi begins by defining human modesty as a state of change and brokenness due to fear of blame. When this attribute is applied to God, it must be reinterpreted. He lays down a universal rule: bodily attributes are carried over to their ultimate purposes, not their physical beginnings. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the Quranic verse and hadith that attribute modesty to God.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand Razi's definition of human modesty
Razi defines modesty as a change and brokenness overtaking a person due to fear of blame. He links it linguistically to 'life' (hayat).
This definition is crucial for seeing why ta'wil is needed.
3
Grasp the universal rule: nihayat al-aghard over bidayat al-a'rad
Razi states that attributes pertaining to bodies, when applied to God, are interpreted by their ultimate purposes, not their initial states. This is the key principle.
Remember: purpose not physical beginning.
4
Apply the rule to modesty
Razi gives the example: the beginning of modesty is physical change (taghayyur), but its end-purpose is avoiding blame or showing respect. So for God, modesty means He avoids blame by being generous.
This explains why God's modesty is about generosity, not shame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع والعشرون الحياء قال الله تعالى :: إن الله لا يستحى أن يضرب مثلا ما ، وروى سلمان - رضى الله عنه - عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن الله حى كريم، يستحى إذا رفع العبد يديه إليه ، أن يردهما صفرا . حتى يضع فيهما خيرا و اعلم : أن الحياء : تغير وانكسار يعترى الإنسان في خوف ما يعاتب ويذم به . واشتقاقه من الحياة . يقال : حي الرجل . كما يقال : نسى الرجل و خشی ، وسطى القوس, إذا أغفلت هذه الأعضاء . جعل الحياء ما يعتريه من الانكسار . والتغير متنكس القوة ، منتقص الحياة . ولهذا يقال : فلان هلك حياء من كذا ومات جياء من كذا ورأيت الهلال في وجهه من شدة الحياء ، وذاب حياء . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا بد من تأويله . وفيه وجهان : الأول : وهو أن القانون الكلى في أمثال هذه الصفات : أن كل صفة تثبت للعبد مما يختص بالأجسام . إذا وصف الله تعالى بذلك ، فهو محمول على نهايات الأغراض، لا على بدايات الأعراض، مثاله : أن الحياء حالة تحصل للإنسان، ولها مبدأ ونهاية . أما البداية فيها فهو التغير
Chapter Twenty-Nine: Modesty (al-Haya'). Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah is not shy to strike a parable of a mosquito...' And Salman may Allah be pleased with him narrated from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, Allah is alive, generous; He is shy that when a servant raises his hands to Him, to send them back empty until He places good in them.' And know that modesty (al-haya') is a change and brokenness that overtakes a person in fear of what he may be blamed and reproached for. Its derivation is from life (al-hayat). It is said: 'The man lived' (hayya), just as it is said: 'The man forgot' (nasiya) and 'feared' (khashiya), and 'the bow became stiff' (satiya) when these organs are neglected. Modesty is made what overtakes one of brokenness and change, decreasing strength, diminishing life. Hence it is said: 'So-and-so died of modesty from such-and-such' and 'died of modesty from such-and-such' and 'I saw the crescent in his face from the intensity of modesty' and 'he melted from modesty.' When this is established, we say: its interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary. And regarding it there are two aspects. The first is that the universal rule concerning such attributes is that every attribute that is affirmed for the servant which pertains to bodies, when Allah Almighty is described by it, is applied to the ultimate purposes (nihayat al-aghard), not to the initial states (bidayat al-a'rad). Its example is that modesty is a state that occurs for a human; it has a beginning and an end. As for its beginning, it is the change...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is both a definition and an interpretive (ta'wil) argument about the divine attribute of modesty (al-haya'). Razi begins by quoting a Quranic verse and a hadith that attribute modesty to God. He then defines modesty in human terms: it is a state of change (taghayyur) and brokenness (inkisar) that occurs in a person due to fear of blame. He traces its linguistic derivation from 'life' (hayat). After establishing the human meaning, he asserts that such an attribute must be interpreted for God. He presents the first of two interpretative principles: the universal rule is that any bodily attribute affirmed for a human, when applied to God, is carried over to its ultimate purposes (nihayat al-aghard) rather than its initial physical states (bidayat al-a'rad). The example is modesty: its beginning is the physical change (taghayyur), but its end-purpose is avoiding blame or showing respect. Hence for God, modesty is interpreted as that which leads to generosity (like not sending hands back empty), not as a change. The passage ends mid-sentence but the core principle is clearly stated.

تأويل
نهايات الأغراض
بدايات الأعراض
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think modesty in God implies a literal state of shame or weakness.
Correction: Razi clarifies that modesty is interpreted by its ultimate purpose (generosity), not by the physical change (taghayyur) that occurs in humans.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the universal rule for interpreting bodily attributes when applied to God?
A) They are to be understood literally without interpretation.
B) They are carried over to their ultimate purposes (nihayat al-aghard), not their initial states (bidayat al-a'rad).
C) They are only applicable to prophets, not to God.
D) They must be rejected entirely as anthropomorphic.

Key Takeaway
Razi's key principle is that attributes implying bodily change in humans are interpreted for God by focusing on their ultimate purpose, not their initial state.

?
If you were raised believing God gets 'angry' or 'jealous' in a human sense, does this universal rule challenge you to rethink other attributes? Are you willing to strip away every mental image of God as a magnified human, even if it feels uncomfortable? What might you lose—or gain—by doing so?

The Meaning of Haya' (Modesty) in Language and Human Experience
Razi defines haya' linguistically and in human experience before applying it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defines haya' (modesty) linguistically and in human experience, and why this human definition cannot be directly applied to God.

Core Idea
In human beings, haya' is a change and breaking; but in God, it must mean something entirely different.

Before we can understand what it means to say that Allah is 'modest' (Hayy), we must first understand the word haya' as it applies to humans. Imam Razi provides a detailed linguistic and experiential definition, tracing the word to its root in 'life'. This lesson unpacks that definition and the idiomatic expressions that surround it, setting the stage for a deeper theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence from Quran and Hadith
Begin by reading the verse and hadith that establish the attribute of haya' for Allah. Notice how they affirm modesty for God without explanation.
The hadith describes God's haya' in relational terms with supplicants.
2
Study Razi's human definition
Read the definition: 'تغير وانكسار' (change and breaking) that overcomes a person due to fear of blame. Ponder how this is a psychological state specific to humans.
3
Examine the etymology and idioms
Learn that haya' derives from 'life' (الحياة). Then look at the idiomatic expressions like 'perished from haya'' to see how deeply ingrained this concept is in Arabic.
This linguistic connection is crucial for later kalām arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع والعشرون: الحياء. قال الله تعالى: إن الله لا يستحي أن يضرب مثلاً. وروى سلمان رضي الله عنه عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: إن الله حي كريم يستحي إذا رفع العبد يديه إليه أن يردهما صفراً حتى يضع فيهما خيراً. واعلم: أن الحياء: تغير وانكسار يعتري الإنسان في خوف ما يعاتب ويذم به. واشتقاقه من الحياة. يقال: حي الرجل. كما يقال: نسى الرجل وخشي وسطى القوس إذا أغفلت هذه الأعضاء. جعل الحياء ما يعتريه من الانكسار والتغير متنكس القوة منتقص الحياة. ولهذا يقال: فلان هلك حياء من كذا ومات حياء من كذا ورأيت الهلال في وجهه من شدة الحياء وذاب حياء.
Chapter Twenty-nine: Al-Haya' (Modesty). Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah is not ashamed to strike an example.' And Salman, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'Indeed Allah is Hayy (Modest), Generous; He is modest, when a servant raises his hands to Him, to return them empty until He places good in them.' And know: that al-haya' is a change and breaking that overcomes a person in fear of what he may be blamed and censured for. Its derivation is from al-hayah (life). It is said: hayya al-rajul (the man was modest), just as it is said: nasiya al-rajul (the man forgot), khashiya (feared), and sata al-qaws (the bow was bent) when these organs are neglected. Haya' is made [to be] that which overcomes him of breaking and change, diminishing strength, reducing life. For this reason it is said: So-and-so perished from haya' of such-and-such, and died from haya' of such-and-such, and I saw the crescent in his face from the intensity of haya', and he melted from haya'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage opens with the chapter heading 'Al-Haya'' and immediately cites scriptural evidence: the Quranic verse 'Indeed, Allah is not ashamed to strike an example' and a hadith describing Allah as modest ('Hayy, Karim') who is reluctant to return empty the hands of a supplicant. Razi then provides a definition of haya' as it occurs in human beings: a state of change and inner breaking that overtakes a person due to fear of blame or censure. He traces the etymology to 'life' (al-hayah), noting that the verb 'hayya' is used analogously to verbs like 'nasiya' (to forget) and 'khashiya' (to fear). The resulting state of haya' is described as diminishing one's strength and life-force—hence expressions like 'he perished from haya'' or 'he melted from haya''. This detailed human definition sets the stage for the need to interpret such an attribute when applied to God, since change and breaking cannot be attributed to the Creator.

Key Terms
الحياء
تغير
انكسار
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that haya' (modesty) in God is exactly the same as the human feeling of shame or embarrassment.
Correction: Razi's definition shows that human haya' involves change and weakness, which cannot be attributed to God. Therefore, the divine attribute must be understood in a way that befits His perfection, often interpreted as the attribute of not leaving a supplicant empty-handed or as a form of divine generosity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the linguistic root of the word 'haya' (modesty)?

B) From 'hyia' (shame)
C) From 'haya' (bashfulness)

Key Takeaway
Razi's definition of haya' as a human state of change and breaking, and its derivation from life, establishes that the divine attribute must be interpreted differently.

Razi mentions that a person can 'perish from haya'' or 'melt from haya''. Has your modesty ever reached such an intensity? If not, what might that indicate about your fear of Allah's displeasure, and what step can you take today to cultivate a more genuine haya'?

Razi's Hermeneutical Principle for Divine Attributes
Razi establishes a hermeneutical rule for interpreting bodily-based divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's universal principle for interpreting anthropomorphic attributes of God: that they refer to ends (outcomes) rather than beginnings (bodily changes).

Core Idea
Divine attributes refer to outcomes, not emotional processes.

Razi begins by asserting that certain attributes found in revelation must be interpreted allegorically. He lays down a universal rule: when an attribute involves a bodily or emotional process in humans, for God it only indicates the final result, not the initial change. This protects divine transcendence while preserving the scriptural text. In this lesson, we will unpack this principle and see how it works with two examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessity of interpretation
Razi opens by stating that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for such attributes. Recognize that this is a response to literalism that would attribute change to God.
The phrase 'إذا ثبت هذا' refers back to a prior argument about divine transcendence.
2
Learn the universal principle
Identify the core principle: 'every attribute proven for the servant which is specific to bodies, if Allah is described by it, it is carried upon the ends of purposes, not upon the beginnings of accidents.' This is the hermeneutical key.
'Nihayat al-aghrad' (ends of purposes) and 'bidayat al-a'rad' (beginnings of accidents) are technical terms.
3
Examine the example of haya'
Razi analyzes haya' (modesty/shyness). In humans, it begins with a bodily change (blushing) due to fear of blame, and ends with refraining from the blameworthy action. For God, only the end (refraining) is intended.
This example shows how the principle is applied to a specific attribute.
4
Examine the example of ghadab
Similarly, ghadab (anger) in humans begins with boiling blood and desire for revenge, and ends with delivering punishment. When attributed to God, only the punishment is meant, not the emotional upheaval.
This reinforces the principle and shows its consistency.
5
Reflect on the purpose of the principle
Consider why Razi formulates this rule. It safeguards divine transcendence by denying that God undergoes change or passion, while affirming that the scriptural terms are meaningful in terms of their outcomes.
This principle is a common tool in Sunni theology for interpreting mutashabihat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لا بد من تأويله. وفيه وجهان: الأول: وهو أن القانون الكلي في أمثال هذه الصفات: أن كل صفة تثبت للعبد مما يختص بالأجسام. إذا وصف الله تعالى بذلك، فهو محمول على نهايات الأغراض، لا على بدايات الأعراض. مثاله: أن الحياء حالة تحصل للإنسان، ولها مبدأ ونهاية. أما البداية فيها فهو التغير الجسماني الذي يلحق الإنسان من خوف أن ينسب إلى القبيح. وأما النهاية فهي أن يترك الإنسان ذلك الفعل. فإذا ورد الحياء في حق الله تعالى، فليس المراد منه: ذلك اللحوق الذي هو مبدأ الحياء وتقدمته. بل المراد: هو ترك الفعل الذي هو منتهاه وغايته. وكذلك الغضب له مبدأ وهو غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام، وله غاية وهو إيصال العقاب إلى المغضوب عليه. فإذا وصفنا الله تعالى بالغضب، فليس المراد ذلك المبدأ - أعني غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام - بل المراد: تلك النهاية، وهي إنزال العقاب. فهذا هو القانون.
Once this is established, we say: it must be interpreted. And there are two ways: the first: which is the universal principle concerning such attributes: that every attribute proven for the servant which is specific to bodies, if Allah Almighty is described by it, it is carried upon the ends of purposes, not upon the beginnings of accidents. Its example: indeed haya' is a state that occurs for a human, and it has a beginning and an end. As for the beginning in it, it is the bodily change that overtakes a person from fear of being attributed to the ugly. And as for the end, it is that the person refrains from that action. So when haya' is reported regarding Allah Almighty, what is meant by it is not that occurrence which is the beginning and antecedent of haya'; rather, what is meant is the refraining from the action which is its outcome and purpose. And similarly anger has a beginning, which is the boiling of the blood of the heart and the desire for revenge, and it has an end, which is delivering punishment to the one against whom anger is directed. So when we describe Allah Almighty with anger, what is meant is not that beginning—I mean the boiling of the blood of the heart and the desire for revenge—but rather what is meant is that end, which is sending down punishment. This is the principle.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two interpretations for understanding attributes like haya' when applied to God. Razi begins by affirming that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary. He states a universal principle ('al-qanun al-kulli'): whenever an attribute is proven for a human servant that is specific to bodies (i.e., involves physical or emotional states), if that attribute is used for Allah, it must be understood as referring to the final purposes or outcomes (nihayat al-aghrad) rather than the initial bodily changes (bidayat al-a'rad). He illustrates with haya': in humans, haya' has a beginning—a bodily change due to fear of being blamed—and an end—the action of refraining from the blameworthy act. When attributed to God, only the end is meant: His refraining from returning empty-handed (as in the hadith). Similarly, anger in humans begins with boiling blood and desire for revenge, but ends with inflicting punishment. For God, only the end (punishment) is intended, not the emotional upheaval. This principle, Razi concludes, is the universal rule for such attributes.

قانون كلي
نهايات الأغراض
بدايات الأعراض
الغضب
الحياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing emotions like anger or shyness to God implies literal emotional change in the divine essence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that only the external outcome (e.g., punishment) is intended, not the internal emotional process.

Checkpoint

B) The fear of being blamed
C) The end result of refraining from an action

Key Takeaway
Razi's universal principle safeguards divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic attributes as referring to their ultimate outcomes, not their initial physical processes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What concrete step can you take today to train your heart to think of Allah's attributes only in ways befitting His majesty?

?
Razi says this principle is universal for all bodily attributes. Is there any attribute of Allah that you have been interpreting with human-like beginnings without realizing it? Will you commit to pausing whenever you encounter such attributes and consciously shifting your mind to the outcome only?

Interpreting God's Attributes: The Principle of Beginning and End
Razi explains the principle of interpreting attributes by distinguishing their beginning and end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret human-like attributes such as modesty and anger when applied to God, using the distinction between the beginning and the end of an attribute.

Core Idea
Divine attributes point to outcomes, not emotions.

When we read that God is 'angry' or 'modest', our minds may jump to human emotions. Razi clarifies that for God, only the end result of these attributes is meant—not the emotional or physical beginning. This lesson establishes the principle that underpins the interpretation of many divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses very precise language for beginning and end.
2
Identify the beginning and end for modesty and anger
Find the definitions of the beginning (mabda') and end (ghaya) for each attribute as described by Razi.
3
Understand why only the end is attributed to God
Ask yourself why Razi insists that only the end is meant for God. Consider what this says about divine transcendence.
This connects to the principle that God is free from physical and emotional changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسماني الذي يلحق الإنسان من خوف أن ينسب إلى القبيح، وأما النهاية فهي أن يترك الإنسان ذلك الفعل، فإذا ورد الحياء في حق الله تعالى، فليس المراد منه: ذلك اللحوق الذي هو مبدأ الحياء وتقدمته، بل المراد: هو ترك الفعل الذي هو منتهاه وغايته. وكذلك الغضب له مبدأ وهو غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام، وله غاية وهو إيصال العقاب إلى المغضوب عليه. فإذا وصفنا الله تعالى بالغضب، فليس المراد ذلك المبدأ – أعني غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام – بل المراد: تلك النهاية، وهي إنزال العقاب. فهذا هو القانون.

والثاني: إن الذي لا يجوز على الله تعالى من جنس هذه الأوصاف. فهل يجوز ذكره على سبيل النفي عن الله تعالى؟ قال بعضهم: إنه لا يجوز إطلاق هذه الألفاظ على طريقة النفي، بل يجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى لا يوصف بها. فأما أن يقال: إنه لا يستحي ويطلق ذلك في حال، لأنه يوهم نفي ما يجوز عليه. وما ذكره الله تعالى في كتابه من قوله: لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم، ولم يلد ولم يولد، فهو وإن كان في صورة النفي، لكنه ليس في الحقيقة. بل المراد منه: نفي صحة الاتصاف. وكذا قوله: ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد، وقوله: ما اتخذ الله من ولد، وقوله: وهو يطعم ولا يطعم. وليس كل ما ورد في القرآن إطلاقه جاز أن يطلق في المخاطبات، بل الحق: أنه لا يجوز إطلاق ذلك إلا مع بيان أنه محال ممتنع في حق الله تعالى.

وقال آخرون: لا بأس بإطلاق هذا النفي، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عن الله تعالى. فكان الإخبار عن عدمها صدقا، فوجب أن يجوز ذلك النفي. بقى أن يقال: إن الأخبار عن انتفائها يقتضى صحة إطلاقها عليه. إلا أنا نقول: هذه الدلالة ممنوعة. فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه. بل لو قرن باللفظ ما يدل على انتفاء الصحة أيضاً، كان ذلك أحسن من حيث إنه يكون مبالغة في البيان في إزالة الإيهام. وليس يلزم من كون غيره أحسن منه كونه في نفسه قبيحا. والله أعلم.
Body-based [shame] which befalls a person from fear that he would be attributed to something ugly. As for the end, it is that the person leaves that action. So when modesty (al-haya') is mentioned with respect to God Most High, what is meant by it is not that occurrence (al-luhuq) which is the beginning (mabda') of modesty and its precedence, but rather what is meant is the leaving of the action which is its end (muntaha) and its outcome (ghaya). Likewise, anger (al-ghadab) has a beginning, which is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance, and it has an end, which is delivering punishment to the one upon whom anger is directed. So when we describe God Most High with anger, what is meant is not that beginning – namely the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance – but rather that end, which is the infliction of punishment. This then is the principle (al-qanun).

Second: That which is not permissible regarding God Most High among this kind of attributes. Is it permissible to mention it by way of negation (nafy) from God Most High? Some said: It is not permissible to utter these words by way of negation; rather, it must be said that He Most High is not described by them. So it is not said: 'He does not feel shame' and then to utter that in any state, because it may imply the negation of what is permissible regarding Him. And what God Most High mentioned in His Book, such as His saying: 'Slumber does not overtake Him nor sleep' (2:255), and 'He did not beget nor was He begotten' (112:3), although it is in the form of negation, it is not in reality [merely negation]; rather what is meant by it is the negation of the validity of attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf). Likewise His saying: 'It did not befit God that He should take a child' (19:35), and His saying: 'God has not taken a child' (23:91), and His saying: 'And He feeds and is not fed' (6:14). And not everything that occurs in the Quran in its utterance is permissible to utter in discourses; rather the truth is that it is not permissible to utter that except with clarification that it is impossible and prohibited concerning God Most High.

And others said: There is no harm in uttering this negation, because these attributes are negated from God Most High. So the report about their absence is true, so that negation must be permissible. It remains to be said that the report about their absence implies the validity of uttering them concerning Him. But we say: This implication is prevented (mamnu'a). For the report about the absence of something does not indicate that that thing is permissible regarding Him or prohibited regarding Him. Rather, if one coupled with the wording something that also indicates the denial of validity, that would be better inasmuch as it would be an exaggeration in clarification for removing ambiguity. And it does not follow from the fact that something else is better than it, that it is in itself ugly. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition of a principle (qanun) for interpreting certain attributes ascribed to God that originate in human experiences, and then a discussion on the permissibility of using negative statements about God regarding such attributes. 

The author, Imam Razi, begins by analyzing two attributes: modesty (haya') and anger (ghadab). He notes that each has a beginning (mabda') and an end (ghaya). For modesty, the beginning is the feeling of shame that deters one from an ugly act; the end is the act of leaving that act. For anger, the beginning is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance; the end is the actual infliction of punishment. When these attributes are applied to God, only the end—not the emotional or physical beginning—is intended. Thus, "God is angry" means He punishes, not that He experiences an emotional state. This is the established principle.

Razi then raises a second question: for attributes that are impossible for God (like human emotions), is it permissible to simply negate them—e.g., "God is not shy" or "God does not get angry"? He reports two positions. The first position holds that such naked negations are not allowed because they might wrongly imply that the attributes could be possible for God (i.e., that He could have them, even though He doesn't). Instead, one should say "God is not described by such attributes" to emphasize impossibility. Razi supports this by pointing to Quranic verses that appear as negations but actually negate the very validity of the attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf), not just the occurrence. For example, "Slumber does not overtake Him" means it is impossible for God to be subject to slumber. He adds that not every Quranic expression can be used freely in ordinary speech; it is only permissible when accompanied by a clarification that the attribute is impossible for God.

The second position holds that simple negations are permissible because the attributes are truly absent from God, and truthful statements are allowed. Razi then critiques this position: reporting absence does not by itself indicate whether the attribute is possible or impossible for the subject. However, if one adds an indicator of invalidity, that is better for removing ambiguity. He concludes that while the better approach is to couple negation with a statement of impossibility, the mere negation is not necessarily ugly (qabih), though it is not the best. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Throughout, Razi demonstrates a nuanced method: he avoids both crude anthropomorphism and simplistic negation, insisting on careful linguistic and theological precision.

Key Terms
الحياء
الغضب
المبدأ
النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when God is described with anger, He experiences the same emotion as humans.
Correction: Razi clarifies that only the end result (punishment) is meant, not the emotional beginning.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's principle, when we attribute 'anger' to God, what is the correct understanding?
A) God experiences a boiling of blood
B) God desires vengeance
C) God inflicts punishment
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
When interpreting human-like attributes for God, we affirm only the outcome, not the emotional process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God 'gets angry' at sinners, do you subconsciously imagine a divine tantrum? How does this lesson force you to purge that image—and what does it replace it with? Will you let go of that comfortable but false picture today?
?
If God's anger is solely the infliction of just punishment, then His mercy must also be solely the bestowal of grace. How does that change your sense of accountability? Are you living as if the outcome of your deeds is what truly matters, or do you hide behind sentiment?
?
This principle strips away all human emotion from God's attributes. Is there any attribute of God you have secretly 'humanized'—thinking He 'feels' disappointment or pride? What would it cost you to surrender that image and worship a God whose actions are pure, untainted by passion? Are you ready to make that surrender now?

Negating Divine Attributes: The Permissibility of Negative Statements
Razi discusses whether it is permissible to negate impossible attributes from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether it is permissible to simply negate impossible attributes from God, and Razi's preferred position.

Core Idea
Negation of an attribute from God must avoid implying that the attribute could be possible.

After establishing how to interpret positive attributes, Razi tackles a new question: Can we simply say 'God is not shy' or 'God does not get angry'? He presents two scholarly views and their reasoning. This lesson unpacks that debate and Razi's nuanced conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two positions Razi reports.
2
Identify the two positions on negation
Find the first position that prohibits bare negation and the second that permits it. Note the reasoning and Quranic evidence cited.
3
Understand Razi's critical analysis and final ruling
Razi critiques the second position and gives his preferred approach. Pay attention to his conclusion that simpler negation is not ugly but not the best.
This connects to the principle of proper theological language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسماني الذي يلحق الإنسان من خوف أن ينسب إلى القبيح، وأما النهاية فهي أن يترك الإنسان ذلك الفعل، فإذا ورد الحياء في حق الله تعالى، فليس المراد منه: ذلك اللحوق الذي هو مبدأ الحياء وتقدمته، بل المراد: هو ترك الفعل الذي هو منتهاه وغايته. وكذلك الغضب له مبدأ وهو غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام، وله غاية وهو إيصال العقاب إلى المغضوب عليه. فإذا وصفنا الله تعالى بالغضب، فليس المراد ذلك المبدأ – أعني غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام – بل المراد: تلك النهاية، وهي إنزال العقاب. فهذا هو القانون.

والثاني: إن الذي لا يجوز على الله تعالى من جنس هذه الأوصاف. فهل يجوز ذكره على سبيل النفي عن الله تعالى؟ قال بعضهم: إنه لا يجوز إطلاق هذه الألفاظ على طريقة النفي، بل يجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى لا يوصف بها. فأما أن يقال: إنه لا يستحي ويطلق ذلك في حال، لأنه يوهم نفي ما يجوز عليه. وما ذكره الله تعالى في كتابه من قوله: لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم، ولم يلد ولم يولد، فهو وإن كان في صورة النفي، لكنه ليس في الحقيقة. بل المراد منه: نفي صحة الاتصاف. وكذا قوله: ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد، وقوله: ما اتخذ الله من ولد، وقوله: وهو يطعم ولا يطعم. وليس كل ما ورد في القرآن إطلاقه جاز أن يطلق في المخاطبات، بل الحق: أنه لا يجوز إطلاق ذلك إلا مع بيان أنه محال ممتنع في حق الله تعالى.

وقال آخرون: لا بأس بإطلاق هذا النفي، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عن الله تعالى. فكان الإخبار عن عدمها صدقا، فوجب أن يجوز ذلك النفي. بقى أن يقال: إن الأخبار عن انتفائها يقتضى صحة إطلاقها عليه. إلا أنا نقول: هذه الدلالة ممنوعة. فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه. بل لو قرن باللفظ ما يدل على انتفاء الصحة أيضاً، كان ذلك أحسن من حيث إنه يكون مبالغة في البيان في إزالة الإيهام. وليس يلزم من كون غيره أحسن منه كونه في نفسه قبيحا. والله أعلم.
Body-based [shame] which befalls a person from fear that he would be attributed to something ugly. As for the end, it is that the person leaves that action. So when modesty (al-haya') is mentioned with respect to God Most High, what is meant by it is not that occurrence (al-luhuq) which is the beginning (mabda') of modesty and its precedence, but rather what is meant is the leaving of the action which is its end (muntaha) and its outcome (ghaya). Likewise, anger (al-ghadab) has a beginning, which is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance, and it has an end, which is delivering punishment to the one upon whom anger is directed. So when we describe God Most High with anger, what is meant is not that beginning – namely the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance – but rather that end, which is the infliction of punishment. This then is the principle (al-qanun).

Second: That which is not permissible regarding God Most High among this kind of attributes. Is it permissible to mention it by way of negation (nafy) from God Most High? Some said: It is not permissible to utter these words by way of negation; rather, it must be said that He Most High is not described by them. So it is not said: 'He does not feel shame' and then to utter that in any state, because it may imply the negation of what is permissible regarding Him. And what God Most High mentioned in His Book, such as His saying: 'Slumber does not overtake Him nor sleep' (2:255), and 'He did not beget nor was He begotten' (112:3), although it is in the form of negation, it is not in reality [merely negation]; rather what is meant by it is the negation of the validity of attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf). Likewise His saying: 'It did not befit God that He should take a child' (19:35), and His saying: 'God has not taken a child' (23:91), and His saying: 'And He feeds and is not fed' (6:14). And not everything that occurs in the Quran in its utterance is permissible to utter in discourses; rather the truth is that it is not permissible to utter that except with clarification that it is impossible and prohibited concerning God Most High.

And others said: There is no harm in uttering this negation, because these attributes are negated from God Most High. So the report about their absence is true, so that negation must be permissible. It remains to be said that the report about their absence implies the validity of uttering them concerning Him. But we say: This implication is prevented (mamnu'a). For the report about the absence of something does not indicate that that thing is permissible regarding Him or prohibited regarding Him. Rather, if one coupled with the wording something that also indicates the denial of validity, that would be better inasmuch as it would be an exaggeration in clarification for removing ambiguity. And it does not follow from the fact that something else is better than it, that it is in itself ugly. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition of a principle (qanun) for interpreting certain attributes ascribed to God that originate in human experiences, and then a discussion on the permissibility of using negative statements about God regarding such attributes. 

The author, Imam Razi, begins by analyzing two attributes: modesty (haya') and anger (ghadab). He notes that each has a beginning (mabda') and an end (ghaya). For modesty, the beginning is the feeling of shame that deters one from an ugly act; the end is the act of leaving that act. For anger, the beginning is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance; the end is the actual infliction of punishment. When these attributes are applied to God, only the end—not the emotional or physical beginning—is intended. Thus, "God is angry" means He punishes, not that He experiences an emotional state. This is the established principle.

Razi then raises a second question: for attributes that are impossible for God (like human emotions), is it permissible to simply negate them—e.g., "God is not shy" or "God does not get angry"? He reports two positions. The first position holds that such naked negations are not allowed because they might wrongly imply that the attributes could be possible for God (i.e., that He could have them, even though He doesn't). Instead, one should say "God is not described by such attributes" to emphasize impossibility. Razi supports this by pointing to Quranic verses that appear as negations but actually negate the very validity of the attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf), not just the occurrence. For example, "Slumber does not overtake Him" means it is impossible for God to be subject to slumber. He adds that not every Quranic expression can be used freely in ordinary speech; it is only permissible when accompanied by a clarification that the attribute is impossible for God.

The second position holds that simple negations are permissible because the attributes are truly absent from God, and truthful statements are allowed. Razi then critiques this position: reporting absence does not by itself indicate whether the attribute is possible or impossible for the subject. However, if one adds an indicator of invalidity, that is better for removing ambiguity. He concludes that while the better approach is to couple negation with a statement of impossibility, the mere negation is not necessarily ugly (qabih), though it is not the best. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Throughout, Razi demonstrates a nuanced method: he avoids both crude anthropomorphism and simplistic negation, insisting on careful linguistic and theological precision.

Key Terms
النفي
صحة الاتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any negation of an attribute from God is automatically permissible and clear.
Correction: Razi warns that bare negation can be misleading; it's better to clarify impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the first position reported by Razi, why is it problematic to simply say 'God does not feel shame'?
A) Because it is false
B) Because it may imply that feeling shame is possible for God
C) Because God does feel shame
D) Because it is disrespectful

Key Takeaway
When negating attributes from God, it is better to indicate that the attribute is impossible, not just absent, to avoid any misunderstanding.

Objection and Answer on Negating Attributes
Imam Razi responds to an objection about permissibility of negating divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against negating attributes of Allah and the author's response clarifying that reporting negation does not imply possibility.

Core Idea
Reporting absence does not imply possibility; negation can be truthful without attributability.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology about whether it is permissible to negate attributes of Allah. Some argued that since the negation is true, it must be permissible. An objector countered that negation implies the attribute could be attributed. Imam Razi responds with a precise logical distinction that resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the different voices: 'others said', 'it remains to be said', and 'we say'.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the claim that negation is permissible (first sentence) and the objection that reporting negation implies possibility (second sentence starting with 'بقى أن يقال').
The objection is introduced as 'it remains to be said'.
3
Analyze the author's answer
Look at the response starting with 'فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء...' The author denies the implication and suggests a stronger phrasing. Ask yourself: Why does the author think the objection fails?
The key phrase: 'لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال آخرون : لا بأس بإطلاق هذا النفي، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية الله تعالى . فكان الإخبار عن عدمها صدقا، فوجب أن يجوز ذلك. بقى أن يقال : إن الأخبار عن انتفائها يقتضي صحة إطلاقها عليه - إلا أنا نقول : هذه الدلالة ممنوعة . فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه بل لو قرن باللفظ ما يدل على انتفاء الصحة أيضاً، كان ذلك أحسن من حيث إنه يكون مبالغة في البيان في إزالة الإيهام . وليس يلزم من كون غيره أحسن منه، كونه في نفسه قبيحاً . والله أعلم
And others said: There is no harm in uttering this negation, because these attributes are negated of Allah Almighty. So reporting their non-existence is true, so it is necessary that it be permissible. It remains to be said: Reporting their negation implies the validity of attributing them to Him—but we say: This indication is not conceded. For reporting the non-existence of something contains no indication that that thing is possible for Him or impossible for Him. Rather, if one were to accompany the expression with something indicating also the negation of validity, that would be better insofar as it would be an exaggeration in clarifying the removal of ambiguity. And it does not follow from something else being better than it, that it is itself ugly. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion concerning the permissibility of negating attributes of Allah. The author presents the view of some who argue that negating attributes is permissible because the statement is truthful—since these attributes are indeed negated of Allah, reporting their absence is true, so it should be allowed. An objector then raises a point: that reporting the negation of these attributes implies that those attributes could conceivably be attributed to Allah (otherwise why negate them?). The author responds by denying this implication. He explains that reporting the non-existence of something does not indicate whether that thing is possible or impossible for the subject. Moreover, he adds that if one were to include a phrase that also negates the validity of applying the attribute, that would be even better as it removes ambiguity more forcefully. However, the fact that something else is better does not make the original statement itself ugly. The passage ends with 'And Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
نفي (negation)
صفات (attributes)
الإخبار عن عدم (reporting absence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Declaring negations about Allah implies those attributes are possible for Him.
Correction: Reporting absence does not indicate possibility or impossibility; it is simply a true statement.

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because negation presupposes possibility.
B) No, because reporting absence does not indicate possibility or impossibility.
C) Yes, but only if the attribute is a positive one.
D) No, unless the statement is explicitly conditional.

Key Takeaway
Negating attributes of Allah is permissible because a truthful statement about non-existence does not imply that the attribute could be attributed.
