Second Hadith: The Book of Mercy Above the Throne
Reporting that Allah's mercy is recorded in a book above the Throne

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this hadith is interpreted by different theological schools and why it is significant in discussions of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The hadith about mercy and wrath is a key proof text for those who assert spatial location for Allah.

This hadith reports that Allah wrote a book stating His mercy prevails over His wrath, and the book is with Him above the Throne. While the literal wording seems to place the book—and by implication Allah—in a direction, mainstream Sunni theology interprets this figuratively. Let's explore the text and its doctrinal implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the phrasing 'فهو عنده فوق العرش' (it is with Him above the Throne).
The hadith is narrated by the author of Sharh al-Sunna from Abu Hurayra.
2
Identify the key controversial phrase
Focus on 'عنده فوق العرش' – 'with Him above the Throne'. This phrase implies a spatial relationship between the book and Allah.
Proponents of anthropomorphism use this to argue Allah has a location.
3
Understand the Ash'ari interpretation
Ash'ari scholars interpret 'with Him' metaphorically as 'in His knowledge' or 'under His decree', avoiding any spatial attribution to Allah.
They emphasize tanzih (transcendence) and reject direction for Allah.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why this hadith is a major point of contention in kalam: it touches on the attributes of mercy and wrath and the issue of Allah's relationship to creation.
The phrase 'رحمتي سبقت غضبي' confirms mercy predominates, which is a key point for the Ash'ari view of divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر الثاني: ما روى صاحب شرح السنة في باب رسمة رحمة الله تعالى عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: لما قضى الله الخلق كتب كتابا، فهو عنده فوق العرش: إن رحمتي سبقت غضبي.
The second report: What the author of Sharh al-Sunna narrated in the chapter on the drawing of Allah's mercy, from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him: When Allah created creation, He wrote a book, and it is with Him above the Throne: 'My mercy prevails over My wrath.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This brief hadith is presented as further scriptural evidence. It states that when Allah created the heavens and earth, He wrote a book stating that His mercy overcomes His wrath. The book is described as being 'with Him above the Throne' (فهو عنده فوق العرش). The phrase 'with Him' using 'inda and 'above the Throne' explicitly place the book in a spatial relation to Allah, implying that Allah Himself is in a direction, or at least that what is with Him is located above. The proponents use this to argue for direction.

Key Terms
العرش
الرحمة
الغضب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves that Allah is physically located above the Throne.
Correction: The Ash'ari interpretation holds that 'with Him' means in His knowledge and decree, not spatial location. Allah is beyond time and place.

Checkpoint
Where is the book of mercy written by Allah located according to this hadith?
A) In the Preserved Tablet.
B) In the hearts of the believers.
C) With Allah above the Throne.
D) In the Garden of Eden.

Key Takeaway
The hadith's literal wording suggests spatial location for Allah, but it is understood figuratively in orthodox Islamic theology to preserve divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you seeking mercy from anything or anyone other than Allah, or are you relying completely on the mercy He has already decreed? What hidden idol of self-reliance might you need to break?

Four Objections to Istiwa as Istila and Their Answer
Razi presents four objections to interpreting istiwa as istila and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised against interpreting God's istiwa as istila and how the response redefines the term while also explaining the special mention of the Throne.

Core Idea
Objections to theological terms often stem from their literal meanings; answers refine definitions.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a classic objection-response exchange. The objector argues that interpreting istiwa as istila (domination) is impossible because the word implies weakness, opponents, or pre-existence of the object. Razi's answer first redefines istila to avoid these implications, then gives two reasons why the Throne is specifically mentioned. This unit shows how kalam thinkers defend metaphorical interpretations of Qur'anic terms by carefully redefining key concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Start by reading the four objections carefully. Each one points to a linguistic or theological problem with taking istiwa as istila.
Notice that each objection assumes a certain meaning of the word.
2
Understand the answer
Read Razi's response. He first redefines istila as 'perfect power free from opponent' to neutralize the objections. Then he addresses why the Throne is specified.
See how the redefinition directly counters each objection without changing the word.
3
Analyze the specification of the Throne
Examine the two reasons for specifying the Throne: 1) it is the greatest creation, 2) it serves as an intermediary for divine management (per al-Ghazali).
The second reason is based on a cosmological model that Razi may later critique.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how this exchange illustrates the kalam method: objections are dismantled by clarifying meanings, not by denying the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا التأويل غير جائز . لوجوه : الأول : إن الاستيلاء عبارة عن صول الغلبة بعد العجز ، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال . الثاني : إنه إنما يقال : فلان استوى على كذا ، إذا كان له منازع ينازعه وذلك فى حق الله تعالى محال . الثالث : إنه إنما يقال فلان استولى على كذا : إذا كان المستولى عليه موجودا قبل ذلك وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . لأن العرش إنما حدث بتكوينه وتخليقه . الرابع : إن الاستيلاء بهذا المعنى حاصل بالنسبة إلى كل المخلوقات. فلا يبقى لتخصيص العرش بالذكر فائدة . والجواب: إن مرادنا بالاستيلاء القدرة التامة الخالية عن المنازع والمعارض والمدافع . وعلى هذا التقدير، فقد زالت هذه المطاعن بأسرها . وأما تخصيص العرش بالذكر . ففيه وجهان : الأول : إنه أعظم المخلوقات ، فخص بالذكر لهذا السبب ، كما أنه خصه بالذكر في قوله : ه هو رب العرش العظيم لهذا المعنى . قال الشيخ الغزالي - رحمه الله . في كتاب إلجام العوام» : «السبب في هذا التخصيص : هو أنه تعالى يتصرف في جميع العالم ، ويدير الأمر من السماء إلى الأرض ، بواسطة العرش فإنه تعالى لا يحدث صورة في العالم مالم يحدثها في العرش كما لا يحدث النقاش والكاتب صورة البناء على البياض ، مالم يحدثها في الدماغ ، بل لا يحدث صورة البناء فى الخارج . مالم يحدث صورته في الدماغ ، بواسطة القلب والدماغ يدبر الروح أمر عالمه الذي هو يدبر فكذا بواسطة العرش يدبر الله أمر كل العالم
If it is said: this interpretation is not permissible. For several reasons: First: istila [taking over] is an expression of overcoming after inability, and that is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Second: it is only said 'so-and-so established [istawa] over something' if he has an opponent who contends with him, and that is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Third: it is only said 'so-and-so took over [istawla] something' if that over which he took over existed before that, and this is impossible with respect to God Almighty, because the Throne only came into being by His bringing into being and creation. Fourth: taking over in this sense is obtained with respect to all creatures, so there remains no benefit in specifying the Throne by mention. The answer: what we mean by istila is perfect power free from any opponent, adversary, or defender. On this estimation, all these objections are removed entirely. As for specifying the Throne by mention, there are two aspects: first, it is the greatest of creatures, so it is specified by mention for this reason, just as He specified it by mention in His saying 'He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' for this meaning. Shaykh al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, said in his book Iljam al-'Awam: 'The reason for this specification is that He, exalted, disposes in the entire world and directs the command from heaven to earth through the medium of the Throne. For He does not bring about any form in the world without first bringing it about in the Throne, just as the painter and writer do not bring about the form of a building on the blank page without first bringing it about in the brain, rather they do not bring about the form of the building externally without first bringing about its form in the brain through the medium of the heart. And the brain manages the command of its world which it manages; likewise through the medium of the Throne God manages the command of the entire world.'
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector raises four linguistic and theological arguments against interpreting the istiwa (establishment) of God over the Throne as istila (domination). First, istila implies overcoming after weakness, which is impossible for God. Second, istiwa (in the sense of settling) presupposes an opponent, impossible for God. Third, istila requires the dominated object to pre-exist, but the Throne is created by God, so it didn't pre-exist His act. Fourth, if istila is taken generally, then the Throne is not special, nullifying the specification. The answer redefines istila as 'perfect power free from any opponent'—this negates the first three objections because the term no longer implies weakness or opponent. Then it addresses the specification of the Throne: it is specified because it is the greatest creation (analogous to the verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne'), and also because, as al-Ghazali explains, the Throne is the intermediary through which God administers the world, similar to the role of the brain in human action. This explanation, however, is based on a particular philosophical cosmology, which Razi will later critique.

Key Terms
استيلاء
استواء
تأويل
عرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that interpreting istiwa as istila is invalid because istila always implies prior weakness or an opponent.
Correction: The author shows that istila can be redefined as 'perfect power free from opponent,' which removes those implications. The term's validity depends on the technical definition, not the literal sense.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy the author uses to answer the first three objections?
A) He denies that istila means taking over.
B) He redefines istila as perfect power without prior weakness or opponent.
C) He argues that God can have attributes of weakness.
D) He says the objections only apply to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Theological terms must be understood by their intended definitions, not their literal senses, allowing for valid interpretations of ambiguous Qur'anic expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi refines the definition of 'istila' to answer objections. Has there been a time when you misunderstood a religious term and acted on that misunderstanding? Are you willing to revisit that term now with a more careful definition?
?
Al-Ghazali's explanation of the Throne as an intermediary for divine administration is profound. How does it change the way you think about God's relationship to the universe? Does it make His governance more intimate or more distant in your heart?

The Two Meanings of 'Above' (Fawq) in the Quran
Razi demonstrates the dual meaning of 'fawq' in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'fawq' has two distinct meanings in the Quran: superiority in rank/power and spatial height, and you will see Quranic examples of both.

Core Idea
'Fawq' in the Quran often means power and rank, not physical direction.

Before we can judge whether verses about God being 'above' imply direction, we must first examine the range of meaning of the word itself. Imam Razi points out that the same word is used in the Quran for both spatial height and for superiority in power or rank. This lesson introduces that linguistic fact.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice how Razi begins by stating that the word 'fawq' is used in two ways: for rank/power and for direction.
The passage includes several Quranic verses as examples.
2
Identify the two meanings
Look for the two meanings: first, 'fawq' meaning superiority in rank and power (with examples from Quranic verses about knowledge, power, and authority). Second, 'fawq' meaning spatial height (with the example of 'what is above it' meaning greater in smallness).
Notice that the second example is non-spatial but uses 'fawq' for an abstract increase.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Razi ends this section with a question: since the word can mean both, why do they insist on the spatial meaning? Think about how this sets up the next proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذي ذكروه ثانياً وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على ذكر الفوقية . فجوابه : أن لفظ الفوق يستعمل في الرتبة والقدرة . فقد قال الله تعالى : ( وفوق كل ذي علم عليم - وإنا فوقهم قادرون يد الله فوق أيديهم والمراد بالفوقية في هذه الآيات : الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة ) ويستعمل فى الفوق ، الذي بمعنى الجهة قال تعالى : بعوضة فما فوقها أي أزيد منها في صفة الصغر والحقارة . وإذا كان لفظ الفوق محتملا للفوق فى الرتبة ، والفوق في الجهة ، فلم حملوه على الفوق في الجهة ؟ والذي يدل على أن المراد بلفظ الفوق ههنا : الفوق بالقدرة والمكنة وجوه : الأول : إنه قال : « وهو القاهر فوق عباده والفوقية المقرونة بالقهر ، هى الفوقية بالقدرة والمكنة ، لا بمعنى الجهة . بدليل : أن الحارس قد يكون فوق السلطان فى الجهة ، ولا يقال إنه فوق السلطان
As for what they mentioned secondly, which is clinging to verses that include mention of 'above' [al-fawqiyya], its answer is that the word 'above' [al-fawq] is used for rank and power. For God Almighty said: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' and 'And We are above them, dominant' and 'The hand of God is above their hands' — and what is meant by 'above' in these verses is superiority by force and power. And it is used for 'above' in the sense of direction. He said: 'a mosquito or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness and contemptibility. And since the word 'above' can indicate superiority in rank and superiority in direction, why did they interpret it as superiority in direction? That which indicates that what is meant by the word 'above' here is superiority in power and capability are several points: first, He said: 'And He is the Supreme [al-Qahir] above His servants,' and 'above' conjoined with force indicates superiority in power and capability, not in the sense of direction. Evidence: the guard may be above the sultan in direction, yet it is not said that he is above the sultan.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that verses mentioning 'above' (fawq) prove God is spatially above. The author first notes that 'fawq' is used in two senses: (1) superiority in rank/power, and (2) spatial height. He cites several Quranic verses where the meaning is clearly rank or power: e.g., 'above every knower is the All-Knowing' (superior knowledge), 'We are above them, dominant' (power), 'God's hand is above their hands' (authority). He also gives an example of the second meaning: 'or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness—a clear non-spatial use. Then he poses a rhetorical question: why interpret it as spatial direction when it could mean superiority? He then provides a decisive proof: the verse 'He is al-Qahir above His servants' links fawq with qahr (compulsion/dominance). Since qahr implies power, the fawq here must be power, not direction. He adds an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan in position, but is not said to be 'above' him in rank; this demonstrates that fawq when combined with qahr indicates power.

Key Terms
فوق
قهر
جهة
رتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'fawq' in the Quran always refers to physical direction or location.
Correction: Razi shows that 'fawq' is used for both rank/power and direction, and in many verses it clearly means power.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Razi give as an example of 'fawq' meaning direction?
A) 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing'
B) 'And We are above them, dominant'
C) 'A mosquito or what is above it'
D) 'The hand of God is above their hands'

Key Takeaway
'Fawq' is used in the Quran for both rank/power and direction, so it cannot automatically be taken as spatial when referring to God.

?

?
Razi questions why opponents insist on the spatial interpretation of 'fawq' for Allah. In your own heart, do you find yourself attached to tangible images of Allah because they feel safer? Are you willing to let go of that and embrace a more transcendent, awe-inspiring concept of Allah—even if it feels less comfortable? Why or why not?

Why 'Fawq' in 'Al-Qahir Fawqa Ibadihi' Means Power, Not Direction
Razi provides a decisive proof from the conjunction of 'fawq' with 'qahr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'He is al-Qahir above His servants' (Qur'an 6:18) must refer to God's power and not to a spatial location.

Core Idea
Fawq paired with qahr proves divine power, not physical height.

The most compelling evidence that 'fawq' in reference to God means power is found in the verse where it is combined with 'qahr' (compelling force). Imam Razi explains this connection and provides a simple analogy to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage where Razi gives the first of several proofs that 'fawq' in this verse means power.
The passage cites the verse and then gives a logical argument followed by an analogy.
2
Identify the key verse and the conjunction
Notice the verse 'Huwa al-Qahir fawqa ibadihi'. Razi points out that 'fawq' is linked with 'qahr', which means force or dominance.
The conjunction is crucial: 'al-fawqiyya al-maghruna bil-qahr'.
3
Understand the logical inference
When 'fawq' is paired with 'qahr', it must indicate superiority in power, not location, because 'qahr' inherently implies power.
4
Reflect on the guard analogy
Razi gives an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan (e.g., on a balcony), but no one says the guard is 'above' the sultan in rank. This shows that mere physical height does not convey superiority.
The analogy proves that 'fawq' with 'qahr' cannot be spatial.

The Mujassim's Defense Against Tashbih
Al-Razi presents the mujassim's defense based on essential difference and Qur'anic parallels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mujassim’s defense against the charge of tashbih and their use of Qur'anic similarities as evidence.

Core Idea
The mujassim argue essential difference prevents tashbih.

This lesson examines the argument of the anthropomorphists (mujassima) who claim that despite affirming God as a body, they are not guilty of tashbih because they believe God is unlike all creation in essence. They cite Qur'anic verses where similar attributes are applied to God and humans without implying full likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the mujassim's claim
Read the passage in Arabic and translation to understand the core argument: essential difference prevents tashbih.
Notice they first assert a distinction in essence.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three examples of attributes shared between God and humans in the Qur'an: hearing/seeing, eyes, hands.
Each example pairs a divine and human usage.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the mujassim think these examples support their position and how this challenges the standard view of tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في أن المجسم هل يوصف بأنه مشبه أم لا ؟ .
قال المجسم : إنا وإن [؟قلنا] : إنه تعالى جسم مختص بالحيز والجهة إلا أنا نعتقد : أنه بخلاف سائر الأجسام في ذاته وحقيقته . وذلك يمنع من القول بالتشبيه. فإن إثبات المساواة فى الصور لا يوجب إثبات التشبيه ، ويدل عليه : أنه تعالى صرح في كتابه بالمساواة في الصفات الكثيرة ، ولم يقل أحد بأن ذلك يوجب التشبيه .
فالأول : قال في صفة نفسه : « إنني معكما : أسمع وأرى » ، وقال في صفة الإنسان : « فجعلناه سميعا بصيرا » :
الثاني : قال تعالى : « واصنع الفلك بأعيننا » ، وقال في الإنسان : « ترى أعينهم تفيض من الدمع » .
الثالث : قال تعالى : « بل يداه مبسوطتان » ، وفي الإنسان : « ذلك بما قدمت يداك » وقال في نفسه : « عما عملت أيدينا [؟أنعاما] » وفي الإنسان : « يد الله فوق أيديهم » .
Chapter Two: On whether the anthropomorphist (mujassim) is described as an assimilationist (mushabbih) or not.
The anthropomorphist said: 'Indeed, even though we say that He, exalted, is a body specific to space and direction, yet we believe that He is different from all other bodies in His essence and reality. And that prevents the assertion of tashbih (assimilation). For the affirmation of equality in forms does not necessitate the affirmation of tashbih. Evidence for it is that He, exalted, stated explicitly in His Book equality in many attributes, and no one said that that necessitates tashbih.'
First: He said about His own attribute: 'Indeed, I am with you: I hear and I see' (20:46), and He said about the human attribute: 'Then We made him hearing and seeing' (76:2).
Second: He, exalted, said: 'And construct the ship under Our eyes' (11:37), and He said about humans: 'You see their eyes overflowing with tears' (5:83).
Third: He, exalted, said: 'But both His hands are outstretched' (5:64), and about humans: 'That is because of what your hands have sent forth' (22:10), and He said about Himself: 'Of what Our hands have created [cattle]' (36:71), and about humans: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the anthropomorphists (mujassima) in defense of their position. They claim that despite affirming that God is a body occupying space and direction, they maintain that His essence is entirely unlike other bodies. This essential difference, they argue, prevents the charge of tashbih (comparing God to creation). They support this by noting that mere similarity in certain descriptions does not amount to full likeness, and they cite the Qur'an as evidence: God attributes to Himself attributes like hearing, seeing, eyes, and hands, and also attributes similar terms to humans, yet no one considers those verses as implying tashbih. The author then lists three pairs of examples: (1) hearing and seeing attributed to God and humans; (2) 'eyes' attributed to God (in the sense of care) and to humans; (3) 'hands' attributed to God and humans. The implication is that the mujassim see their own position as consistent with the Qur'anic method of attribution without falling into outright anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
مجسم (mujassim)
مشبه (mushabbih)
تشبيه (tashbih)
مساواة في الصور
يد
عين
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mujassim see no difference between God and creation; they affirm essential difference.
Correction: The mujassim explicitly state that God is different in essence from all bodies, and that similarity in descriptions does not amount to tashbih.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the mujassim use to argue that they are not guilty of tashbih?
A) They deny all divine attributes.
B) They claim that God is unlike all bodies in essence, despite affirming bodily attributes.
C) They reject the Qur'anic verses about God's attributes.
D) They say that only hands and eyes are attributed to God, not hearing or seeing.

Key Takeaway
The mujassim defend their position by asserting an essential difference and using Qur'anic parallels to argue that shared descriptions do not imply full likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If someone today accused you of tashbih for holding a particular belief about God's attributes, would you be able to defend yourself with the same level of scriptural evidence the mujassim used? Why or why not?

Divine Names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali
Razi lists four divine names with Quranic proofs and notes on human usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural basis for the divine names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali, and how some are shared with human beings in a limited sense.

Core Idea
Divine names are rooted in revelation, and some are shared with creation.

Imam al-Razi continues his enumeration of the beautiful names of Allah by presenting four more names with their Quranic proofs. This lesson covers the tenth to thirteenth names, where we see that while some names apply exclusively to Allah, others are used for humans as well, reminding us of the distinction between the Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the four names carefully. Notice the pattern: each name is stated, followed by a Quranic verse.
Pay attention to the note about human usage for Al-Ali.
2
Understand each name
For Al-Hakam, note the two verses: one for divine judgment, one for human arbitrators. For Ar-Rahim, note the brief comment 'and this is apparent.' For Ash-Shakur, the verse shows appreciation. For Al-Ali, the name is used for humans as a given name.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask why Razi mentions human usage for Al-Ali. It shows that some names are shared, but the divine meaning is absolute and perfect.
This illustrates an important principle in understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: « الحكم » قال الله: « ألا له الحكم » ووصفنا به فقال: « فابعثوا حكما من أهله وحكما من أهلها ». الحادي عشر: الراحم والرحيم. وهذا ظاهر. الثاني عشر: الشكور قال: « إن ربنا لغفور شكور ». الثالث عشر: العلى والإنسان يسمى نفسه بذلك. ومنه: «على» رضي الله عنه. الرابع عشر: الكبير قال عن [؟] وهو العملى [؟] الكبير نفسه: وقال: « إن له أبا شيخا كبير » وقال حكاية عن المرأتين: « وأبونا شيخ كبير ». الخامس عشر: الحكيم والله تعالى وصف نفسه في كتابه به فقال: « تنزيل من حكيم حميد ». السادس عشر: الشهيد قال في حق الخلق: « فكيف إذا جئنا من كل أمة بشهيد » وقال في نفسه: « أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ». السابع عشر: الحق قال: « فتعالى الله الملك الحق » وبالحق أنزلناه وبالحق نزل. الثامن عشر: الوكيل. قال الله تعالى: « وهو على كل شيء وكيل » وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك، فيقال: فلان وكيل فلان. التاسع عشر: المولى. قال تعالى: « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم » ثم قال في حقنا: « ولكل جعلنا مولى » والتي قال: « من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه ». العشرون: الولى. قال الله تعالى: « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا » وقال التي: « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل » وقال تعالى: « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ». الحادي والعشرون: الحى. قال تعالى: « هو الحى لا إله إلا هو » ألم الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم، وقال: « وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي ».
The tenth: 'Al-Hakam (The Judge).' Allah said: 'Indeed, judgement is His.' And He described us by it, saying: 'Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family.' The eleventh: Ar-Rahim and Ar-Rahim. And this is apparent. The twelfth: Ash-Shakur (The Appreciative). He said: 'Indeed, our Lord is Forgiving, Appreciative.' The thirteenth: Al-Ali (The Most High). And a man may call himself by that. Among it: 'Ali' – may Allah be pleased with him. The fourteenth: Al-Kabir (The Great). He said concerning [someone?] and he is the blind [؟], the great himself: And He said: 'Indeed, he has an old father' and He related from the two women: 'Our father is an old man.' The fifteenth: Al-Hakim (The Wise). And Allah the Exalted described Himself in His Book by it, saying: 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy.' The sixteenth: Ash-Shahid (The Witness). He said concerning creation: 'So how will it be when we bring from every nation a witness?' And He said concerning Himself: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' The seventeenth: Al-Haqq (The Truth). He said: 'So exalted is Allah, the Sovereign, the Truth.' And with the truth We sent it down, and with the truth it descended. The eighteenth: Al-Wakil (The Disposer of Affairs). Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is over all things a Disposer.' And creation may be described with that, so it is said: So-and-so is the agent of so-and-so. The nineteenth: Al-Mawla (The Protector). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Protector of those who believe, and the disbelievers have no protector.' Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have appointed heirs' and the Prophet said: 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' The twentieth: Al-Wali (The Ally). Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe.' And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid.' And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies of one another.' The twenty-first: Al-Hayy (The Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' – 'Alif Lam Meem. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Ever-Living, the Sustainer of existence.' And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve divine names (from the tenth to the twenty-first) from the Islamic tradition of al-asmā' al-ḥusnā. For each name, Imam al-Rāzī provides a Qur'anic proof text and often notes that the same name can be applied to human beings in a limited sense.

The structure is consistent: the name is stated, then a relevant Qur'anic verse is quoted. Sometimes additional clarification is given. For example, for 'al-Ḥakam' (The Judge), he cites verse 6:62 and also notes that the term 'ḥakam' is used for human arbitrators in 4:35. For 'ar-Rāḥim' and 'ar-Raḥīm' (The Compassionate and the Merciful), he simply says 'and this is apparent,' meaning the meaning is clear. For 'al-'Alī' (The Most High), he remarks that humans also use that name (e.g., the Companion 'Alī). For 'al-Kabīr' (The Great), the text appears corrupted but cites verses about an old father (12:78, 28:23). For 'al-Ḥakīm' (The Wise), he quotes 41:42. For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الحكم
الراحم
الرحيم
الشكور
العلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All these names are exclusively divine and cannot be used for humans.
Correction: While the divine names are perfect and absolute, some like Al-Hakam and Al-Ali can be applied to humans in a limited, derivative manner, as the passage shows.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'And He described us by it, saying: Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family'?
A) Al-Ali
B) Al-Hakam
C) Ash-Shakur
D) Ar-Rahim

Key Takeaway
The names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali are scripturally established, and some are shared with humans in a limited sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone called 'judge' or 'the compassionate one,' does your heart immediately remember that these attributes belong fully and perfectly to Allah? If not, what would it take to train your heart to see His attributes behind every human reflection?
?
The name 'Ash-Shakur' tells us that Allah appreciates even the smallest good deed. Are you more focused on the weight of your sins than on seeking His appreciation through sincere acts? Will you today perform one small deed solely hoping for His shukr?
?
You may share the name 'Ali' or another divine name, but do you live with the awareness that only Allah is Al-Ali in absolute sense? How does that awareness change the way you view your own status and worth?

Divine Names Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid
Razi lists three divine names with Quranic proofs, including human usage examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proofs for the divine names Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid, and how they are used in the Quran for both Allah and creation.

Core Idea
Even names that appear in human contexts point to Allah's unique perfection.

Imam al-Razi continues with the fourteenth to sixteenth divine names: Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid. Each is accompanied by Quranic verses that sometimes also refer to human beings, highlighting the distinction between the Creator's absolute attributes and their limited manifestations in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the three names. Note the structure: name, then verses, and sometimes a note.
The text for Al-Kabir has some unclear words, but the verses are clear.
2
Examine each name
For Al-Kabir, the verses refer to an old father (used for humans). For Al-Hakim, a direct divine attribution. For Ash-Shahid, two verses: one about human witnesses, one about Allah as witness.
Notice the pattern of dual usage.
3
Consider the theological lesson
Why does Razi include verses that describe humans? It shows that the root meanings are shared, but Allah's attributes are absolute and unparalleled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: « الحكم » قال الله: « ألا له الحكم » ووصفنا به فقال: « فابعثوا حكما من أهله وحكما من أهلها ». الحادي عشر: الراحم والرحيم. وهذا ظاهر. الثاني عشر: الشكور قال: « إن ربنا لغفور شكور ». الثالث عشر: العلى والإنسان يسمى نفسه بذلك. ومنه: «على» رضي الله عنه. الرابع عشر: الكبير قال عن [؟] وهو العملى [؟] الكبير نفسه: وقال: « إن له أبا شيخا كبير » وقال حكاية عن المرأتين: « وأبونا شيخ كبير ». الخامس عشر: الحكيم والله تعالى وصف نفسه في كتابه به فقال: « تنزيل من حكيم حميد ». السادس عشر: الشهيد قال في حق الخلق: « فكيف إذا جئنا من كل أمة بشهيد » وقال في نفسه: « أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ». السابع عشر: الحق قال: « فتعالى الله الملك الحق » وبالحق أنزلناه وبالحق نزل. الثامن عشر: الوكيل. قال الله تعالى: « وهو على كل شيء وكيل » وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك، فيقال: فلان وكيل فلان. التاسع عشر: المولى. قال تعالى: « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم » ثم قال في حقنا: « ولكل جعلنا مولى » والتي قال: « من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه ». العشرون: الولى. قال الله تعالى: « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا » وقال التي: « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل » وقال تعالى: « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ». الحادي والعشرون: الحى. قال تعالى: « هو الحى لا إله إلا هو » ألم الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم، وقال: « وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي ».
The tenth: 'Al-Hakam (The Judge).' Allah said: 'Indeed, judgement is His.' And He described us by it, saying: 'Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family.' The eleventh: Ar-Rahim and Ar-Rahim. And this is apparent. The twelfth: Ash-Shakur (The Appreciative). He said: 'Indeed, our Lord is Forgiving, Appreciative.' The thirteenth: Al-Ali (The Most High). And a man may call himself by that. Among it: 'Ali' – may Allah be pleased with him. The fourteenth: Al-Kabir (The Great). He said concerning [someone?] and he is the blind [؟], the great himself: And He said: 'Indeed, he has an old father' and He related from the two women: 'Our father is an old man.' The fifteenth: Al-Hakim (The Wise). And Allah the Exalted described Himself in His Book by it, saying: 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy.' The sixteenth: Ash-Shahid (The Witness). He said concerning creation: 'So how will it be when we bring from every nation a witness?' And He said concerning Himself: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' The seventeenth: Al-Haqq (The Truth). He said: 'So exalted is Allah, the Sovereign, the Truth.' And with the truth We sent it down, and with the truth it descended. The eighteenth: Al-Wakil (The Disposer of Affairs). Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is over all things a Disposer.' And creation may be described with that, so it is said: So-and-so is the agent of so-and-so. The nineteenth: Al-Mawla (The Protector). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Protector of those who believe, and the disbelievers have no protector.' Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have appointed heirs' and the Prophet said: 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' The twentieth: Al-Wali (The Ally). Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe.' And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid.' And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies of one another.' The twenty-first: Al-Hayy (The Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' – 'Alif Lam Meem. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Ever-Living, the Sustainer of existence.' And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve divine names (from the tenth to the twenty-first) from the Islamic tradition of al-asmā' al-ḥusnā. For each name, Imam al-Rāzī provides a Qur'anic proof text and often notes that the same name can be applied to human beings in a limited sense.

The structure is consistent: the name is stated, then a relevant Qur'anic verse is quoted. Sometimes additional clarification is given. For example, for 'al-Ḥakam' (The Judge), he cites verse 6:62 and also notes that the term 'ḥakam' is used for human arbitrators in 4:35. For 'ar-Rāḥim' and 'ar-Raḥīm' (The Compassionate and the Merciful), he simply says 'and this is apparent,' meaning the meaning is clear. For 'al-'Alī' (The Most High), he remarks that humans also use that name (e.g., the Companion 'Alī). For 'al-Kabīr' (The Great), the text appears corrupted but cites verses about an old father (12:78, 28:23). For 'al-Ḥakīm' (The Wise), he quotes 41:42. For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الكبير
الحكيم
الشهيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These names are only used for Allah in the Quran.
Correction: The Quran uses 'kabir' and 'shahid' for humans as well, but in a limited sense. Allah's attributes are perfect and absolute.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy'?
A) Al-Kabir
B) Al-Hakim
C) Ash-Shahid
D) Al-Hakam

Key Takeaway
Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid are divine names with Quranic proofs, and their roots appear in human contexts, highlighting divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is the Greatest), do you feel the weight of His absolute greatness in your daily decisions, or has it become a hollow phrase? Will you today pause before each prayer and truly magnify Him in your heart?
?
Allah is Ash-Shahid, witnessing your every thought and action. Is there a secret sin you are comfortable with because you think no one sees? How would your behavior change if you lived every moment aware that the Witness is always present? Commit now to a specific step toward accountability.
?
The name Al-Hakim reminds you that Allah's wisdom is perfect, even when your life feels chaotic. Is there a situation where you are resisting His decree because it seems unfair? Will you today submit that matter to His wisdom and trust that He knows what you do not?

Divine Names Al-Wali and Al-Hayy
Razi lists two divine names with Quranic proofs and human usage examples.

Learning Objective

Allah is the ultimate Ally and the Living, while humans share these names in a limited sense.

In this final lesson from the sequence, Imam al-Razi presents the twentieth and twenty-first divine names: Al-Wali and Al-Hayy. Each is supported by multiple Quranic verses, and for Al-Wali, he also includes a hadith and a verse about human guardians and allies, demonstrating the shared linguistic root.

Guided Path

Read the passage

He said concerning creation: 'So how will it be when we bring from every nation a witness?' And He said concerning Himself: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' The seventeenth: Al-Haqq (The Truth). He said: 'So exalted is Allah, the Sovereign, the Truth.' And with the truth We sent it down, and with the truth it descended. The eighteenth: Al-Wakil (The Disposer of Affairs). Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is over all things a Disposer.' And creation may be described with that, so it is said: So-and-so is the agent of so-and-so. The nineteenth: Al-Mawla (The Protector). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Protector of those who believe, and the disbelievers have no protector.' Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have appointed heirs' and the Prophet said: 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' The twentieth: Al-Wali (The Ally). Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe.' And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid.' And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies of one another.' The twenty-first: Al-Hayy (The Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' – 'Alif Lam Meem. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Ever-Living, the Sustainer of existence.' And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve divine names (from the tenth to the twenty-first) from the Islamic tradition of al-asmā' al-ḥusnā. For each name, Imam al-Rāzī provides a Qur'anic proof text and often notes that the same name can be applied to human beings in a limited sense.

The structure is consistent: the name is stated, then a relevant Qur'anic verse is quoted. Sometimes additional clarification is given. For example, for 'al-Ḥakam' (The Judge), he cites verse 6:62 and also notes that the term 'ḥakam' is used for human arbitrators in 4:35. For 'ar-Rāḥim' and 'ar-Raḥīm' (The Compassionate and the Merciful), he simply says 'and this is apparent,' meaning the meaning is clear. For 'al-'Alī' (The Most High), he remarks that humans also use that name (e.g., the Companion 'Alī). For 'al-Kabīr' (The Great), the text appears corrupted but cites verses about an old father (12:78, 28:23). For 'al-Ḥakīm' (The Wise), he quotes 41:42. For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الولي
الحى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'Wali' is only used for Allah in the Quran.
Correction: The Quran uses 'Wali' for humans as well, such as guardians and allies, but Allah is the ultimate Wali.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe'?
A) Al-Hayy
B) Al-Wali
C) Al-Mawla
D) Al-Haqq

Key Takeaway
Al-Wali and Al-Hayy are divine names with scriptural proofs, and their human usage highlights the depth of their meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You acknowledge that Allah is your Wali, the ultimate Ally. But when you face trials, do you actually turn to Him as your first and only protector, or do you rely on people? What does your reliance reveal about your true belief in this name? Commit today to making dua to Him as Al-Wali in your next difficulty.
?
Allah is Al-Hayy, the Living, the source of all life. You are alive by His will. Are you living your life in a way that honors the Giver of life, or are you wasting it on distraction? List one habit you will change today to live more consciously for Him.
?
The lesson shows that the same words (Wali, Hayy) are used for God and for creation, but in radically different ways. Have you ever fallen into the trap of imagining God's attributes are like human ones? What specific thought pattern do you need to correct to maintain proper tawhid? Write down one way you will remind yourself of the difference starting today.

Al-Wakil: The Trustee
Razi presents al-Wakil with Quranic proof and creaturely usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Wakil is a divine name proven by the Quran, and that the term also applies to creatures in a limited, metaphorical sense without implying similarity to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is the absolute Trustee over all things; human trusteeship is derived and limited.

Razi begins the eighteenth divine name, al-Wakil, by citing a Quranic verse that establishes Allah as the Trustee over all things. He then notes that the same term is applied to creatures, but this does not imply any similarity in the essence of the attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the name, then the Quranic proof, then the creaturely application.
The verse is from Surah al-An'am (6:102).
2
Identify the Two Uses
Observe that the term 'wakil' is used for Allah in an absolute sense and for creatures in a limited sense. Mark the difference in context.
3
Reflect on the Distinction
Ask yourself: Why is it important to recognize that the same Arabic word can apply to Allah and creation without implying similarity?
This guard against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عشر : الوكيل. قال الله تعالى : وهو على كل شيء وكيل (٣٦) وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك ، فيقال : فلان وكيل فلان .
التاسع عشر : المولى . قال تعالى : « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا ، وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم (۳۷)، ثم قال في حقنا : « ولكل جعلنا مولى (۳۸) والتي قال : « من كنت مولاه ، فعلي مولاه (۳۹) ،
العشرون : الولى . قال الله تعالى : « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله ، والذين آمنوا (٤٠) ، وقال التي : « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل (٤١) ، وقال تعالى : « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض (٤٢) ،
الحادي والعشرون: الحى . قال تعالى: «هو الحى لا إله إلا هو (٤٣) - ألم . . الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم (٤٤) وقال : وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي (٤٥) ،
(٣٦) الأنعام ١٠٢ (۳۷) محمد ۱۱ (٣٨) النساء ۳۳ (۳۹) أحاديث الفرق هي أحاديث آحاد (٤٠) المائدة ٥٥ (٤١) أنظر مناقب الشافعى لفخر الدين الرازی - طبع الكايات الأزهرية (٤٢) التوبة ٧١ (٤٣) غافر ٦٥ (٤٤) أول آل عمران (٤٥) الأنبياء ٣٠
Eighteenth: al-Wakil (the Trustee). Allah Most High said: 'And He is over all things a Trustee (Wakil)' (36). And creatures are described with it, so it is said: 'So-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so.'
Nineteenth: al-Mawla (the Patron). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Patron (Mawla) of those who believe, and that the disbelievers have no patron' (37). Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have made heirs (mawla)' (38). And the Prophet said: 'For whomever I am his patron (mawla), 'Ali is his patron' (39).
Twentieth: al-Wali (the Ally). Allah Most High said: 'Your ally (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe' (40). And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian (wali), her marriage is invalid' (41). And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies (awliya') of one another' (42).
Twenty-first: al-Hayy (the Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' (43) - 'Alif Lam Mim. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Living, the Self-Subsisting' (44). And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing' (45).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four divine names (al-asmā’ al-ḥusnā) in sequence, each with Quranic proof and often a note on how the same term applies to creation. Razi follows the same pattern for each entry: first he states the name, then he quotes one or more Quranic verses that establish it for Allah, and finally he shows that the term is also used for creatures, but in a limited or metaphorical sense.

For al-Wakīl (the Trustee), the verse from al-An‘ām 6:102 proves that Allah is over all things a Wakīl. Then Razi notes that creatures are described with this word, as in 'so-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so', but the meaning for Allah is unique and absolute.

For al-Mawlā (the Patron), he cites Muḥammad 47:11 stating that Allah is the Mawlā of believers, while disbelievers have no mawlā. He then mentions the verse from al-Nisā’ 4:33 which uses mawlā for human heirs, and the famous hadith 'man kuntu mawlāhu fa-‘Aliyyun mawlāhu', though he notes the hadith is āḥād (singular transmission). This shows the term applies to humans as well, but divine mawlā is superior.

For al-Walī (the Ally), Razi uses al-Mā’idah 5:55 to show Allah and the Messenger are the believers' walī. Then he cites a hadith about the necessity of a guardian for marriage, and al-Tawbah 9:71 where believers are awliyā’ of each other. Thus the term is shared, but the meaning differs: Allah's walī is unique and absolute.

For al-Ḥayy (the Living), he gives Āl ‘Imrān 3:2 and Ghāfir 40:65 asserting that Allah is the Living, and then al-Anbiyā’ 21:30 saying 'We made from water every living thing' for creatures, showing that created life is derived and limited.

This passage demonstrates Razi's method: he defends the divine names by scripture while acknowledging that the same Arabic words are used for contingent beings, but without any similarity in essence.

Key Terms
الوكيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a name applies to both Allah and creation, then the attribute is shared in the same sense.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the term 'wakil' is used for both, but the context shows that Allah's trusteeship is absolute and over all things, whereas human trusteeship is limited and metaphorical.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Razi cite as proof for the divine name al-Wakil?
A) 'He is the Living' (Ghafir 65)
B) 'And He is over all things a Trustee' (al-An'am 102)
C) 'Allah is the Patron of those who believe' (Muhammad 11)
D) 'Your ally is only Allah' (al-Ma'idah 55)

Key Takeaway
Allah is uniquely and absolutely al-Wakil, the Trustee over all things, while creatures use the term in a finite sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly entrust all your affairs—your health, wealth, relationships, and future—to Allah as the absolute Wakil, or do you rely on human agents as if they have independent control? How can you practice that trust in a specific decision you're facing today?
?
When you delegate a task to someone else, do you remember that their trusteeship is derived from Allah and limited, and that Allah is the ultimate Trustee over that matter? How does that change the way you assign responsibility and manage worry?
?
The verse says Allah is 'over all things a Trustee.' Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are the wakil instead of Allah? What would it look like to surrender that area to Him starting now?

Al-Mawla: The Patron
Razi lists al-Mawla with Quranic proofs and a hadith, plus creaturely application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Mawla is a divine name meaning the Patron and Protector, with Quranic and hadith evidence, and that the term also applies to human relationships.

Core Idea
Allah is the true Patron of believers; human patronage is derivative and limited.

Razi moves to the nineteenth name, al-Mawla, citing a verse that distinguishes believers who have Allah as their Mawla from disbelievers who have none. He then shows the term's human usage through a Quranic verse and a prophetic hadith, underscoring the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Mawla. Note the three citations: one Quranic verse for Allah, one for humans, and a hadith.
The hadith is classified as ahad (singular transmission) by Razi.
2
Analyze the Evidence
Compare the divine usage (believers' Mawla) with the human usage (heirs in inheritance) and the Prophet's statement about 'Ali.
3
Understand the Hierarchy
Reflect on how the same word carries different levels of meaning: absolute for Allah, contingent for humans.
This illustrates the principle of tawhid in names.

Al-Wali: The Ally and Guardian
Razi presents al-Wali with Quranic verses and a hadith on guardianship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Wali is a divine name meaning the Ally and Guardian, supported by Quran and hadith, and that the term is also used for human guardians and allies.

Core Idea
Allah is the ultimate Wali; human guardianship is a shadow of His absolute wali-ship.

Razi presents the twentieth name, al-Wali, with a verse naming Allah and the Messenger as the believers' wali, then a hadith about marriage guardianship, and a verse about mutual alliance among believers. This shows the term's range from divine to human.

Read the Passage

3

This reinforces the concept of tanzih (transcendence).

A) 'For whomever I am his mawla, 'Ali is his mawla'
B) 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her wali, her marriage is invalid'
C) 'The believing men and believing women are awliya' of one another'
D) 'And He is over all things a Trustee'

Key Takeaway
Allah is the supreme Wali; human guardianship and alliance are derived and limited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you treated Allah's guardianship as one option among many, rather than the sole source of protection? Will you commit to deriving your sense of safety solely from Him starting today?
?
The hadith on marriage guardianship underscores that even human wali-ship is a delegation from Allah. Whom do you currently rely on as your 'wali' in decision-making—parents, friends, or yourself? Is that authority ultimately rooted in Allah's commands?
?
If Allah is your Wali, why do you still fear the opinions of people more than His displeasure? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed He is in control?

Divine Attributes

Al-Hayy: The Living
Razi presents al-Hayy with Quranic proofs and the creaturely life from water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Hayy is a divine name meaning the Living, proven by multiple Quranic verses, and that creaturely life is derived from water and limited.

Core Idea
Allah is the Living who never dies; all created life is contingent and finite.

Razi concludes the sequence with the twenty-first name, al-Hayy, quoting verses that affirm Allah as the Living and Self-Subsisting. He also notes that creatures are described as living, as in the verse 'We made from water every living thing,' but this life is created and temporary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Hayy. Note the three verses cited.
Verses are from Ghafir (40:65), Al 'Imran (3:2), and al-Anbiya' (21:30).
2
Compare Divine and Creaturely Life
Observe that Allah's life is affirmed without qualification, while creaturely life is described as something made from water.
3
Reflect on the Implication
Why is it important to distinguish between Allah's life and created life? This relates to the concept of qidam (eternity) and hadath (temporality).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عشر : الوكيل. قال الله تعالى : وهو على كل شيء وكيل (٣٦) وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك ، فيقال : فلان وكيل فلان .
التاسع عشر : المولى . قال تعالى : « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا ، وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم (۳۷)، ثم قال في حقنا : « ولكل جعلنا مولى (۳۸) والتي قال : « من كنت مولاه ، فعلي مولاه (۳۹) ،
العشرون : الولى . قال الله تعالى : « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله ، والذين آمنوا (٤٠) ، وقال التي : « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل (٤١) ، وقال تعالى : « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض (٤٢) ،
الحادي والعشرون: الحى . قال تعالى: «هو الحى لا إله إلا هو (٤٣) - ألم . . الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم (٤٤) وقال : وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي (٤٥) ،
(٣٦) الأنعام ١٠٢ (۳۷) محمد ۱۱ (٣٨) النساء ۳۳ (۳۹) أحاديث الفرق هي أحاديث آحاد (٤٠) المائدة ٥٥ (٤١) أنظر مناقب الشافعى لفخر الدين الرازی - طبع الكايات الأزهرية (٤٢) التوبة ٧١ (٤٣) غافر ٦٥ (٤٤) أول آل عمران (٤٥) الأنبياء ٣٠
Eighteenth: al-Wakil (the Trustee). Allah Most High said: 'And He is over all things a Trustee (Wakil)' (36). And creatures are described with it, so it is said: 'So-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so.'
Nineteenth: al-Mawla (the Patron). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Patron (Mawla) of those who believe, and that the disbelievers have no patron' (37). Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have made heirs (mawla)' (38). And the Prophet said: 'For whomever I am his patron (mawla), 'Ali is his patron' (39).
Twentieth: al-Wali (the Ally). Allah Most High said: 'Your ally (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe' (40). And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian (wali), her marriage is invalid' (41). And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies (awliya') of one another' (42).
Twenty-first: al-Hayy (the Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' (43) - 'Alif Lam Mim. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Living, the Self-Subsisting' (44). And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing' (45).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four divine names (al-asmā’ al-ḥusnā) in sequence, each with Quranic proof and often a note on how the same term applies to creation. Razi follows the same pattern for each entry: first he states the name, then he quotes one or more Quranic verses that establish it for Allah, and finally he shows that the term is also used for creatures, but in a limited or metaphorical sense.

For al-Wakīl (the Trustee), the verse from al-An‘ām 6:102 proves that Allah is over all things a Wakīl. Then Razi notes that creatures are described with this word, as in 'so-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so', but the meaning for Allah is unique and absolute.

For al-Mawlā (the Patron), he cites Muḥammad 47:11 stating that Allah is the Mawlā of believers, while disbelievers have no mawlā. He then mentions the verse from al-Nisā’ 4:33 which uses mawlā for human heirs, and the famous hadith 'man kuntu mawlāhu fa-‘Aliyyun mawlāhu', though he notes the hadith is āḥād (singular transmission). This shows the term applies to humans as well, but divine mawlā is superior.

For al-Walī (the Ally), Razi uses al-Mā’idah 5:55 to show Allah and the Messenger are the believers' walī. Then he cites a hadith about the necessity of a guardian for marriage, and al-Tawbah 9:71 where believers are awliyā’ of each other. Thus the term is shared, but the meaning differs: Allah's walī is unique and absolute.

For al-Ḥayy (the Living), he gives Āl ‘Imrān 3:2 and Ghāfir 40:65 asserting that Allah is the Living, and then al-Anbiyā’ 21:30 saying 'We made from water every living thing' for creatures, showing that created life is derived and limited.

This passage demonstrates Razi's method: he defends the divine names by scripture while acknowledging that the same Arabic words are used for contingent beings, but without any similarity in essence.

Key Terms
الحى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since both Allah and creatures are called 'living', life is the same attribute.
Correction: Allah's life is necessary and eternal; creaturely life is contingent and originated from water.

Checkpoint
Which verse does Razi use to show that creaturely life is made from water?
A) 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him'
B) 'Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Living, the Self-Subsisting'
C) 'And We made from water every living thing'
D) 'And He is over all things a Trustee'

Key Takeaway
Allah is the Living in an absolute sense; all created life is derived and temporary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all created life is merely water that will perish, why do you cling so tightly to your possessions, status, and plans as if they will last forever? What would it take for you to truly live as if only Allah's life is real and enduring?
?
When you fear death—your own or a loved one's—are you actually doubting that Allah alone is the Living who never dies? How would your heart change if you internalized that your life is a borrowed breath from al-Hayy, not your own?
?
Your body is mostly water. That water will one day be gone. Does that truth humble you enough to reorient your ambitions toward the truly Living? What is one attachment you can release today because you know it is only water?

The Divine Name Al-Wahid (the One)
Razi presents al-Wahid with Quranic proof and linguistic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Wahid and how it applies to Allah uniquely, even though the word 'one' is used for created things.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness is absolute and unlike any other 'one'.

Razi begins the twenty-second name, al-Wahid, by citing a Quranic verse that affirms Allah's exclusive oneness. He then notes that the same word 'one' is used for everyday objects like a garment or a person. This sets up the key theological principle: divine attributes are unique to Allah, even when the same Arabic term is applied to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
Read the Arabic line for al-Wahid. It states the name, then gives the verse from Qur'an 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' This is the direct proof.
Notice the concise style: 'al-Wāḥid' followed by 'qāla ta‘ālā'.
2
Observe the application to creation
Razi adds: 'And this description applies to most things, so it is said: one garment, one human.' Think about how the same word is used in everyday language.
This shows linguistic commonality but theological distinction.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why Razi includes this. He is emphasizing that the attribute 'one' when applied to Allah is not like the 'oneness' of created things. Allah's oneness is absolute and exclusive.
This is a foundational point in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
الواحد
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that since 'one' is used for both Allah and creatures, Allah's oneness is similar to the oneness of a created object.
Correction: Razi shows the word 'one' applies to many things, but Allah's oneness is unique and absolute, not comparable to created oneness.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is cited as proof for the divine name al-Wahid?
A) Qur'an 6:19
B) Qur'an 4:16
C) Qur'an 47:38
D) Qur'an 24:35

Key Takeaway
The name al-Wahid affirms Allah's absolute oneness, distinct from any created 'one'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Ahad' in your prayers, do you truly feel in your heart that there is no other 'one' like Him? Or have you let worldly 'ones'—your career, your family, your desires—compete in your heart for the place that belongs only to the Unique One? Repent today and reaffirm His exclusive oneness in your actions.
?
Think of a time you felt Allah's oneness wasn't enough for you, when you turned to a created thing as your ultimate hope or refuge. What was that created 'one'? How does Razi's warning about the difference between created oneness and divine oneness pierce that idol in your heart? Now, what step will you take to uproot that reliance?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is al-Wahid—uniquely one in a way no creature can be—how would your daily anxieties change? For example, when you fear someone's opinion or trust in your own abilities, are you not putting another 'one' beside Him? This lesson calls you to a radical tawhid in your inner life. Will you respond today?

The Divine Name Al-Tawwab (the Acceptor of Repentance)
Razi presents al-Tawwab with Quranic proof and the name's application to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-Tawwab and how Allah's acceptance of repentance is perfect, while created beings are called 'tawwabin' in a derivative sense.

Core Idea
Allah's acceptance of repentance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

The twenty-third name, al-Tawwab, is presented with a Quranic verse that describes Allah as 'ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful.' Razi then notes that humans are also called 'tawwabin' (those who repent), but the divine attribute is unique and free from need.

Guided Path
1
Read the entry for al-Tawwab
The passage states the name, then the verse from Qur'an 4:16: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful.' Read it carefully.
2
Notice the application to creation
Razi says: 'And creation is named by it, so He said: Indeed Allah loves those who repent.' Here, humans are called 'tawwabin'.
The word is used for both, but the meaning differs.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how Allah's acceptance is perfect and self-sufficient, while human repentance is a created act that needs Allah's grace.
This reinforces tanzih (transcendence).

?

?

?
Your repentance is a reflection of His name al-Tawwab. How does knowing that He is perpetually the Acceptor shape your attitude toward your own repeated failures?
