Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Divine Name Al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient)
Razi presents al-Ghani with Quranic proof and linguistic examples from creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Ghani, absolutely self-sufficient, while created wealth is a dependent attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent of anything.

The twenty-fourth name, al-Ghani, is introduced with a direct Quranic statement: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient.' Razi then cites additional verses and a tradition that use the same word 'ghani' (rich) for people, showing that the human concept of wealth is a mere analogy for Allah's perfect independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says 'al-Ghani' followed by the verse from Surah Muhammad (47:38): 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient.'
2
Notice the additional evidence
Razi then quotes Qur'an 9:93 about 'those who are rich (aghniyā')' and an athar about taking from the rich. This shows the word is used for creatures.
The athar is a prophetic tradition or saying.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider how human wealth is temporary and dependent, while Allah's self-sufficiency is eternal and independent of all.
This highlights tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
الغنى
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Allah's self-sufficiency is like human wealth, which implies having possessions.
Correction: Razi shows the word is used for creatures, but Allah's al-Ghani is absolute independence, not having possessions.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse directly states that Allah is al-Ghani?
A) Qur'an 47:38
B) Qur'an 9:93
C) Qur'an 6:19
D) Qur'an 24:35

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghani means Allah is absolutely self-sufficient, unlike any created notion of wealth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your bank account, your job, or your health, do you see them as signs of Allah's Ghani or do you feel self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that only Allah is al-Ghani and you are in absolute need of Him at every second?
?
The athar says to take from the rich and give to the poor. If you are among the rich (even if only by a little), will you give something today that you consider 'yours' and not 'His'? What does your reluctance or willingness say about your belief in al-Ghani?
?
You know that Allah needs nothing from you—not your worship, not your money. Yet He commands you to worship and give. Does that make you love Him more, or does it expose a hidden expectation that you deserve something in return? Answer honestly before Him.

Divine Attributes

The Divine Name Al-Nur (the Light)
Razi presents al-Nur with two Quranic verses showing divine and created light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine name al-Nur as a metaphor for Allah's guidance and existence, distinct from physical light.

Core Idea
Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and self-manifestation.

The twenty-fifth name, al-Nur, is presented with the famous 'Light Verse' (Qur'an 24:35) and another verse about the believers' light. Razi uses this to show that while 'light' is used for both Allah and believers, the divine light is unique and incomparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage states al-Nur and then the verse: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth.' This is the primary proof.
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds: 'Their light will run before them' from Qur'an 66:8, referring to the believers' light on the Day of Judgment.
This shows the word is applied to creatures.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how Allah's light is not a physical light but a metaphor for guidance, existence, and knowledge. Created light is a physical phenomenon.
This is crucial for understanding tanzih.

Source Text

Which verse speaks about the believers' light running before them?
A) Qur'an 24:35
B) Qur'an 66:8
C) Qur'an 47:38
D) Qur'an 6:19

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, where do you turn for guidance—are you actively seeking light from Allah (through Qur'an and sunnah) or are you chasing other sources that seem bright? What will you stop doing today to orient your heart towards Allah as the only true Light?
?
The believers' light on the Day of Judgment is described as 'running before them.' Does your current life reflect that you are walking in a path that will generate that light? If you were to examine your deeds today, would they be producing a light that runs ahead of you or a darkness that trails behind?
?
You have just read that Allah's light is uncreated and incomparable, yet you may still imagine Him in physical terms. Are you willing to let go of any mental image you have of Allah and accept His transcendence? What thought pattern about Allah do you need to repent from today?

The Divine Name Al-Hadi (the Guide)
Razi presents al-Hadi with two Quranic verses showing divine and prophetic guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Hadi, the ultimate Guide, while human guides merely convey guidance.

Core Idea
Allah's guidance is absolute and effective; human guidance is only a means.

The twenty-sixth name, al-Hadi, is supported by two verses: one affirming that Allah guides whom He wills (Qur'an 28:56), and another stating that every nation has a guide (Qur'an 13:7). Razi thus distinguishes between divine guidance, which is creative and decisive, and human guidance, which is merely instructive.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Hadi and then the verse from Qur'an 28:56: 'But Allah guides whom He wills.'
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds 'And He said: You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (13:7). Here, a prophet is called a guide.
This shows the word is applied to creation.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider how Allah's guidance is effective and causes the guided person to be guided, while human guidance is just calling and teaching.
This emphasizes tawhid in guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the role of the human guide according to Qur'an 13:7?
A) They create guidance
B) They are warners and guides
C) They control people's hearts
D) They are the same as Allah's guidance

Al-Hadi means Allah is the only true Guide, while human guides are merely means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek guidance from scholars or teachers, do you recognize that their guidance is merely a means, and that true guidance comes only from Allah? How does that change your attitude when you listen to them?
?
The verse says 'Allah guides whom He wills.' Have you ever considered that you are only guided because Allah willed it? Does that make you humble or proud?
?
If Allah is the only Guide, then in what areas of your life are you still refusing to be guided by His revelation? What is one step you can take today to seek His guidance specifically?

Divine Attributes

The Divine Name Al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing)
Razi presents al-Mustami‘ with two Quranic verses on divine and human listening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Mustami‘, the All-Hearing, whose hearing is without ears and encompasses all, while human hearing is physical and limited.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing is perfect, without any bodily organ.

The twenty-seventh name, al-Mustami‘, is proven by a verse where Allah speaks to Moses and Aaron: 'Indeed We are with you listening.' Razi then quotes a second verse where Moses is commanded to listen. This shows that 'listening' is used for both Allah and humans, but the divine attribute is transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Mustami‘ and then the verse from Qur'an 26:15: 'Indeed We are with you listening.' This is the direct proof.
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds 'And He said to Moses: So listen to what is revealed' from Qur'an 20:13. Here, the command is for Moses to listen.
This shows the word is applied to a prophet.
3
Reflect on the difference
Think about how Allah's hearing is not with ears but is an attribute that encompasses all sounds without any instrument. Human hearing is physical and limited.
This is a key kalam point about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
المستمع
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Allah hears with ears like humans.
Correction: Razi shows the same word is used for humans, implying that divine hearing is incomparable and not physical.

Checkpoint
Which verse commands Moses to listen?
A) Qur'an 26:15
B) Qur'an 20:13
C) Qur'an 28:56
D) Qur'an 6:19

Key Takeaway
Al-Mustami‘ means Allah hears all, without any physical organ or limitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah hears your every word, even the ones you whisper to yourself. When was the last time that awareness stopped you from uttering a sin? What will you do today to let this truth guard your tongue?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is al-Mustami‘—the All-Hearing who listens to your dua with perfect attention—how would it change the way you pray? Will you pour out your heart to Him right now, knowing He hears you better than anyone ever could?
?
Allah's hearing is not like yours; it is without limitation. Does your awareness of His constant presence move you to seek His forgiveness for the words you have spoken carelessly? Commit to one specific change in your speech today as a direct response to this lesson.

The Divine Name Al-Qadim (the Ancient)
Razi presents al-Qadim with a Quranic verse showing a created thing described as 'old'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Qadim means Allah is eternal, without beginning, while created things are only relatively old.

Core Idea
Allah's 'ancientness' is eternal and uncreated, unlike temporal old age.

The twenty-eighth and final name in this passage, al-Qadim, is supported by a verse describing an old palm stalk. Razi thus shows that the word 'qadim' can refer to something created that has aged, but when applied to Allah, it signifies pre-eternity and self-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Qadim and then the verse from Qur'an 36:39: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk.' The word 'old' here is qadim.
2
Understand the linguistic usage
Razi cites this verse to show that 'qadim' is used for a created object that has become old over time.
3
Reflect on the divine meaning
Consider that for Allah, 'Qadim' does not mean aged, but eternal without beginning. This is a key kalam term.
This name is particularly important in theological discussions about the pre-eternity of Allah.

Divine Names 22–28: al-Wahid to al-Qadim
Lists divine names 22-28 with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven divine names from al-Wahid to al-Qadim and recall their primary Quranic evidence as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
Seven divine names, each anchored in a Qur'anic verse, reveal distinct aspects of Allah's nature.

This passage continues the enumeration of the ninety-nine names, presenting names twenty-two through twenty-eight. Each name is introduced with a simple definition and supported by one or two verses from the Qur'an. As you read, notice how the author briefly notes linguistic usage for some names, such as 'al-Wahid' also applying to created objects, while primarily focusing on establishing the scriptural basis for each divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of seven names
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Familiarize yourself with each name and its meaning.
Notice that each name is numbered sequentially.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence for each name
For each name, find the provided Quranic verse(s) and note the chapter and verse reference from the footnotes. This shows the author's method of grounding each name in revelation.
Some names have two verses.
3
Observe the comment about 'al-Wahid'
The author notes that 'one' is also used for created things like a garment or person. This hints at the principle that divine names may share words with creation but refer uniquely to Allah.
The author does not elaborate on the distinction here.
4
Reflect on the diversity of attributes
From Oneness (al-Wahid) to Acceptor of Repentance (al-Tawwab) to Ancient (al-Qadim), these names cover different aspects of Allah's essence and acts. Consider how each name relates to Allah's majesty.

The twenty-second: The One. He said, the Most High: 'Say: He is only One God' (46) and this description applies to most things. So it is said: one garment, one human.
The twenty-third: The Acceptor of Repentance. He said, the Most High: 'Indeed Allah is Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47) creation to him [?], then He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48), and is called.
The twenty-fourth: The Self-Sufficient. He said, the Most High: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) and He said: 'The blame is only on those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50), and He said in the tradition: 'Take it from their rich and return it to their poor.'
The twenty-fifth: The Light. Allah, the Most High, said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51), and He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).

Commentary
This passage enumerates seven divine names from the list of ninety-nine names, continuing a sequence from earlier in the book. Each name is presented with its Arabic term, followed by a Quranic verse as evidence. For the name 'al-Wahid' (The One), the author notes that the term 'one' is also used for created things like a garment or a person, but the divine use is unique. For 'al-Tawwab' (The Acceptor of Repentance), the verse from An-Nisa' and Al-Baqarah are cited. The phrase 'الخلق به [؟]' is unclear in the OCR, possibly referring to God's acceptance of repentance from His creation. The name 'al-Ghani' (The Self-Sufficient) is supported by verses from Muhammad and At-Tawbah, plus a prophetic tradition about charity. 'An-Nur' (The Light) is supported by the famous Light Verse and a verse from At-Tahrim. 'Al-Hadi' (The Guide) uses verses from Al-Qasas and Ar-Ra'd. 'Al-Mustami'' (The Listener) is proven by verses from Ash-Shu'ara' and Ta-Ha. Finally, 'al-Qadim' (The Ancient) is supported by a verse from Ya-Sin describing the old palm stalk. The footnotes at the end provide the Quranic chapter and verse references for each citation, confirming the source of each proof.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that Allah is al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient). When you wake up tomorrow and feel the weight of your needs, will you remember that He is completely independent and that your dependence on Him is the only path? Does this name humble your pride in your own resources?
?
Allah is al-Hadi (the Guide). Today, you may feel lost in a decision or uncertain about your direction. Are you sincerely asking Him for guidance, or are you leaning on your own plans? What would change if you truly believed that He guides whom He wills?
?
The name al-Tawwab (Acceptor of Repentance) is a call for you to turn back to Him. Think of the last sin you committed and didn't fully repent. What is keeping you from running to the One who loves those who repent? Will you let this name break your heart open to His mercy right now?

Affirming Divine Attributes Without Likeness
Introduction to the shared principles and the first Sunni approach to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first school affirms divine attributes like hand and istiwa' while strictly avoiding anthropomorphism and modality.

Core Idea
Affirmation without comparison: we say 'hand' but not 'like our hands'.

The passage first establishes the undisputed foundations of tawhid and tanzih. Then it presents a group of scholars, led by Ibn Khuzayma, who affirm the transmitted attributes exactly as reported, while emphatically denying any resemblance to creation. This approach balances the affirming verses with the negating verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundational principles
Read the first part where the author states the agreed-upon tawhid and tanzih. Notice that all Muslims agree on these.
2
Identify the first school's method
Then read how they affirm attributes like 'hand' and 'istiwa'' while explicitly denying any similarity or modality. They say: 'hand not like our hands'.
This is the core of the salaf method: ithbat bi-la tamthil.
3
Examine the balance of verses
Notice how they use both affirming verses (e.g., 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and negating verses (e.g., 'there is none equal to Him') to maintain tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : إن الله - عز وجل بين في القرآن الكريم : انه إله واحد، ولا إله غيره. وبين: أنه ليس كمثله شيء. والمسلمون لا يختلفون في الوحدانية ، وفي التنزيه الله - عز وجل – وإنما يقول بعضهم : هو واحد ، له يد ، ليست كأيدينا - ومنهم الشيخ ابن خزيمة - ورجل ، ليست كارجلنا، ويستوى على العرش ، بدون لا تمثيل لكيفية الاستواء. وهكذا يثبتون الصفات الخبرية ، الواردة في القرآن والحديث عن الله . وهذا الإثبات لظاهر الأخبار التي تثبتها . مثل : « يد الله فوق أيديهم ، وينفون التشبيه والتمثيل . وهذا النفى لظاهر الأخبار التي تنفيها . مثل : د ولم يكن له كفوا أحد، أى ليس له مثل ولا كف. ولا نظير . ويقول بعض المسلمين : هو واحد . ليس له جسم . لأن الجسم يتخيله العقل على أى شبه كان . وحيث نفى عن نفسه التشبيه . فإذن لا يكون جسما . ومأورد في الأخبار عن يده ورجله وعينه ، وسائر الأعضاء التي تدل على أن الله جسم : تؤول على معنى : القدرة وبجى الأمر ، وأنه يسمع ويرى ، وما شابه ذلك . ومن هؤلاء المسلمين الشيخ محمد بن عمر ، مؤلف أساس التقديس ، - في علم الكلام - وهؤلاء يقولون أيضا : إن الذي ورد في القرآن وفي الأحاديث من أن الله له صفات تشبه صفات البشر ، من المكر والخداع والاستحياء والغضب والرضا والمحبة والسكره، و ماشابه ذلك. ليس على الحقيقة . وإنما معناه : أن الله يتحدث عن للبشر ، كأنه واحد منهم ، ليقرب ذاته إلى عقوطم . وهو ليس مثلهم ، ولا مثل أى شىء د وقد أسندت هذه الأعمال إلى الله من باب المشاكلة . وهي تسمية الجزاء على الشيء ، باسم ذلك الشي.
Secondly, Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - clarified in the Noble Qur'an that He is one God, and there is no god besides Him. And He clarified that there is nothing like Him. Muslims do not differ regarding monotheism and regarding the transcendence (tanzih) of Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - rather some of them say: He is one, He has a hand, not like our hands - among them is the shaykh Ibn Khuzayma - and a leg, not like our legs, and He establishes Himself on the Throne (istiwa') without tashbih of the modality of istiwa'. Thus they affirm the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya) that are reported in the Qur'an and Hadith about Allah. And this affirmation is due to the apparent meaning of reports that affirm them, like 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', and they deny tashbih and imitation. And this denial is due to the apparent meaning of reports that deny them, like 'And there is none equal to Him', i.e., He has no equal, no match, no counterpart. And some Muslims say: He is one, He has no body, because the intellect imagines a body in any form whatsoever. Since He denied tashbih of Himself, therefore He is not a body. And what is reported in the traditions about His hand, His leg, His eye, and all the members that indicate that Allah is a body: they are interpreted (tu'awwal) to mean power, [؟] the command, and that He hears and sees, and the like. Among these Muslims is the shaykh Muhammad ibn 'Umar, author of Asas al-Taqdis, in kalam. And these also say: That which has been reported in the Qur'an and the Hadith that Allah has attributes resembling those of humans, like plotting, deceit, modesty, anger, pleasure, love, drunkenness, and the like, are not literal (haqiqa). Rather their meaning is that Allah speaks to humans as if He were one of them, to bring His essence closer to their intellects. And He is not like them, nor like anything. And these actions have been attributed to Allah by way of mushakala (figurative usage), which is naming the recompense for a thing by the name of that thing.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary contrasting two authoritative Sunni approaches to the divine attributes, especially the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya). The author first establishes the agreed-upon principles: Allah is one (tawhid) and transcendent (tanzih), as stated in the Qur'an. He notes that no Muslim disputes these. Then he presents the first approach, exemplified by Ibn Khuzayma and others: they affirm attributes like hand, leg, and istiwa' (establishing on the Throne) but reject any comparison (tashbih) or modality (takyif). They take the apparent meaning of affirming verses (e.g., 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and also take the apparent meaning of negating verses (e.g., 'there is none equal to Him'), thus balancing affirmation and negation without figurative interpretation (ta'wil). The second approach, attributed to Imam al-Razi and his school, denies that Allah has a body because bodies are subject to the imagination and Allah has negated likeness. Therefore, any bodily terms in scripture are interpreted metaphorically: 'hand' means power, 'eye' means perception, etc. This group also interprets anthropomorphic actions like plotting, deceit, anger as literal meanings, saying they are figurative expressions (mushakala) used to bring the divine near to human understanding. Mushakala is defined as naming the recompense of an action by the name of the action itself. The passage thus presents two valid methodologies within Sunni orthodoxy: the salaf method of affirmation without modality or comparison, and the kalam method of metaphorical interpretation to safeguard transcendence.

Key Terms
التنزيه
التشبيه
الصفات الخبرية
الإثبات بلا تمثيل
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first school described in the passage, what do Muslims generally differ on?
A) They differ on the unity of Allah (tawhid).
B) They differ on the transcendence of Allah (tanzih).
C) They differ on how to approach the transmitted attributes.
D) They differ on whether Allah has a hand.

Key Takeaway
The first school affirms divine attributes without comparison or modality, balancing the affirming and negating verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah has a hand not like our hands, do you find yourself trying to imagine it or are you content with the text as it is? What does your reaction reveal about your reliance on your own intellect versus submission to revelation?
?
This lesson teaches that Muslims agree on tawhid and tanzih, yet differ in approach. Which approach do you find more humbling: affirming without asking how, or explaining away? Examine your own heart: are you seeking to protect Allah's transcendence or to satisfy your own curiosity?

Divine Attributes

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Expressions (Ta'wil and Mushakala)
Explanation of the second Sunni approach that interprets bodily attributes metaphorically using ta'wil and mushakala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second school interprets bodily attributes metaphorically using ta'wil and how mushakala explains anthropomorphic actions attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah speaks to us 'as if' one of us, but He is beyond likeness.

The second group, represented by Imam al-Razi, prioritizes tanzih to the point that they interpret any bodily reference in scripture as metaphorical. They argue that since Allah has no body, terms like hand must mean power, and actions like 'deceit' are figurative, using a device called mushakala, where the recompense takes the name of the action.

Guided Path
1
Read the second school's starting point
Read from 'ويقول بعض المسلمين' and note their key premise: Allah has no body, because He negates likeness and the intellect imagines a body in forms.
2
See how ta'wil is applied
Then they interpret hand, leg, eyes as power, command, and perception. This is ta'wil: explaining the apparent meaning in a way that fits tanzih.
They do not deny the attributes but reinterpret them.
3
Understand mushakala
Finally, they explain anthropomorphic actions like 'deceit' as mushakala: naming the recompense after the action. For example, Allah's 'mocking' the disbelievers means He recompenses them in a way like mockery, but He does not actually mock.
This safeguards tanzih while accepting the scriptural terms.

Divine Attributes

Imam al-Shafi'i on Quranic Language and Divine Attributes
Editorial quote of Imam al-Shafi'i explaining Quranic anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Shafi'i interprets anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran by distinguishing between human language and divine reality.

Core Idea
Quranic anthropomorphic expressions are for human understanding, not literal divine attributes.

This lesson presents a key hermeneutical principle from Imam al-Shafi'i regarding divine attributes. He explains that the Quran speaks about Allah in human language to convey meanings, not to imply resemblance. He then applies this to the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (30:27), showing how the apparent meaning must be understood in light of human estimation, not divine capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to al-Shafi'i's overall statement about Quranic language before tackling the specific verse.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the hermeneutical principle
Al-Shafi'i opens by saying the Quran speaks about Allah and His attributes 'bi-lisan bani Adam' (in the tongue of the children of Adam). This is the key principle: divine speech uses human conceptual categories for communication.
This principle guards against literalism.
3
See the principle applied to the verse
Read how al-Shafi'i explains 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi'. He argues that 'ahwan' (easier) refers to human estimation, not divine capacity. The example of creating from non-existence vs. restoring something illustrates this.
The word 'al-'ibra' (estimation) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قال الإمام الشافعي - رضي الله عنه في هذه الصفات ، كلاماً معناه : إن القرآن تحدث عن الله وصفاته بلسان بني آدم . قال في قوله تعالى : ( وهو أهون عليه ، : « معناه : في العبرة عندكم . لأنه لما قال للعدم كن، فيخرج تاماً ، كاملا بعينيه وأذنيه وبصره وأنفه وسمعه ومفاصله . فهذا فى العبرة أشد من أن يقول لشيء قد كان وفى : عد إلى ما كنت . فالمراد من الآية : وهو أهون عليه بحسب عرفكم ، لا أن شيئاً يكون أهون على الله تعالى من شيء آخر
And Imam al-Shafi'i - may Allah be pleased with him - said regarding these attributes, a statement whose meaning is: That the Qur'an spoke about Allah and His attributes in the tongue of the children of Adam. He said regarding the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (Quran 30:27): 'Its meaning is: in estimation among you. Because when He says to non-existence 'be', it emerges complete, perfect with its eyes, ears, sight, nose, hearing, and joints. This in estimation is more severe than saying to something that has been and gone: return to what you were. So the intended meaning of the verse is: 'And He is easier' according to your custom, not that anything is easier upon Allah Almighty than another thing.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Imam al-Shafi'i, as transmitted by the editor, regarding the interpretation of divine attributes. Al-Shafi'i first establishes a hermeneutical principle: the Qur'an speaks about Allah and His attributes using the language of humans (bi-lisan bani Adam). This means that when the Qur'an attributes human-like actions or qualities to Allah, it does so in a way that humans can understand, not that Allah literally resembles them. He then applies this principle to the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi) from Surah Ar-Rum (30:27), which some might take to imply that some things are 'easier' for Allah than others, suggesting limitation. Al-Shafi'i explains that the word 'ahwan' (easier) here refers to human estimation (fi al-'ibra 'indakum). He provides a striking example: when Allah commands non-existence to 'be' (kun) and a fully formed human being emerges with all its limbs, that is, in human estimation, more remarkable than restoring something that previously existed. So the verse means: 'it is easier in your estimation, O people' – it is a way of communicating a point about divine power using human conceptual categories. The actual meaning is not that one act is literally easier for Allah than another, because Allah is omnipotent and nothing is difficult for Him. Thus al-Shafi'i reinforces the principle that scriptural expressions must be understood in light of how language conveys meaning to humans, avoiding any suggestion of divine limitation or anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
لسان بني آدم
العبرة
أهون
العدم
القول كن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi' means that resurrecting is literally easier for Allah than initial creation.
Correction: Al-Shafi'i clarifies that 'ahwan' refers to human estimation (al-'ibra), not an actual difference in divine power. In human view, creating from nothing is more astonishing than restoring, but for Allah all acts are equally effortless.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, how should the phrase 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi' (and He is easier upon Him) be understood?
A) It means that resurrection is literally easier for Allah than initial creation.
B) It refers to the ease in human estimation, not an actual difference in divine power.
C) It indicates that some things are difficult for Allah.
D) It is a command to make things easier for believers.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i teaches that Quranic expressions about divine attributes must be interpreted according to human linguistic usage, not as literal descriptions of Allah's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'and He is easier upon Him,' does your heart initially assume that some actions are harder for Allah than others? Al-Shafi'i says the Qur'an speaks in human language so you can understand. But are you willing to submit to that interpretation, or do you insist on a 'clearer' answer that suits your human logic? What does that insistence reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom over your own?
?
The phrase 'bi-lisan bani Adam' means Allah speaks in your language. But do you ever unconsciously expect Allah to adapt to your expectations of how He should be described? Al-Shafi'i's principle demands that you accept that divine speech is accommodated to human understanding, not that Allah resembles what you understand. Have you ever caught yourself visualizing Allah's 'hand' or 'face' in worship? How does this lesson correct that thought, and what will you do next time it arises?
?
Al-Shafi'i's example of creation from nothing being 'more remarkable' in human estimation is meant to show that Allah's power is incomparable. But do you live as if that is true? Consider a worry you face today: does your trust in Allah's ability to create from nothing affect how you view that problem? If not, haven't you effectively believed that something is 'easier' for Allah than solving your problem? What specific change will you make in your dua to reflect belief that nothing is difficult for Allah?

The Two Views on Divine Attributes
Enumerates two opposing views on anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and Ibn Taymiyya on interpreting anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
Razi interprets figuratively; Ibn Taymiyya accepts literally without 'how'.

This lesson introduces the two major approaches to divine attributes discussed in classical Islam. Imam Razi represents the theological tradition of ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation), while Ibn Taymiyya upholds a literal acceptance without modality. Understanding their differences is key to appreciating later debates on divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the two views side by side
Read the Arabic passage listing both views. Notice the common ground ('not a body', 'nothing like Him') and the key differences in how they handle the 'hand' and 'foot' reports.
Both agree on God's incomparability but differ on method.
2
Identify the core difference
Razi interprets the hand as power and the foot as the coming of command. Ibn Taymiyya takes them on apparent meaning without asking 'how'.
This difference reflects deeper methodological commitments.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The source warns that both deny anthropomorphism outwardly, but their methods are distinct. Consider why this distinction matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأوضح رأى الفريقين على النحو التالي :
۱ - يقول الإمام فخر الدين الرازي : إن الله ليس جسما ، وليس كمثله شيء . وهو فى السماء بالعلم ، وفى الأرض بالعلم . وليس للذات الإلهية مكان يشار إليه . وما ورد فى الأخبار من اليد والرجل، يؤول إلى القدرة ومجيء الأمر . وهكذا .
٢ - ويقول ابن تيمية : إن الله ليس جسما ، وليس كمثله شيء . وهو فى الأرض بالعلم ، وفى السماء فوق العرش ، بدون تشبيه . وما ورد في الأخبار من اليد والرجل . نأخذه على ظاهره ، بلا كيف ، ولا نؤوله .
و بيان رأى الإمام فخر الدين هكذا :
( أ ) يد الله فوق أيديهم ، (ب) ليس كمثله شيء .
قوله : «ليس كمثله شيء : قول محكم ، له معنى واحد ، و هو نفى الشبيه والنظير ، عن الله عز وجل . وقوله : « يد الله : قول متشابه يحتمل معنيين : الأول : اليد الجارحة . والثاني اليد : كناية عن القدرة . وحيث أنه متشابه . يتعين الرجوع إلى المحكم. والمحكم وهو « ليس كمثله شيء ، لا يدل على معنيين كالمتشابه ، بل يدل على معنى واحد ، وهو نفى التشبيه .
ويسأل الإمام فخر الدين هذا السؤال : أي المعنيين من معنيي التشبيه المتشابه هو الذي يتفق مع المحكم ؟ إذا عرفته فقد عرفت مراد الله تعالى من قوله : يد الله فوق أيديهم ، ويجيب بقوله : إن المعنى المتفق مع المحكم هو أن اليد كناية عن القدرة . لأن اليد الجارحة لا تتفق مع نفى التشبيه .
وقوله تعالى : ( هو الذى أنزل عليك الكتاب . منه آيات محكمات من أم الكتاب . وأخر متشابهات . فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيغ ، فيتبعون
And I will clarify the opinion of the two groups as follows:
1- Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi says: God is not a body, and nothing is like Him. He is in the heaven by knowledge, and on the earth by knowledge. The divine essence has no indicated location. And what has come in the reports regarding the hand and the foot is interpreted as power and the coming of the command. And so on.
2- And Ibn Taymiyya says: God is not a body, and nothing is like Him. He is on the earth by knowledge, and in the heaven above the Throne, without anthropomorphism. And what has come in the reports regarding the hand and the foot, we take it on its apparent meaning, without 'how', and we do not interpret it.
And the statement of Imam Fakhr al-Din is as follows:
(a) 'The hand of God is above their hands.' (b) 'Nothing is like Him.'
His saying: 'Nothing is like Him' is a clear (muhkam) statement, having one meaning, which is the negation of likeness and similitude from God Almighty. And His saying: 'the hand of God' is an ambiguous (mutashabih) statement, bearing two meanings: first: the limb of the hand, and second: the hand as a metaphor for power. And since it is ambiguous, it is necessary to refer to the clear (muhkam) text. And the clear text, which is 'Nothing is like Him', does not indicate two meanings like the ambiguous, but indicates one meaning, which is the negation of similitude (tashbih).
And Imam Fakhr al-Din asks this question: Which of the two meanings of similitude (tashbih) is the ambiguous one that agrees with the clear text? If you know that, then you have known the intended meaning of God from His saying: 'The hand of God is above their hands.' And he answers by saying: The meaning that agrees with the clear text is that the hand is a metaphor for power, because the limb of the hand does not agree with the negation of similitude.
And the saying of the Exalted: 'It is He who sent down to you the Book. In it are clear verses (muhkamat) that are the mother of the Book, and others ambiguous (mutashabihat). As for those in whose hearts is deviation, they follow...'
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the two opposing views on divine attributes and then explains Razi's methodology using the Quranic principle of muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous) verses. It begins by listing the two views: Razi interprets anthropomorphic expressions metaphorically, while Ibn Taymiyya accepts them on the surface without modality (bila kayf). Then it focuses on Razi's argument: He takes two verses, 'The hand of God is above their hands' and 'Nothing is like Him'. He explains that 'Nothing is like Him' is muhkam, having one clear meaning: negation of any likeness. The phrase 'hand of God' is mutashabih, carrying two possible meanings: a literal hand (body part) or a metaphorical hand (power). Because it is mutashabih, it must be referred to the muhkam verse. The muhkam verse negates any similarity, so the literal meaning (which would imply similarity) is rejected, confirming the metaphorical meaning of power. Razi then poses a question: which meaning of the mutashabih agrees with the muhkam? And answers: the metaphorical meaning of power, because the literal hand implies tashbih (anthropomorphism), which contradicts the muhkam. The passage concludes by quoting the Quranic verses (from Surah Al 'Imran 3:7) that establish the principle of muhkam and mutashabih.

Key Terms
يد
رجل
تشبيه
تأويل
بلا كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both Razi and Ibn Taymiyya have the same method because both deny anthropomorphism.
Correction: While both deny anthropomorphism, Razi employs metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to reconcile ambiguous texts with clear ones, whereas Ibn Taymiyya accepts the literal wording without seeking a figurative meaning, relying on the principle of 'bila kayf' (without how).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Ibn Taymiyya handle the report about the 'hand' of God?
A) He interprets it as power.
B) He takes it on its apparent meaning without asking 'how'.
C) He rejects the report as unreliable.
D) He says it is a metaphor for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Both Razi and Ibn Taymiyya affirm God's transcendence, but they differ fundamentally in how they approach ambiguous texts: Razi uses ta'wil, Ibn Taymiyya accepts the apparent meaning without modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse describing God's 'hand' or 'face', which of these two approaches naturally rises in your heart—immediate interpretation or quiet submission without understanding? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's words?
?
Both scholars affirm 'nothing is like Him.' Do you truly let that truth govern your imagination when you pray to Allah as being 'above the Throne'? Or do you unconsciously form a picture despite your denial?
?
If you had to choose between Razi's interpretive security and Ibn Taymiyya's literal acceptance without explanation, which would cost you more in terms of intellectual humility? Are you willing to embrace that cost today?

Razi's Muhkam-Mutashabih Method
Demonstrates Razi's method of interpreting mutashabih by referring to muhkam using the example of 'hand'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses the Quranic principle of muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous) to interpret anthropomorphic expressions like 'the hand of God'.

Core Idea
Mutashabih must be interpreted in light of muhkam, which negates any likeness to God.

After presenting the two views, the passage delves into Razi's detailed methodology. He demonstrates how to apply the Quranic principle of muhkam and mutashabih to a concrete example: the verse 'The hand of God is above their hands.' This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verses
Razi takes two verses: 'يد الله فوق أيديهم' (mutashabih) and 'ليس كمثله شيء' (muhkam). Note how he classifies each.
The muhkam verse has one clear meaning; the mutashabih has two possible meanings.
2
Understand the classification
'ليس كمثله شيء' is muhkam because it has one meaning: negation of likeness. 'يد الله' is mutashabih because it could mean a literal hand or metaphorical power.
3
Follow the question and answer
Razi asks which meaning of the mutashabih agrees with the muhkam. He answers: the metaphorical meaning, because the literal hand would imply tashbih (anthropomorphism).
This is the core of his method: the muhkam arbitrates the mutashabih.
4
Connect to the Quranic principle
The passage quotes Quran 3:7, which mentions muhkamat and mutashabihat, grounding the method in scripture.
The verse warns against those who follow the ambiguous seeking fitnah.

Ibn Taymiyyah's Literalist Position on Divine Attributes
Ibn Taymiyyah asserts that all divine attributes must be taken literally without ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Taymiyyah's assertion that divine attributes must be interpreted literally (haqiqa) without resorting to figurative interpretation (majaz) or ta'wil.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyyah insists on literal interpretation of divine attributes without ta'wil.

This passage introduces a central stance in the debate over divine attributes. Ibn Taymiyyah argues that the language of the Qur'an should not be subjected to metaphorical interpretation, and therefore all divine attributes must be taken literally. Understanding his position is key to grasping the classical Sunni dispute over ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the short, declarative style of this argument.
2
Identify the three key terms
The passage uses three technical terms: حقيقة (literal), مجاز (metaphorical), and تأويل (interpretive explanation). Identify them in the Arabic text.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many assume Ibn Taymiyyah rejects all figurative language in the Qur'an. However, as the objections in later passages show, he allows it based on context. Consider why he insists on literal interpretation for divine attributes specifically.
This connects to the broader sectarian discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ينبغي أن تخضع له لغة القرآن. وعلى ذلك فإن جميع الصفات الإلهية يجب أن تحمل على الحقيقة ، لا على المجاز ، ولا حاجة إلى تأويلها. هذه حجته.
The language of the Qur'an should not be subjected to it. Therefore, all the divine attributes must be taken literally, not metaphorically, and there is no need for their interpretation. This is his argument.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Ibn Taymiyyah. It is a brief statement arguing that the language of the Qur'an should not be subjected to something (likely metaphorical interpretation) and therefore all divine attributes must be interpreted literally (haqiqa) without resorting to metaphor (majaz) or interpretive ta'wil. The phrase 'هذه حجته' indicates that this is his argument that the following objections will address.

Correction: Ibn Taymiyyah allows figurative language based on context, but insists that divine attributes must be taken literally without ta'wil.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyyah's Inconsistency on Context and Metaphor
First objection argues that Ibn Taymiyyah uses context for metaphor in some places but denies it in others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against Ibn Taymiyyah: that he inconsistently uses context to justify metaphor in some places (like the Black Stone hadith) while denying it for other divine attributes.

Core Idea
Context can make a word metaphorical, so why deny it for divine attributes?

This lesson presents the first objection leveled against Ibn Taymiyyah’s approach to divine attributes. The objector points out that Ibn Taymiyyah himself admits that context can transform a literal word into a metaphor, as he does with the Black Stone hadith. Yet he refuses to apply the same reasoning to other Qur’anic verses about Allah’s attributes. The passage exposes this apparent inconsistency and compares Ibn Taymiyyah’s method to that of classical rhetoricians.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the argument as a whole. Notice the rhetorical structure.
The objector begins by quoting Ibn Taymiyyah's own words.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyyah's own precedent
Observe how Ibn Taymiyyah interprets 'yamin' (right hand) as metaphorical due to context. This establishes that he accepts context-based metaphor.
3
Compare with the lion example
See how rhetoricians use context to make 'asad' (lion) metaphorical. Ibn Taymiyyah acknowledges this principle, yet he rejects it for divine attributes.
This comparison highlights the inconsistency.
4
Pinpoint the inconsistency
Ask yourself: If context can make one phrase metaphorical, why can't it do the same for other attributes? The objection is that Ibn Taymiyyah is inconsistent.
The objector uses Ibn Taymiyyah's own logic against him.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider what this objection means for Ibn Taymiyyah's methodology. It suggests a selective application of interpretive principles.
This is a key critique in the debate over divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولا : قال ابن تيمية فى بعض كتبه في معنى الحديث الشريف : الحجر الأسود يمين الله فى الأرض قال : اليمين مجاز هنا ، وليس حقيقة فى اليد اليمنى الجارحة . وهو مجاز ليس في لفظ « يمين »، بل هو مجاز لأن سياق العبارة يدل على أنه مجاز . أى ان لفظ « يمين »، لا يستعمل ككلمة مفردة إلا على الحقيقة . وأما فى الجملة فإنه يستعمل مجازاً للقوة . وعلى قوله هذا ، فإن سياق العبارات فى إيراد بعض ألفاظ الصفات الخبرية عن الله عز وجل تدل على المجاز ، ولا تدل على الحقيقة . ومثال ذلك قوله تعالى : ( وهو معكم أينما كنتم )، يفسره ابن تيمية بقوله : دل سياق الآية على أنها معية علم ، حيث بدئت الآية بالعلم وختمت بالعلم . وقوله : ( ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم )، إلى قوله : ( إلا هو معهم أينما كانوا ) يدل على أنه معهم بعلمه لا بجسمه . وعلى قوله هذا . إذا ثبت التأويل في موضع ، فلماذا يمنع في موضع آخر إذا كان مشابهاً له ؟ وعلى قوله هذا يقال له : إن سياق العبارة يضع الحقيقة أو المجاز من النص . كما تقول . فلماذا تستبعد المحكم من تعيين أحد معنى المتشابه ، وعلى المحكم والمتشابه دليل من القرآن ، وليس على سياق العبارات دليل . اللهم : و إلا القرينة العقلية ؟ وعلماء البلاغة يقولون : إن لفظ الأسد ، كلفظ مفرد هو حقيقة على الحيوان المفترس. وكلفظ مفرد لا يدل مجازاً على رجل شجاع . وإنما يدل على رجل شجاع إذا كان فى عبارة ، و في العبارة قرينة تدل على ذلك . أى أن سياق العبارة هو الذى يجعل لفظ « الأسد » مجازاً. لا أن لفظ « الأسد »، في ذاته حقيقة ومجاز معاً . وابن تيمية معترف بذلك . فأى فرق بينه وبين علماء البلاغة ؟ ولماذا يمنع المجاز في القرآن وفي الصفات ؟
First: Ibn Taymiyyah said in some of his books regarding the meaning of the noble hadith: 'The Black Stone is the right hand of Allah on earth.' He said: 'Yamin (right hand) here is metaphorical, not literal in the sense of the physical right hand.' And it is metaphorical not in the word 'yamin' itself, but it is metaphorical because the context of the phrase indicates it is metaphorical. That is, the word 'yamin' is not used as a single word except literally. But in a sentence it is used metaphorically for power. And according to this statement of his, the context of expressions in presenting some of the reportive attributes of Allah Almighty indicates metaphor and does not indicate literal truth. An example is His statement: 'And He is with you wherever you are.' Ibn Taymiyyah interprets it by saying: The context of the verse indicates that it is a companionship of knowledge, as the verse began with knowledge and ended with knowledge. And His statement: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' up to His statement: 'except that He is with them wherever they are' indicates that He is with them by His knowledge, not by His body. And according to this statement of his, if ta'wil is established in one place, why is it prevented in another place if it is similar to it? And according to this statement it is said to him: The context of the phrase determines the literal or metaphorical meaning of the text. So why do you exclude the decisive (muhkam) from determining one of the meanings of the ambiguous (mutashabih), while the decisive and the ambiguous have evidence from the Qur'an, and there is no evidence from the context of phrases? Unless there is an intellectual contextual clue? And rhetoricians say: The word 'asad' (lion), as a single word, is literally the predatory animal. And as a single word, it does not indicate metaphorically a brave man. Rather, it indicates a brave man only when it is in a phrase, and in the phrase there is an indication that indicates that. That is, the context of the phrase is what makes the word 'asad' metaphorical. Not that the word 'asad' in itself is both literal and metaphorical together. And Ibn Taymiyyah acknowledges that. So what is the difference between him and the rhetoricians? And why does he prevent metaphor in the Qur'an and in the attributes?
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against Ibn Taymiyyah. It begins by quoting Ibn Taymiyyah's own interpretation of the hadith about the Black Stone, where he admits that 'yamin' (right hand) is metaphorical because context indicates so. The objector argues that if context can make something metaphorical in one place, then why deny that possibility for other divine attributes? The objector also compares Ibn Taymiyyah's method to that of rhetoricians, who hold that a word like 'asad' (lion) is literal in isolation but becomes metaphorical in a phrase with context. Ibn Taymiyyah himself acknowledges this principle, yet he rejects metaphor in Quranic verses about attributes. Thus, the objection highlights an inconsistency in his approach. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: why does he forbid metaphor in the Quran and attributes?

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Taymiyyah rejects all metaphor entirely.
Correction: In fact, he accepts context-based metaphor in some cases but inconsistently denies it for divine attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection against Ibn Taymiyyah?
A) He misinterpreted the hadith about the Black Stone.
B) He inconsistently applies context-based metaphor to some attributes but denies it for others.
C) He denies the existence of metaphor in Arabic.
D) He rejects the authority of rhetoric scholars.

Key Takeaway
The first objection highlights an inconsistency in Ibn Taymiyyah's method: he allows context-based metaphor for some attributes but denies it for others, contrary to his own practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'the hand of Allah is above their hands,' do you instinctively take it literally or do you pause to consider contextual evidence? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your reliance on reason versus the plain text?
?
Have you ever been inconsistent in applying a principle of interpretation—accepting it for one case and rejecting it for another because of a hidden bias? How can you train yourself to be more consistent in understanding Allah's attributes?
?
The rhetoricians' principle says a word is literal in isolation but can become metaphorical in context. When you recite or hear Quranic attribute verses, do you actively think about the context of the whole surah and the verses around it? If not, what changes in your approach are needed?
